HOW TO III! THB POWDHi
The Amazing Moore
The Red Page
Vale, the concluding volume of George
Moore’s delightful and maliciously witty
trilogy. Hail and, Farewell (Heinemann, per
Sydney Book "Club), continues the Odyssey of
his Soul to his departure from Ireland. This
section of his autobiography ranges wide
through his present and his past, or pasts.
Moore begins by Ills pardonable desire to
re-acquire the habit of reading. But after
valiant efforts to read Shakespeare, Balzac,
Goncourt, Pater—-“a tide of honeyed words
preached by a divine from an ivory pulpit”-—-
Tom Jones, remembered “merely as a very
empty work written in a breezy manner,” he
gave the attempt up, consoling himself with
the thought that: “Though I' may have lost
the habit of reading, I have acquired, per-
haps more than any other human being, an-
other habit, the habit of thinking. I love
my own thoughts.” And his thoughts take
him back to his youth. Once, wo learn, he
acthally agreed to enter the army, on his
father’s advice. The death of his father,
however, set him free. Then his mother died,
“believing me to have been the best of sons,
though I never sacrificed my convenience to
hers. So we come to the gorgeous youth
of George Moore.
“To- be ridiculous has always been mon
petit luxe, but can anyone be said to be
ridiculous if he know that he is ridiculous?
Not very well. It is the pompous that are
truly ridiculous.” Yet did George Moore
know he was ridiculous when he was a
ridiculous youth? One suspects not.
Jim would throw himself on the sofa and
tell me for the hundredth time that the only
women worth loving were tall women with
abundant bosom and flaxen hair, the only
women that men with a sense of the beautiful
could admire, and his, control over my mind
was so great that for years I followed the
tall and abundant, convinced that I liked
deep in reality I liked hardly
any ; and 1 remember well the evening when
the thin girl sitting on my knee revealed my
own taste to me, and my regret that my best
efforts had been wasted on fat women.
Some of these efforts Moore conscientiously
details.. Next he was to be an artist, but
in Paris he found out his mistake, tried
drama and poetry, was introduced to French
literature, published in London his first book,
Flowers of Passion, and was told by The
Would that “The author of these poems
should be whipped at the cart’s tail, while
the book was being burnt in the market-place
by the common hangman.” But in Paris he
met everybody in literary and artistic society.
Especially was it his good fortune to know
the Impressionists before they were known
to the rest of the world. His theory of edu-
cation was that it was better conducted in a
cafe than in a university. He took with
him a valet, but he was soon dismissed, for
Moore felt that to be himself he sho f uld have
to live the physical as well as the intellectual
life of the Quarter. “Myself was the goal
I was making for, instinctively if you will,
but still making for it.”
Back we come to Yeats, who had returned
from the States “with a paunch, a huge
stride, and an immense fur overcoat.”
“Willie Yeats’s best poems were written, ac-
cording to JEi when he was a poor boy in
Sligo. All his best poems were written be-
fore he went, to London. And now he had
ceased to write poetry. M thought that his
long silence was due to his search for a
style: he would have written volume after
volume if he had been content to write
simply.” When Symons had shown Moore
Rosa Alchemica, Moore had replied: “His
inspiration is at an end, for he talks about
how he is going to write.” A man may tell
the subject of his poem and write it, but if
he fell how he is going to write his poem he
will never write it. Moore contrives in this
malicious, indirect way to convey the impres-
sion that Yeats is finished. The proba-
bility, when we consider the variants he has
made, and published, of his early verse, is
that he is.
Then there is a fine picture of Synge,
tramping through Ireland, “thinking out the
dialogue of his plays, modifying it at every
style after a gossip with some herdsman or
pig-jobber, whomever he might meet, return-
ing through the cold, spring evening, when
the stars shine brightly through the naked
trees, licking his lips, appreciating the fine
flavor of some drunkard’s oath or blas-
phemy.”
He collected some hundred turns of speech
in the West, and from different classes, and
these, he was able to work up together,
creating what amounted practically to a
sr ? l an guage understandable by everybody
who knows English, but sufficiently far re-
moved from ordinary speech to give his
plays an air that none others have—that air
of aloofness, of art, which we all deem neces-
sary, though we, differ as to how it may be
obtained. Synge had listened more atten-
tively than Lady Gregory, and had discov-
ered that it was possible to write beautifully
in peasant idiom. Everybody could write it.
Lady Gregory as well as another, but*no one
but Synge could write beautifully in it.
Yet we must get it into our heads that
the Abbey Theatre would have come to
naught but for Lady Gregory’s talent for roll-
ing up little anecdotes into one-act plavs.
She never would have written a play
if she had not met Yeats, nor would
Synge, who is now looked upon as an artist
as great as Donatello or Benvenuto
Cellini, and perhaps I should have not
have written The Lake or The Untitled
Field, or the book I am now writing. So
all the Irish movement rose out of Yeats
and returns to Yeats. . . .We hake all wanted
repertory theatres and art theatres and lite-
rary theatres, but these words are vain
words ind mean nothing. Yeats knew ex-
actly what lie wanted; he wanted a folk
theatre, for if Ireland was ever to produce
any literature he knew that it would have
to begin in folk, and he has had his reward.
Ireland speaks for the first time in litera-
ture in the Abbey Theatre.
A vivid portrait of James Stephens fol-
lows, his discovery„ by his strange
literary protnise. Then came some amaz-
ingly frank confessions of Moore’s thoughts
when, “at 46 or thereabouts, one begins to
feel that one’s time for love is over, one is
consultant rather than practitioner.” What
happened, Moore pleasantly explains, is that
he loved a woman who had too much love to
give; and Moore insists that “men have died
of love,” and asserts that he very nearly did.
And when the lady told him that she was
thinking of being married (to the other
man) Moore was unduly elated, “and tried
to assume the interest that a friend should.”
’ ' j*
Let us hastily conclude—this page is not
allowed to be as frank as George Moore may
be —by his swan-song of love: —
Love’s period is over for me. Life is for
ever changing, and very little remains after
5U for a man, and still less for a woman. We
are for ever dying. Woolly bear is suc-
ceeded by the cricket-bat, tlie bat is followed
by the rod, the gun, the horse, the girl, and
between 50 and 60 we discover that our love-
life is over and done. Our interest in sex,
however, remains the same, but it is an intel-
lectual interest, changed, transformed, lifted
out of the flesh. Our eyes follow the move-
ment of the body under the silken gown, a
well-turned neck and shapely bosom please
us, and we like to look into the feminine
eyes and read the feminine soul; but we do
not kiss the point of white shoulders when
thoughtless ladies lead us away after dinner
into a corner of the shadowy drawing-room
and cry in our ears, “No, all is not over yet.”
The question for English literature now
is: “After Vale, what?”
April
Like her spring sister in England, our
autumn April is a maid of many moods. Her
waywardness has appealed to many of our
sonneteers. There were 43 singers of
our April, several of whom merely sent in
14 lines of anticipation of the coming glories
of football. Sonnets of medium quality were
forwarded by: Ben Sun, “The Maorilander,”
lhos. V. Tierney (who uses the antique
locution what time” for the less pretentious
T when” twice in his octette), George It. H.,
L - J- Villiers, L. A. Roylett and II. C. Bailey.
lv. G. Henderson fails to develop his fine
theme to its logical conclusion, but thus far
he is allowed to appear in print:—

Attack the flatulent leather

“Kuaka’s” sonnet personifies April charm-
ingly:—
Still Lady April bears her old-world fame
Of fickleness, of sudden shine and shower,
And wayward fancies, changeful as the
hour
That brings the dawn. The sun-king's loan-
ing flame,
Like golden mantle wrought for queenly
dame, ! . ■
Enfolds her gently. From her ruddy bower
She sees the red fruit fall, while storm-
clouds lower,
And sighing winds are whisp’rihg Winter’s
name.
0 Maid, ivhose tresses match the russet
leaves,
Whose eyes are azure as the seas at rest,
Why pause you now with sudden, shudd’ring
gasp?
Alas! you slowly pass as one that grieves ;
With tawny head on Autumn's shoulder
prest,
And warm, unwilling hand in Winter’s grasp.
Each of these sonnets is worthy of the
prize, were the competition not so keen.
Herbert Tracey and Nina Murdoch are
left. The latter, as usual, paints the more
vivid picture, but the sense of her final
image does not bear analysis. There
is some confusion in its expression. Why
should the year slip unnoticed past her
prime because April has stolen the “golden"
heart of some great woman? Herbert
Tracey’s sonnet finely expresses a striking
and appropriate idea in his octette, reflected
in his sestette, and his final metaphor is
happily Australian. The “bloodhounds,”
however, are a flaw. And with two sonnets
thus approximately equal, the prize is given
to the sonneteer who has hitherto failed to
win: Herbert Tracey, Leura, Eastern-
avenue, Kensington, Sydney. This does not
mean that past winners in this series of
competitions are barred. Had Nina Mur-
doch’s sonnet been better it would have
won.
Carols the magpie, “Lo, again ’tis Spring!”
For tipped with green is each pittosporum
tree,
And, on the heath, boronias pinlc ioith glee
Laugh to the sun, and on the breezes fling
Their pungent scent. A swift kingfisher’s
wing
Is not more purple-green than gleams the
sea,
Yet, like a sad loord dropped unwittingly,
The wind blows keenly with a sudden sting.
The year’s decline with you has had no part,
O autumn April with the vernal face,
Is it a compact you have made with Time,
Or haply did you steal the golden heart
Of come great woman, so with mellow grace
The year might slip unnoticed past her
prime?
Nina Murdoch.
APRIL.
By Herbert Tracey.
Didst hear a stirring in the trees last night?
Eerie and low it came, and went again,
But long the gaunt gums groaned like souls
in pain!
Was it the Ghost of Summer taking flight?
’Tis sa/id that often when the stars are bright
It strives to woo fair April—but in vain —
The night-winds chase their quarry o’er the
plain,
And come like bloodhounds, dripping, from
the fight.
There is a portent in the pastel sky
Of wind, and drear discomfort, lurking near.
The mist-wrapped dawn wakes heavy eyed
each day
But turns to softened sunshine, warm and
clear;
And Autumn, frisking like the lamb at play,
Heeds not the Dingo, Winter, creeping nigh.
Entries, addressed to this page, for the
sonnet on our Australian May must be re-
ceived by June 4.
Interesting to N.S.W. by reason of his
Lieut.-Governorship of this State, is the
Life of Admiral Sir Harry Rawson, by
Geoffrey Rawson (Arnold; 12s. 6d. in Eng-
land.) Rawson had an interesting and
adventurous career in the navy, ultimately
rising to the command of the Channel squad-
ron. His sojourn in N.S.W. is the subject
of two uninformative chapters.
His prodigious strength loosed the other’s
hold. “Stand back!” she cried. “I am his
wretched, unhappy wife!” “Are you man
or devil?” His eyes caught sight of Ed-
ward’s face. A frightful change came over
his countenance. The two combatants went
headlong over the cliff. From these titles to
the illustrations -of Tools of Satan, by Lan-
celot Booth (N.S.W. Bookstall Co.) the
reader can gather that he is in for an excit-
ing time.

given on Bed Page, April 16.

Robert Kaleski is to be congratulated. Ilis
Australian Barkers and Biters (N.S.W.
Bookstall, Is.) is good-Australese. He
writes, disguising his expert knowledge of
Australian dogs under a light and colloquia
style, of the kelpie, the dingo, the cattle-dog
the barb, the bush greyhound, the kangaro
dog and the beagle. In itself this sectio
is a condensed encyclopaedia of Borg; bn
he overflows with information and goo
“Abo” yarns of cat-fights, cock-fights, buck
jumping and racehorses. Some sample
that lighten a book that needs ho aeration :
“I have never seen any kelpie come up t
a little black and tan bitch I had once. In
her spare time she used her kennel for yard-
ing gohannas and frill lizards, keeping a big
pickle bottle specially for snakes. On
morning early -I heard a fearful row at th
yards, went down to see about it, and found
she had yarded a small cherub in the killing
pen, wings and all. I intended to sell it t(
the museum, but it managed to bite through
the fencing wire I had fastened it with am
flew away. I have told Sarah to keep her
eyes open for another, but so far we ka,v<
had no luck. I don’t think they often coirn
round our way.
“The dingo will never be sajnted in tlm
Australian calendar, yet there are plenty of
butter-agents worse.
“If you want to annoy a dingo deeply, cal
him a dog.
“To bachelors thinking of matrimony, a
course of breaking in cattle-pups should be
invaluable.
“The best bit of show work I ever saw
was done by Quinn’s blue dog, Coil. He
worked the sheep faultlessly right through
to the pen; worked the audience up to a
white heat of excitement; then worked the
three judges into the bar, and up to a bottle
of whisky.
“Some hush greyhounds are very fast and
determined. I had a pair once got away
after a very big old hare, and never came
home again. A week afterwards, a skin-
getter from the back gullies told me he had
seen what looked like the end of a hare’s
tail bounding through the scrub, hotly pur-
sued by a yellow front paw and a brindle
hind one; so they must be worn out alto-
gether by this time.”
For some,reason or other, probably in the
interests of art, the book is illustrated.
• i
Michael Macdonagh, in The Reporters'
Gallery (I-lodder and Stoughton, per Angus
and Robertson, 14s. 6d. here) interestingly
traces the strange history of the growth of
the reporters’ gallery from its birth, when
the reporting of debates was looked upon
by a Parliament jealous of its dignity as an
evil thing to be punished by fines and im-
prisonments, to its final recognition as an
essential part of representative assemblies,
as, indeed, the chief safeguard in the realm
for honest government. The ground has
been covered with an earnest thoroughness;
and the comparative neglect by historians of
this phase of the battle of the Press against
Parliament gives this volume its value.
The first part of the third volume of J. H. Maiden’s
Critical Revision of the Genus Eucalyptus (2s. 6d.)
has come to hand. The eucalyptus looks like becom-
ing a numerous family.
Under the auspices of the Australian His-
torical Society, James R., Tyrrell, Sydney,
publishes in magazine form the first of a
series of P.ceps at the Past. This comprises
the. republication of various interesting
incidents of our early history that appeared
weekly during 1909-10 in the Sydney Sun.
Much of the information given is scrappy,
but for this reason it is much more likely to
be read by the. general reader than sought
laboriously for in the heavy pages of an offi-
cial liistorv. And many N of the articles con-
tain new data gained by the Australian His-
torical Society. This useful publication is
illustrated.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Dryads of the Blue Gums.
The bush has many dryad-bands, and all are
meet for love.
From the golden-headed little maids that
haunt the wattle-grove
To those proud, sad-hearted dwellers in the
sheoaks dark and tall —
But the dryads of the blue gums are the
fairest of them all.
A few are brown as Tongan girls, but most
are whiter far,
With a luminous, smooth whiteness, than
our mortal women are.
And Oh ! the woods are full of them ; for only
yesterday
I sat alone a little while ‘Mid watched them
at their play.
My trampling through the fern had scared
them silent, and they drew
Around their bodies, slim and pale, their
filmy scarves of blue
(But are those soft scarves blue or pink or
purple, who can say?
They change beneath our very eyes from
violet to grey,
Elusive as that shadow-dance of lavender
which falls
From tossing branches, in the spring, upon
smooth, whitened walls.)
But soon those nymphs forgot me finite and
thought they were alone,
For they were still and very still, but I was
still as stone;
So they began to dance again, with milky
arms upflung
And murmured magic singing in their own
sweet unknown tongue.
Now, blinded with their beauty’s light, how
can I tell it plain,
Their swaying, stooping, soaring grace I
fain would watch again?
Though close they leaned, their veils half-
dropped, and I could see their eyes
That are as green as beryl-stones, as grey
as rainy skies,
I cannot bring the wonder home, nor keep
it clear and free,
A talisman of green and grey—it fades in
spite of me.
At night when all the wild things wake,
these dryads are not shy
(They have small need for any sleep, their j
days go softly by).
Across the clearness of the sky, with small
pale stars bestrewn,
Above a silver forest lone there rides the
naked moon.
The dryads of the blue gums all are danc-
ing in her light—- |
Oh wistful, laughing, lovely ones, so radi- I
antly white,
Clear let me keep the sight of you to guard
me as a charm.
A green and silver talisman to hold my
heart from harm!
N.S.VV.
DOROTHEA MACKELLAR.
The Fount of Youth.
If the olden bards spoke truth
There was once a fount of youth ;
Whoso drank thereof, ’twas sung,
Lived and loved, for ever young.
Never grim, corroding cares
Brought them scant and whitened hairs,
Stooping shoulders, palsied gait,
Sloping to the senile state.
Never testy mood and whim,
Gouty foot and eyesight dim,
Wheezy breath and rheumy tears.
Damped their mirth and dulled their ears.
But for each the bursting bud
Boused their pulses into flood,
While new hopes, on pinions bright.
Sprang from promise to delight.
Just beyond their skyline lay
Glories of a lordlier day—'
Spires and minarets of gold.
Magic wealth of dreams untold !
Age, that never quaffed their cup.
Your mischance was waking up ;
Waking to the sordid scheme —
Had you drunk you still might dream !
Age, that sees all earth decayed—
Fortunes fail, and fashions'fade.
Falsehoods vain, and actions mean—
Death attending every scene ;
Had you drunk that magic draught
You had sung, and danced, and laughed ;
Never waning to the sere
Yellow autumn of the year.
You had tripped, a happy sprite,
Through a world of flowers and light.
Careless as the leaves that fall,
Counting life the best of all;
Action, airy loves and strife,
These had summed your lore of life;
All your thought, and all your time,
Drifting like a mazy rhyme.
Drear and dull are all the schools—
Paradise has happier fools—
Pedagogue and crabbed seer
Never drank these waters clear!
Therefore cherish what remains
Of the drops the shard contains.
Which, so olden hards have sung.
Make the soul for ever young.
Better than a sea of truth
Is that old elixir. Youth;
Drain it not with careless haste
Lest you perish on the was<o !
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused, by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, MAY 14, 1914.
A LEAGUE OF BOY NARKS.
In Australia these days the Boy is more of
an institution than he used to be. He can
no longer be whacked with the cheerful im-
punity of bysone days. He has rights that
were unknown when his toothless grand-
father was young. Wages Boards and such-
like institutions take cognisance of the fact
that he is upon the earth. He is strongly
urged to be a Boy Scout, and he is addressed
in whirling language on Empire Day—or he
used to be, but Empire Day is already almost
dead, and ivill soon be a mere bony remnant
—about his duty to the glorious conglomera-
tion of territories which repose in the shadow
of the throne of Geo. V. And, which is the
most important point of all, the Boy supplies
the bulk, if not the whole, of the compul-
sory army so far -as things have gone. If
war broke out to-morrow —the new defence
system being still very young—little Tommy
could be legally rushed to the front, while
—barring some exceptional legislation for a
levy en masse —his father and his uncle
and his bigeest brother could stay behind
and shout cheerfully at the football match.
The privileges of the Boy have increased
greatly, but so also have his responsibilities.
As one of his responsibilities, the habit of
leaving him the bill to pay, for .no reason
except that the incompetent old man can’t
be troubled, has grown to an enormous ex-
tent. And owing to the lavish extension of
the suffrage, ancient great-grandfathers in
the Benevolent Asylum, who used to be struck
off the roll, and who have hardly a shadow of
interest left in this world’s affairs, may now
be beguiled by flattery or cakes and ale to
vote for the robust candidate who promises
to borrow £8,500,000 a year for his own
glory and the better security of his own
billet, and to leave the whole burden of re-
payment to the Boy. The Boy, with his rifle
and bayonet in the right hand, and his
cannon under the right arm, and the account
for the old man’s profligacies in ' the left
hand, and the old man’s temporary and
unproductive works under the left arm, has
a lot to carry. To some extent the Boy in
this case includes the Girl. She doesn’t
carry the rifle and the cannon, but she
struggles with her share of the liabilities.
Last year the N.S.W. Government did
something heroic in the way of borrowing.
It raised, mostly in England, close on
£8,000,000. (This year it proposes to raise
more.) For a Ministry which attained to
ofiice on its solemn pledge, as a gentleman,
or a fraud, or a miserable liar and shuffler,
or something of the sort, that it would re-
duce borrowing, this is something remark-
able, because the borrowing that it pro-
mised to reduce was well below £3,000,000
a year. Out of £7,703,594 of loan money
actually expended during the year, £813.354
stood for works which even the N.S.W.
Government’s own Statistician didn’t dare,
however much his billet might be endan-
gered, to describe as “reproductive” in any
sense whatsoever. As regards the balance,
£4,618,907 was invested in Railways and
Tramways, which yielded so much less re-
turn every time an extra pound was in-
vested that the large profit formerly derived
from the Rail and Tram Department changed
into a loss. Incidentally, a process of de-
moralisation set in, and it became a common
jest that yesterday’s train might be expected
about the middle of next week, which is
perhaps the reason why the regular weekly
contributions of two of The Bulletin’s
Melbourne correspondents are 48 hours
behind time this week. Water Sup-
ply and Sewerage, which are fairly solvent
items, absorbed £1,595,093. And Harbors
and Rivers, which are wholly insolvent,
accounted for £676,180. None of that money
went to open up a really good harbor which
would be <jf value to the State, for a really
good harbor is liable to compete with Syd-
ney, where Holman and McGowen hang
out and have their political interests. The
borrowed coin was fooled away on shallow
river mouths, where the bar is dredged
away to-day and is silted up again to-
morrow, and where hyacinths and other
fresh-water weeds are making navigation
more and more impossible. As the outcome
of all this muddle, it was found necessary
to pile on nearly £2,000,000 a year of extra
taxation. All these were no small doings
for a party which was pledged, unless it was
a complete scoundrel and had no more prin-
ciple than a Chinaman of the lower orders,
to use no loan money except for works which
would pay full interest and sinking fund. On
top of all the other items were the overdraft
and the miscellaneous deficit. In a liberal,
off-hand fashion these things were left to the
Boy, who has no vote.
As the Boy carries the gun, it is up to
him to enter a protest. There is room for
a League of Boy Narks, which would really
be a much more practical and business-like
concern than a League of Boy Scouts. The
League should be founded right here and
now. Every male youth of the gun-
carrying age should be eligible to join
it, and every girl of similar age. The
business of the Boy Narks League should
be to explain to Coiienstein, abroad and
at home, that it won’t, when it comes of age,
be responsible for any debts incurred by the
unprincipled old man from this date onwards
unless these debts are represented by assets
that will yield enough to cover the interest.
In other words, the Boy who humps the
heat and burden of defence in the present,
and who is supposed to hump the dead horse
in the future, should intimate that he will
no longer drag around his progenitor’s
shady and unjust embarrassments. The pro-
genitor is much addicted to importing because
he is too lazy and unpatriotic to manufac-
ture on a big enough scale; and he
is addicted to going on strike because
he has either got swelled head and
is full of imaginary grievances, or
because he has made such a mess of his
system of government that a suspension of
industry is his only means of obtaining
redress against himself. These things the
old man, with the connivance and assent of
the old woman, has some right to do, though
it is rough on the old woman, for she is very
seldom consulted. But when the old man
sells the public estate, which is the property
of the whole community for all time, and
uses the proceeds as his own revenue, he is
doing something that no gentleman should
do. When he makes the Boy carry the gun
for the defence of the country and all its
contents, he begins to look very much like a
cur. And when, in a single year, in the
single State of N.S.W., he bands down to
the Boy a bill for nearly £8,000.000 of fresh
loans, with nothing save a huge deficit to
set against the interest charge, the old man
looks very’like a common confidence trick, or
a slave-dealer who sells his children for
tobacco and drink and football money. In-
cidentally, the old man in Westralia is an
even more violent borrower, in nrooortion
to population, than the one in N.S.W. But
the Westralian person has this shred of
decency: He attaches a sinking fund to everv
loan and does some of the repayment himself.
The possibilities of the Boy Nark as a
political force have not yet been realised. A
vast society might be. formed out of the
young people who have become old enough
to take up the rifle and the bayonet, and yet
are not old enough to vote. These folk, in
the hands of a Baden Powell or a Dan
O’Connor, or a brilliant lunatic, might be a
power. They would simply explain that,
when a sufficient number of them came of
age to control affairs, they would hold an
audit of the accounts of their miserable and
shuffling parents, and pay as much as was
fair, but no more. When the middle-aged and
drill-shirking father Smith and uncle Smith
combine with grandfather Smith —now in
his dotage and tottering on his last legs—to
put the name of grandson Smith on the back
of an I O U, and use the proceeds for pur-
poses which leave no visible good behind
'them —well, it is up to grandson Smith to
say plainly that the dust of the borrower
and the bones of the lender may settle the
matter between them. Grandson Smith has
every justification, in certain circumstances
and provided he gives due notice, to repudiate
the signature which he didn’t write, but
which certain dreadful ancestors wrote for
him. An announcement of audit and possi-
ble repudiation, served on the universal
money-lender by a numerous body of Boy
Narks, would make the financier pause in his
headlong career. It would supply him with
new ideas. Hitherto he has fancied that he
could take as security the flesh and blood,
the body and soul, of everybody under the
voting age, and regard the security as good.
But now that the voteless, under-age Aus-
tralian of the present generation is the only
legitimate and autlioidsed and compulsory
defender of his country, he has some right
to say that he won’t be sold like a naked
cow in the market.
THE NIGGER AND THE NORTH
The same old sidelong crawl after colored
labor in the Northern Territory ap-
peared again last week in the Syd-
ney Telegraph —the sidelong, crab-like lit-
erary dodge which may end in selling half
Australia, or all Australia, to the heathen,
unless something of a plain, simple, business-
like character is done. It isn’t necessary to
attempt anything extraordinary to avert
the crawl. All that wants doing is the ob-
vious—the matter that is sticking out —the
sort of affair which is perpetrated anywhere
else in the humble course of affairs. As
matters stand, the Fisher Government
marooned a lot of new officials in a sort of
Robinson Crusoe isolation at the hottest
end of the Territory. It did this to de-
velop the place and to show the Govern-
ment’s energy, just as it might have sent a
librarian and an Egyptologist and a great
authority on Sanscrit and Chinese art and
Greek roots to Port Stephens or Coff’s Har-
bor or Jervis Bay (N.S.W.), for the same
good purpose. The Cook Government is
now recalling these men to'show its economy,
and between the energy on one side and the
economy on the other, the place gets very
little show. On this subject the Sydney
Telegraph spoke last week as follows:—
The cancellation of five official appointments in
the Northern Territory may be justified by the saving
of the £3250 a year that will result from it, but
the question is: What does 'the Government propose
to do next? The return south of these men will, it
is said, seriously diminish the official population of
the Territory. And seeing that the official popula-
tion constitutes the mainstay of the settlement, as it
dwindles, so must the rest of the community, which
lives largely on the money which the Government em-
ployees have to spend there. What this retrenchment
indicates is that the scheme of the late Government
for settling the Territory is now a demonstrated and
acknowledged failure. Instead of the official popula-
tion following ordinary settlement it went before,
and has been left there with practically nothing to
do. WHITE MEN WILL NOT FOLLOW TO DO
THE WORK that in other tropical countries is done
by coolies, even though special wage inducements are
offered, and in the absence of manual labor develop-
ment must remain at a standstill. . Briefly, that is
all the very expensive experiment tried by the late
Government, and continued by their successors, has
resulted in proving. And the beginning now made
with the dismantling of the useless official machinery
may be regarded as the admission of that fact. For
this retrenchment, indicating as it does the failure of
the settlement scheme, is bound to be followed by
more. The question, therefore, is: Has the Govern-
ment got an alternative scheme, and, if not, what is
proposed to be done with the white elephant of the
North?
There is always somewhere in this sort
of story the insinuation that colored labor
is the one thing that will save the Terri-
tory. White men, it is said, “will not fol-
low to do the work.” The question arises:
What work is there for them to do? The
Land Board at Darwin, sitting down amid a
complete absence of instructions, intimated
recently that it hadn’t yet prepared a scheme
to make some bits of soil available for selec-
tion. In the absence of instructions that
could hardly be called the Board’s fault. The
railway extension hasn’t started. The
man who grows things in the neighbor-
hood of Darwin depends on a very long and
very precarious steamboat service to get his
cabbages to market. The boat comes in
semi-occasionally, and stays a minute,
and flees. The man on the Roper
River depends on a wonderfully casual
ketch, or river craft, to get his produce even
as far as Darwin, and the vegetable is liable
to be three months old before it gets to a
great centre of population. Yet the fer-
tility of the Territory is such that Port
Darwin doesn’t get on any worse than Port
Stephens or Twofold Bay or Jervis Bay,
which are close alongside Sydney. On the
evidence of results, it is no more in want of
colored labor than they are. Moreover, it'
has tried colored labor on a bigger scale
than , any other province of Australia, and
with the result that the Tory press per-
sistently calls it a failure, a dead loss, a
white elephant, and a place that can only
be redeemed by drinking another cask of the
dog that bit it.
* * * *
There would be no reason to hurry and
fuss about the development of the Northern
Territory if it was possible to get a reliable
guarantee that no colored race would at-
tempt to take the place. It would develop
itself, at leisure, just as some other parts of
Australia did before the Colored Danger or
Yellow Peril developed, and just as England
and France and Germany did. There being
reason, however, to assume that it is desir-
able to put on a spurt, it seems a fair
thing to spurt. That can’t lie done
by sending a horse doctor, or a school inspec-
tor, or a digger-up of Scandinavian myths,
or an authority on old coats of mail out to.
the Back Yard of the Remote. They may
be good men in their line, but even the best
bookbinder or collector of stamps is of no
value about the upper waters of the Ama-
zon. The Territory is no worse than River-
ina was before roads and railways were
built, and Port Darwin is exactly the same
sort of incomprehensible white elephant that
Botany Bay was before the ’bus started run-
ning and the, tram-line was thought of.
Probably if the original Australian settle-
ment had been made on Port Stephens instead
of P.ort Jackson, the present site of Sydney
would be a similar elephant, and the Port
Stephens Telegraph would hold that only
abundant Chinese labor could make Potts
Point habitable.
* * * *
The disaster of the Territory is that it is
a sort of pawn in a political game. Most of
the country is high, cool, reasonably fertile,
well-watered, and altogether promising. But
it can’t be connected by railway with the
older settlements without connecting it with
some place. The straight line to which the
Australian Government is pledged can’t be
built without alienating votes in the East.
The proposed crooked deviation can’t be con-
structed without the loss of votes in the
Centre and West. So nothing is done save
the occasional despatch by an energetic Gov-
ernment of a Professor of Theological Bot-
any or a Lecturer on Dry Bones or some
person of the sort, by a three or four or five
weeks’ sea trip, to the tail of things, where
he is doomed to live among an Asiatic popu-
lation. And then there follows his occa-
sional recall by an economical Government,
which says that the Asiatics don’t require
any Theological Botany or any Lecturer on
Dr.v Jobs. So the Territory’s big port re-
mains not much better than Westernporf
CVic.) is to-day. The remedy is to take the
line of least resistance, and at present the
width of that line is exactly 4ft. 84in.
PANKHURSTIAN PHILOSOPHY.
The oration of “0.1 v.”
British suffragettery, represented by the pleasing
personality of Miss Adela Pankiiurst, had a lot
to say for itself at Melbourne Auditorium the other
night. Adela is a quite nice Pankiiurst of uncer-
tain age, with a winsome voice, a fluency of speech
inherited from her dauntless progenitor, and a good
sense of humor, which is so excellent and unusual
a thing in woman. Moreover, she doesn’t blow
the family trumpet. Within the limits of her ob-
session she is equally tactful and witty. She spoke
for an hour or more of suffragette performances, and
how they dated back to 1900, when two compara-
tively harmless women were slung out of a public
meeting for making less disturbance than is practised
with impunity by male hooligans at election times.
She misrepresented the Suffrage movement militant as
a belated rebellion of Everywoman against the injus-
tice suffered by women workers in comparison with
male ditto. She brought down the house by explain-
ing that the vandalism of militancy was woman’s
way of gaining a political -.end/ and that only by
injuring Cabinet Ministers inThgir precious dignity
could the suffragettes bring them to submission, see-
ing that men squirm under satire and purr under
flattery. And this she said with a -pitying smile at
man’s weakness, as though women, on lithe other hand,
despised flattery and accepted satire with pleasure.
Because they have been the coarse joke of the male
world in all ages and all society, therefore they don’t
mind being laughed at. “Women are used to such
ridicule,” said the gentle Adela. They are also
used to having men’s hats raised to them, to having
men’s seats in public vehicles given up to them, to
having all sorts of courtesies empty or solid paid to
them by the men whom they ridicule —but Miss
Pankiiurst said nothing of these things.
* * * *
Her speech was a pink tissue of partisanterie, em-
broidered with forget-me-nots of truth, edged with
sentimentality and tasselled with the striking sug-
gestion that a Unionist Government will, if the
Liberal Government gets souted at next election, ex-
tend the franchise to - womfen in order to silence
the militants and look-dignified. /Both the matter
and the manner of Miss P.’s interesting harangue
showed all the sexual egotism of the suffragette.
She never spoke of men save in a spirit of restrained
antagonism. She admitted no excuses for their
failures as workers, politicians, husbands, fathers or
sons. There were tears in her voice when she spoke
of long-suffering wives, self-sacrificing mothers, and
pale, wan factory girls, who sell their “souls” to
brutal man because they lack means to keep body
and soul together. But of hungry husbands and
henpecked fathers, striving to support the family
without the strength of maternal instinct to support
them in their unselfishness ; of the young men whose
prospects are ruined by their natural but unfortunate
infatuation for feather-brained gadabouts; of the
well-meaning lads whose characters are turned awry
by vicious flappers—of these suffering males the one-
sided Adela had not a word to say. They don’t
exist as far as she and her mother and sisters Sylvia
and Christabel are concerned. She lives in a world
where nobody suffers, except womankind.
Talking of the militants destroying works of art
she explained that no picture would be worth consid-
eration until every woman in creation could look ftie
picture of happiness in a picture hat, or balderdash
to that effect. Adela wa3 consistently illogical as
a matter of course. She pointed out the uselessness
of private members of the House of Commons,
describing them as mere, party voters (which they
are) incapable of effecting any reforms on their own
initiative, and all the while she was arguing that
woman’s happiness depended on the recognition of her
right to share in the foolishness of electing these
impotent members of Parliament. She gloried in
the fact that suffragettes were destroying letters in
pillar-boxes and playing h—l with national treasures
in public buildings, and were making responsible
politicians ridiculous in all men’s eyes.
But she never paused to admit that these militant
tactics, these “women’s ways” of agitating could
not have been pursued but for the allowances which
the Government made for the offenders in considera-
tion of their sex. The suffragettes’ policy of making
themselves a terror to the community has prospered
thus far because the Law has dealt sympathetically
with the hysterical felons for womanhood’s sake. They
think the iron hand of the Law has dealt roughly
with - them, and the mob has illused them, but they
have never experienced a sincere biff from the Law’s
straight left, or' known how nasty an English mob can
be.
Adela doesn’t admit the truth of the present ab-
surd situation in Britain, where no suffragettes have
been scared out of their militancy as they were in
Dublin, on the one and only occasion when they tried
to get a footing on Irish soil. Doubtless she is blind
to the truth of it. Her mind has but one eye which
is fixed ever upon the Cause, the whole Cause, and
nothing but the Cause. And what is the Cause?
Justice for women. Political equality with men.
The right to vote for useless M’s.P. with a view to
raising the average 7s. 4d. per week wage of women
workers to the average £1 Os. lid. per week wage
of the male worker, without consideration to the
difference in the sorts of labor, or the rights of
fathers to provide maintenance for wives and chil-
dren where there are means of support to be sued
for under man-made Law (Eve’s curses on its injus-
tice!), and without consideration of the economic
question whether the raising of women’s wages must
result in an average reduction of the men’s.
This is the political Cause for which Miss Pank-
tiurst and a few hundreds or thousands of energetic
women are agitating - as though the Millennium were
attached to it. They haven’t thus far persuaded the
vast unthinking majority of their own sex, or con-
vinced the deep-thinking minority of the other sex
that women are entitled to the franchise, or ever can
be entitled to it, until they qualify themselves as
sailors, roadrnakers, soldiers, pioneers and agricul-
turists, ship builders, miners, iron.workers, factorv
builders, machinery inventors and performers ot all
the great productive forms of labor which arc essen-
tial to the civilisation on which the system of Par-
liamentary government is founded. The suffragettes
who fail to win anybody to their Cause bv burning
a church would be unanswerable if they built a bridge
or constructed a railway. Every thoughtful Person
hopes for a steady improvement in the condition of the


masses of women and men workers, but no student
of Parliamentary history sees how the improvement
can be hastened by putting women on an equality
with men as regards the franchise.
* * *
Man makes practically everything that makes the
difference between savage and refined life. His
poetic brain has created the splendidly-false ideals
of womanhood from which social courtesies radiate.
It is the Y.M.C.A., not the Y.W.C.A , which
is about to inaugurate an annual Mothers Day a
holy feast of St. Mother —in this country, and
nobody asks, “What about poor Father”?
AVomen owe all the comforts, all the graces of
civilisation, to man’s initiative, enterprise and hard
labor. All the grinding poverty that Adela Pank-
hukst complains of is due to circumstances over
which the masses of men are beginning to obtain
control through unionism of labor, the advantages
whereof they have shown to women. Women in
English-speaki: g countries certainly get more than
a half share of all the luxurious home life and
public entertainment that is enjoyed by the wealthy
and “comfortably-off” classes. The Franchise could
not give them more. And in the sharing of life’s
worst hardships, taking all sorts of poverty and
shabby gentility together, who shall say that women
come worst off, and that their undue proportion of
suffering is due to their disfranchised state?
Adela Pankhurst may say so, but she is talking
from her fanaticism, not from her own or any
other woman’s experience. Here in Australia she
talks to women who have had the vote for years.
Well, under the influence of Woman Suffrage poli-
tical Government of the Commonwealth has come to
a standstill, with a fair prospect of staying there.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Woman Who was Dashed.
The Bulletin tries hard to live at peace
with all men, except public borrowers, anti-
Defence folk, and advocates of long hours,
low wages, land monopoly, big importations,
strikes and nigger labor; also, the indivi-
duals who say that the Northern Territory
is a hopeless proposition; likewise apostles
of Congestion, floaters of wild cats, wowsers,
bigots, rich clubmen who offer a Dreadnought
to Britain but don’t provide the money," and
a few dozen or hundred sundries. But at
times it finds its errand of mercy rather
burdensome, and one character who makes it
so is the Rev. Yarrington, of Sydney. As
an instance of its troubles with the Rev.
Yarrington, it quotes the following from a
daily paper of last Saturday:—
The Rev. S. D. Yarrington, organising secretary
of the Anglican Mission Zone Fund, speaking at a
largely-attended meeting at Hornsby yesterday after-
noon, said that the problems which faced the Church
were gigantic. Sydney, with its crowded and. con-
gested areas, wherein was found the undercurrent of
iniquity, with all its flotsam and jetsam, needed the
practise of practical Christianity. No man could
view the morality of Sydney with calmness.. Thou-
sands of young women were DASHED to moral
destruction every year. Legislation had miserably
failed to stem the tide.
Now, about the thousands of young women
who are DASHED to destruction every year
in Sydney, according to the Rev. Yarring-
ton. A woman can only be wholly dashed
once. When she has been dashed she can’t
be dashed back again. No one can undo
moral destruction. Therefore the matter
becomfes one of figures and common-sense and
reasoning. Supposing womankind in the beau-
tiful Harbor City is dashed to utter ruin at
the average age of 19. The Rev. Yarring-
ton may take any other age he pleases as
the average one at which the dash and the
ruin are done, but 19 years is suggested as a
fairly possible age. According to the census
of 1911 there were 6824 women of that age
—not in Sydney alone, but in Sydney and all
its 40 or more suburbs. If Yarrington likes
to take 10 as the age of destruction, there
were 6229 women to choose from —or to
dash from. The Rev. Yarrington’s large
statement about thousands of young women
going to moral corruption in Sydney every
year certainly seems to imply that half, or
three-quarters, or perhaps the whole of the
flappers who, in any particular year, attain
the age at which marriage or something of
the kind is possible, become Raiiabs and
loose characters. There are quite a number
of decent citizens —including Jews, Roman
Catholics, Unitarians and Agnostics—who
would resent the implication that from one-
half of their daughters-to all their daughters
are Rahabs, but for the fact that their utter
contempt for the Yarrington kind of man
makes it hardly worth while to reply to
him. If they took that kind of preacher seri-
ously they would possibly hang him on a
lamp-post. What saves him is (1) the fact
that very few people read his fulminations,
and (2) the fact that those who do read
them pass them by with a pitying smile as
mere hysteria. The father of a family has
either to sandbag the Yarrington species of
cleric or ignore him. As a rule, he ignores
him. And in doing so he generally ignores
the church that he belongs to, and which is
sufficiently low-down to tolerate him. The
incumbent who states, or suggests, or implies
in a general sort of way, that the bulk of the
young women of a big city—and a fairly
respectable city—like Sydney are lost souls,
is a misfortune. He and his kind keep the
intelligent class—which is very often the
paying class, and the class which takes a
regular hot bath—away from the church.
Those who take sufficient interest in church
affairs to read the sayings of this variety of
parsondom are liable to get an idea that the
tabernacle is dirty and ignorant and hope-
less. They think it looks like an ash-barrel.
Those who don’t take sufficient interest in
church affairs to read the sayings of parson-
dom never go to the tabernacle in any case.
A bitter Sydney barrister once told the writer
that the" only witnesses who can never be
relied upon are clergymen and women. He
was not sober at the time, and he died soon
afterwards. Still, he was a wise old man in
his way, and he knew things.

About Clubs.
Last week the Naval and Military Club.
Melbourne, agreed by 56 votes to 18 to expel
Major T. M. MTneu.ney because, at a Home
Rule meeting, he had said about Lord Rob-
erts things which till the present Belfast
trouble started, had been accepted generally
as platitudes. M’lnerney alleged that Rob-
erts was suffering from “senility” (“senility”
is defined by Webster as “old age,” and
Roberts is 83) ; that he had delayed the
finish of the S’African war by his hesitant
methods; and that, whereas he was “dra-
matic, old and worn-out,” Kitchener was
“vigorous and powerful.” An old-established
axiom lays it down that the rottener a club
is the touchier it is. In London, during the
Boer War, while men like Campbell-Ban-
nerman, Sir Edward Grey, Lord Morley,
Asquith, ‘General Butler, and so forth, were
denouncing the “methods of barbarism” of
their-countrymen, in and out of historic clubs
like Brooks’s and the Athenaeum, and suffer-
ing not at all therefrom in a social sense,
obscure traders were being ousted in shoals,
for a precisely similar 'offence, from pot-
houses like the Junior Constitutional. A bad
club is as embarrassing a circumstance to be
associated with as a man who is doubtful of
his position. Neither of them is able to act
naturally. They both alternate between
blatant arrogance and equally blatant servil-
ity. Also, it is not easy to know when they
are going to be arrogant and when servile.
However, a general line may be got in this
\/f 7 : Whatever political or other doctrine is
being espoused for the moment, by the people
with most money and so on, will be adopted
eagerly by both these types. The more the
newspapers from which they get their in-
spiration blither on the subject, the more ex-
cited they will become. After a certain stage
they will accept the last extremity of press
guff , as imperishable truth. They will then
be prepared to tear to pieces, as a traitor,
pro-Boer, disloyalist, Socialist, or what not,
anyone who keeps his head sufficiently to be
able to see things in the light in which they
stood before the uproar began. A majority
of the members of the Naval and Military
Club, Melbourne, is evidently at the blatant
stage at the time of writing. Such solemn
asses are capable, for the moment, of leav-
ing a smoking-room as a protest against
someone who happened to mention that Sir
Edward Carson once owned a dog that got
the mange. In six months or so the Belfast
trouble will be over. The drunk will have
passed. The Naval and Military Club will
pull itself together, and wonder what all the
fuss was about, and why it made a public
idiot of itself.
The U.S. Beef Trust, Its State Rights
Ally, and the Horrible Position of
Britain.
Dear Bulletin, —It is four years now since
the U.S. Beef Trust began sparring for an
opening in Australia. Reputable papers all
over the U. States mentioned at the time, and
have been mentioning at intervals
ever since, that the Beef Trust needed
to collar the bulk of the Australian
meat-exporting trade, if it was to
fulfil its dream of making good in the United
Kingdom. Public men of standing, both in
Britain and the U.S. said the same thing.
Incidentally The Bulletin said it. Liberal-
ism in Australia, speaking with the superb
confidence of a Party which never looks a
yard beyond its Q.wn bailiwick, said that the
whole thing was preposterous; that the Beef
Trust would never want to come to a God-
forsaken place like Australia; that if it did
happen to go mad and decide to begin opera-
tions here, our Anti-Trust laws were ade-
quate to cope with it; and that, in any case,
those laws wouldn’t be needed, since the rail-
ways were “in the hands of the States.” As
a sort of final string to its argumentative
bow, Liberalism averred that it would be
an excellent thing for everybody if the Trust
did settle down in Australia. In due course
the Trust began business in Queensland.
Liberalism continued to deny its existence, so
far as Australia was concerned—this though
Swifts had already spent some £2,000,000 in
the Northern State. Last week, however, the
Party, speaking with the mouth of Prime
Minister Cook, officially swallowed all its
denials of years past. Here is a Parlia-
mentary extract which would have been un-
thinkable as lately even as six months ago:—
Mr, Sharpe (Q.): Swift and Company had
established the largest works in the world
on the Brisbane River, works that would be
capable of dealing with 1000 head of cattle
and 5000 head of sheep daily. They had
also obtained control of works at Townsville and
Cairns, and it was rumored that 1000 square miles
of country had been acquired in Central Queensland,
together with the Lakes Creek Works, near Rock-
hampton. Armour and Company had arranged with
Swift and Co., of Brisbane, to kill cattle for them.
Mr. Cook said that this question had just been
under discussion for over three weeks on the censure
debate, yet the first proposal after this censure was
disposed of was this same matter. -
Mr. Webster (N.S.W.) : Do you object?
Mr. Cook : Yes, I do ; because it is bailing up the
business while it is giving us no new information.
The Trust has been in Australia two years.
And now the Party which has all along
denied that the Trust was in Australia, or
was capable of doing any damage if it did
come here, proposes to make a belated effort
to deal with it. That anything short of an
amendment of the Constitution, giving the
Australian Parliament full control, not only
of inter- but of intra-State commerce, must
necessarily be futile, was shown by the lyric
outburst of Q. Minister for Agriculture
White, last week. Said this large-visioned
Imperial patriot:—
Many people had the idea that because a large
company was erecting works on Brisbane River it was
the cause of the rise in the price of meat. He
hoped the price would go still higher, because every
penny would help Queensland. Queensland was
going to be the abattoirs of the world. If the big
companies dealing in meat were prepared to spend
their thousands here he would welcome them, and
be glad to see them. The more that came the bet-
ter. He was not afraid of the Labor party or what
they thought. They could do with these great syn-
dicates. It was going to be a great benefit, and all
that money would come into Queensland.
Undoubtedly the above expresses the view
of the typical State-righter. What does it
matter to White that the price of meat
should rise all over Australia, provided
Queensland becomes “the abattoirs of the
world?” What does he- care that the poor
and middle-class folks of the United King-
dom should have to pay monopolist prices
for their meat so long as great syndicates
scatter largesse in his corner of the earth?
In no way does one get a better estimate of
the,- fraudulency of the average “Imperial
spirit” than by persuading the Whites of
this continent to give their views on the Beef
Trust question. This Trust which White
welcomes is not likely to harm Australia
materially perhaps for many years. It is
going to do Queensland a lot of good. There-
fore, let it be received with cheers. Its objec-
tive, so far as profits are concerned, is, of
course, Britain. High Commissioner Reid
put this point very plainly during his recent
visit to the Commonwealth. He remarked — 1
acting, obviously, under London influences—
that the U.S. Beef Trust was rapidly gain-
ing a footing at Smithfield; that it already
controlled the whole of the British meat
supply from Argentina and the U.S.; and
that the only factor which debarred it from
declaring a Trust price for imported meat in
London, day by day, was the ever-present
possibility of the arrival of a shipment of
Australian meat. Swifts and Armours, as
Reid suggested, are not lavishing millions in
Queensland to secure the comparatively pal-
try trade of Australia. They are after the
profits that are to be wrung from the millions
of meat consumers that inhabit the United
Kingdom. The Queensland Government wel-
comes Swifts. Armour and Co. It re-
gards the Beef Trust as a blessing. It is
prepared to put its railways, or anything else,
at the Trust’s disposal in consideration of
the thousands the latter proposes to spend.
Where is the use of the Cook party blither-
ing about restrictive legislation, in the cir-
cumstances? None but the Queeensland
Parliament can hamper the Beef Trust in
the present state of the Australian Consti-
tution ; which is only another way of saying
that unless human nature changes completely,
in the next year or so, the outlook as re-
gards the already ill-nourished Britisher is
blacker than any ink. —Yours, etc., 10-2-4.
Boots and Wheat and Pimlico.
Percy Hunter, Director of the Immigra-
tion Department, who is in Britain whoop-
ing up farm inhabitants for the empty spaces
of N. S. Wales and Victoria, has written a
report to the effect that he finds recruiting
“tremendously difficult,” and things are
worse than middling. In spite of all the
efforts of the considerable organisation at
Percy’s command, only 17 families of
alleged landseekers were secured for Victoria
in the first four months of the year, in addi-
tion to a handful who described themselves
as agricultural laborers, probably on the
strength of some distant connection with a
barrow of turnips. From this it looks as
though people elsewhere are beginning to
perceive that in Australia the farmer is
being handed the dirty end of the higli-
wages stick. He certainly is getting the
dirty end whether people elsewhere perceive
it or not; and he himself has a vague and
uncomfortable feeling that it is so. Almost
the whole community is at the game' of
“passing it on.” The cost of living goes up
sixpence, whereupon the employee in the
clothes-peg factory asks and generally gets a
rise—not of sixpence, but of a shilling, to be
on the safe side. Then the man who owns
the clothes-peg factory, finding that the cost
of making the weapons has advanced 2d.,
sends up the price—not 2d., but 4d., for he
wants to be on the safe side, too. After
which, the laundry-man, finding it costs |d.
more to wash Reginald’s shirt and add to
it the discomforts demanded by civilisation,
requires Id. more of Reginald, who notes
it as one reason why he should get a rise of
ss. a week. So the whole business goes on
and on and round and round till it gets to
the farmer who is trying to grow some wheat
up beyond Rupanyup, or outside of Bogga-
bri. Every time he opens his mouth to buy
the high cost of living jumps up and hits
this cocky in the teeth. He has to pay more
than he used to for his clothes and his boots,
for his farm implements and his bags and
his twine and his tinned tucker; and when
he makes his solemn pilgrimage to town the
sixpenny meals of old are ninepence, and his
old fourpenny-tram ride in Sydney on Sun-
day has swelled up to sevenpence. He is
told that high prices are a blessing and a
sure sign of prosperity, and he would pro-
bably not argue the point if any of the prices
came his way. But when he opens his
mouth to sell, all he hears about high prices
is that the cost of living has gone up, conse-
quently freights have been advanced, and
there’s another ha’penny a bushel to pay for
commission. The price he gets, less the
advanced freight and the commission with
the extra ha’penny tied to it, is not what
Australians can afford to pay out of their
much-increased wages, but what the for-
eigner can pay out of wages which have
hardly increased at all. The butter producers
and mutton growers are better off. They get
the uttermost farthing that it is safe to
extract from Australia, and what can’t be
consumed on the premises is exported, and
sold very frequently at a price much lower
than Australia has paid. The man who re-
tails boots in George-street or Bourke-street
doesn’t care tuppence what footwear is fetch-
ing in Piccadilly or Pimlico; what has that
got to do with him, he wants to know, so
long as he has a decent Protective duty?
His concern is with the wages-levels and the
consequent price-levels of his own country.
If he is called upon to pay more for making
boots —very well; he will charge more and the
depressed state of the boot-trade in Pimlico
is nothing to him. But with the wheat-
farmer the foreign price is everything. The
Australian, having had his wages put up,
could afford to pay more for his wheat,
ground into flour; and he ought to pay more,
because it is costing more to grow. But he
doesn’t pay more. Unlike almost everything
else except a few such things as wool, copper
and tin, he pays for wheat at a price fixed
not by local but by foreign conditions, while
the farmer is paying out on everything lie
needs at prices fixed by local conditions. And
the farmer cannot go on that way indefi-
nitely. There is a breaking point somewhere,
and it will be a shockingly bad thing for this
country to reach it. Apart from the fact
that it is a most wasteful thing to leave capi-
tal idle —and the agricultural lands of Aus-
tralia represent the largest part of the coun-
try’s capital—the States have spent their
best borrowed millions on railways into agri-
cultural lands; and if those millions don’t
earn anything, there will soon be a pretty
poor look-out for the other millions. How
the difficulty is going to be got fiver this
paper doesn’t know for certain ; all it knows
for certain is that when the farmer pays
out all the time on a high-wage basis, and
collects all- the time on a low-wage basis,
farming is not going to be popular; and
yet if farming doesn’t become far more popu-
lar than it is even now, Australia is going
to have a large unearned interest bill to pay
on its public works, and there will be a
larve increase in the chances of a brown man
looking down at us from the Empty North
along the barrel of a gun.,
About Sky-Pilotage.
The Bulletin received this letter (6/5/’l4)
in respect of a controversy which is growing
somewhat wearisome:—
Dear Bulletin, —As, in spite of my contradiction
regarding your statements, founded, as you admit,
upon newspaper reports, you still continue to affirm
that the Departmental machines are dangerous, I
make a further effort to convince you of the error of
your ways. , ,
You seem to draw some consolation from the
fact that the Press reports upon which you formed
your opinion were never contradicted. Might I
ask what is the use, so far as you are concerned, of
contradicting reports when, in spite of such contra-
diction, you allow one of your correspondents in the
very next column to that in which the contradiction
appears, to re-affirm the canard as if it were a proved
and accepted fact? (See issue of 9/4/T4.)
I do not know that I can offer any better proof
that the machine, which you describe as dangerous,
as prehistoric, and as a death trap, was in my
judgment a safe and reliable one than the fact that
I, with every confidence, accompanied the depart-
mental aviator in one flight and certainly hope to
repeat the experience before long. For the rest, let
the aviators who use the machines speak for them-
selves. I enclose their report.
Regarding barrack expenditure, you say that what
you took exception to was the fact that provision
had been made on the current year’s estimates
for a continuance of these relatively unnecessary
works. These buildings had been started, as you
admit, contracts let and the work was nearing com-
pletion during the last financial year; for which I
was in no way responsible. The only amounts appear-
ing on the current year’s Estimates are those necessary
to pay the balance of the contracts then drawing
to completion. Unless provision had been made to
meet these liabilities, the Government would have
been a financial defaulter and the buildings left in-
complete. Do you seriously suggest that this alterna-
tive should have been adopted?—Yours faithfully,
E. D. Millen.
Senator Millen encloses a document headed
“Report by Departmental Aviators Lieuten-
ant Petre and Lieutenant Harrison.” It is
much too long to print in full, but the gist of
it is that the machines which The Bulletin
suggested some months ago were dangerous
are in reality perfectly safe. According to
the aviators in the employ of the Defence
Department:—
The articles in The Bulletin are founded upon
sensational accounts appearing in Melbourne papers,
written by persons without technical knowledge of
aviation.
The Melbourne representative of Sydney
Evening News interviewed Aviator Haw-
ker on the 4th inst. Hawker can hardly be
described as “a person without technical
knowledge of aviation.” According to the
News (5/5/’l4) :
Mr. Hawker had a hearty word of commendation
for the work which the Commonwealth Defence avia-
tors are now doing at Point Cook. At the end of Jan-
uary he condemned the machines then in the Defence
Department’s stores as antiquated and useless. Now,
however, he declares that, with the two new li. K.
machines Lieutenants Eric Harrison and Petre are
doing capital work.
The position, therefore, is as follows: The
aviators in the employ of the Defence De-
partment agree, for the purposes of publica-
tion, that the old machines are safe. Millen
is of the opinion, for what it is worth, that
the machines are safe. Aviator Hawker, on
the other hand, who is entirely free from
political influences, considers that the
machines in question are “antiquated and
useless.” In this matter The Bulletin is
satisfied to back the opinion of Hawker.
As regards most of the absurd barracks ex-
penditure this paper is certainly of the
opinion that it should have been cut off at
the metre at the earliest possible opportunity.
The money saved even after the contractor's
had been genefously compensated, would
have gone a long way in the direction of buy-
ing and housing and maintaining those hydro-
planes which Hawker recently asserted to
be “indispensable for Australian coastal de-
fence work.”
The eternal hold-up has been in progress
for some weeks at Cockatoo Dock, Sydney,
and the building of the warships on which
the very existence of Australia may shortly
depend has been consequently delayed. This
time it wasn’t a case of the artisan being
locked out by the private capitalist, or strik-
ing against the private capitalist, or striking
against his own Labor Government, or strik-
ing against his own Arbitration Court. It
was a case in which certain unions dis-
agreed. Now, any person has a right to
leave off work at his own expense and risk,
whenever he feels that way. But in N.S.W.
nobody seems to do anything quite at his
own expense. Under an utterly rotten
regime, the State has got to the
point of borrowing about £8,000,000 per
annum abroad, and leaving the stupendous
bill to the kid, with nothing to show for it
as yet save a great increase of taxation and
a grievously diminished net revenue from the
business undertakings of the country. The
old man’s monopolies, lockings-out and
strikes, his disputes, his loafings, his swelled
head, his motor-car, his tail-coat, his by-
drinkings, his Fatness, his Leanness, and
his failure to run his own Government
and his own policy and his own Arbitration
system on workable principles—all these, or
rather the cost of them, are left to the in-
fant. And this is done, not through incapa-
city, but simply because the loan habit has
eaten into the public conscience, and it has
become customary to take no thought for the
morrow. The', business isn’t honest, and it
isn’t manly. But the influx of borrowed
money keeps the land boom going, and the
land boom keeps the land-.iobbers and the
land-boomers going, and their advertisements
keep the Tory dailies going, so it is very
rarely that anybody tells the old man what
a careless, casual fraud he has become.
The other day the usual cat and dog couple
came into the N. S. Wales Divorce Court,
both parties being so utterly full up of one
another that they asked the Bench to lay
down a road to a separation. Justice Gor-
don, who heard the case, admitted that the
circumstances justified at least one of the
parties in getting what was sought; not-
withstanding which he proceeded to pour
out the usual well-intentioned advice to the
parties to “make it up”—for the sake of the
child. If the dog can turn cat and the two
can purr in chorus on the hearth, or if the
cat can turn dog and the two can hunt
amicably together, it is first-rate advice; but
iff they can’t—and they very rarely can—-
it is mostly doing the children a shockingly
bad turn to “patch it up” for their sake.
Latching it up for the child’s sake is mostly
patching it up for appearance’s sake: it saves
the parent’s face, but it scratches the child's.
It really wouldn’t matter a great deal how
much snapping and scratching there was out-
side so long as there was none at home; but

when things are patched up “for the child’s
sake” the snapping and scratching have all
to be reserved for the home, and the child
gets the benefit of the lot. Some day civili-
sation is going to make an earlier start at
securing domestic peace and homes where
the youngsters have a fair chance of decent
training and continuous discipline. Probably
it will put up cold chambers and require
every engaged person to sit in : one of them
for three weeks, on a low diet, and think over
matters. And before any marriage takes
place there might be a period of three days
in which either party should have the right
to let the other party know that he (or she)
had thought better of it. In fact, the idea
of fixing a day, say a month before the mar-
riage. when either party might cry off with-
out fear of any legal or other consequences
—presuming they had been living decently
—might be tried even before the cool cham-
bers are built.
“Bosun,” from the flying peak:—
•Judge Backhouse put his finger on what looks
like a weak spot in maritime matters when dealing
with a ferry-boat master before N.S.W. Marine
Court last week. The skipper was in charge of
the Kosciusko, and lie managed to strike bottom
off Middle Head, a proceeding which might easily
have resulted in a collection of damp, untidy corpses,
with barnacles in their whiskers and back hair,
Middle Head' being exposed to the ocean roll
through the Heads. The skipper confessed he didn’t
know the depth of water off Middle Head, or any
other Head in Port Jackson, as he never had seen
a chart of the harbor. The .Judge elicited the inform-
mation that the rules don’t require candidates for
masters’ harbor certificates to show any knowledge
of the depth of water in the port. While the law
deems it mandatory for a harbor-master to have a
certificate, it doesn’t bother to see that the docu-
ment has any special bearing on his work. The
rules objected to have been in force going on for
50 years, so the Judge’s protest is a bit belated.
Still, by much dispensation of Providence it arrives
ahead of the catastrophe the system is calling ten-
ders for.
The much-discussed problem as to whether
a railway passenger can reserve his seat
in a carriage by leaving a rug or an em-
balmed bulldcg thereon while he gads up and
down the platform or sprints to the
refreshment room, where there ‘ is fre-
quently little room and about the
same amount of bona-fide refreshment,
was decided in the affirmative in a Sydney
police-court on May 8. A passenger for the
Blue Mountains pegged out a corner seat in
one of the coaches, put the evening paper on
it, and sauntered up the platform. The mana-
ger of one of Sydney’s biggest eating palaces
entered the carriage, ignored the evidence of
the journal (it wasn’t much in the way of
evidence, anyhow), and jumped the claim.
On the return of the original pegger-out high
words smote the serenity of things generally.
The bun-man stuck to his corner, and the
dispossessed party sat in the middle of the
seat. Eventually a stipendiary magistrate
fined the claim-leaper £2 with £2 9s. costs.
Possibly an appeal would blow the judgment
out. If a man leaves the family diamonds
and plate on his seat it seems to suggest that
that seat is his seat. But papers and cheap
novels are always being abandoned. And if
the fact that Smith says a paper (possibly
one just abandoned by Brown) is his paper
—if that fact enables him to drive Jones out
of his newly acquired corner nook, the life of
Robinson will hardly be worth living, and he
will probably speak bitterly to Thomson on
the subject.
From “10-2-4”:
An astonishingly unfortunate host eventuated last
week, in the person of the Darling-hurst (Sydney)
gentleman who, while entertaining a few friends at a
birthday party, was visited by a herd of persons in
the conventional fancy costume of the burgling craft.
The average suburban birthday party is a modest
affair of claret-cup and such-like horrible drinks,
nlatitudinous oratory, sandwiches, genteel chat about
the menace of Labor and the imperishable charm and
virtue of Danny Levy, bridge at 3d. a hundred, and
so on. Rarely, if ever, at such entertainments does
one find anyone with hundreds of pounds in gold and
notes about him. In fact, the members of the local
Burglars’ Union agreed, some months ago, to place
all birthdav parties, for purposes of classification, on
a level with Band of Hope meetings, L.O.L. gather-
ings, and reunions of Methody clerics who had been
driven to file their schedules by reason of Aus-
tralia’s growing tendency in the direction of lax
Sabbath observance. Yet at this birthday party ready
money was as plentiful almost as it is at Tattersall's
on a settling-day. One genius managed to stuff £lOO
in notes into his mouth as the robbers entered. An-
other furtively cast a hundred or so on the floor and
stood on it. A third turned his priceless diamond
ring round, on his finger, so that he should appear
to be wearing an ornament of little worth. These
three got off lightly. The rest had a horrible time
financially speaking. No wonder the host was furi-
ous, and flatly accused the burglars of “making a"
noise and giving the house a bad name.” The one
bright jrloam in the whole lamentable business con-
sists in the fact that the host is said to have insisted
on fully compensating his friends for their losses,
inasmuch as the back gate of his domicile had been
accidentally left open, thus facilitating the entrance
of the malefactors. An unwritten law of very ancient
standing in Darlinghurst society lays it down that the
back gate (to sav nothing of the front door) shall
alwgvs be hermetically sealed when a quiet birthday
game is going on and there is more than £5OO in
the room.

The dictograph, an instrument for magni-
fying sound, was shown at work on the
stage of Sydney Criterion on Saturday eveu-
ing. Unfortunately the dictograph was used
in the wrong place; it should have been
offered for the use of the audience. By means
of this sqund-magnifier the occupants of the
back and' top seats might have been able to
understand why and how the late Mr. Argyh;
was murdered, what William Ilarrigan said,
what took place in the attic where the dicto-
graph brigade was in action, and much else
that escaped their hearing. “The Argyle Case”
is an interesting drama. Technically it
violates the first rule: “Never keep a secret
from the audience.” But dramatic rules,
like rules of morality, are made to be broken,
provided they can be broken successfully.
The interest of the public consists in its
efforts to discover who killed Mr. Argyle.
People look at the case not through their
own eyes; but through the eyes of Asche
Kayton. the ’tec. Every member of the stage
company is in turn suspected. The dialogue
is cleverly worded so as to keep Asche Ivay-
ton and his hearers guessing. Kayton is no
Sherlock Holmes. He proceeds by a method-
ical examination of the facts of the case,
and by a process of elimination arrives at
the criminal. Much is made of technical
devices for the discovery of crime; but the
arrangements necessary for the installation
and use of the dictograph are so elaborate,
so liable to discovery,, and require so many
manipulators that the dramatic probabilities
are inordinately stretched. And the dicto-
graph only discovers the presence of a cer-
tain man in the counterfeiter’s den—which
could much more easily be discovered by the
battalion of detectives that Kayton employs
to watch the house. Kayton solves the mys-
tery by the old trick of getting two suspects
to accuse each other.
* * * *
Chas. A. Millward, as the detective,
smokes 18 cigars, and makes of the
character a real personality. His work
is finished, liis appearance and manner
effective. William Harrigan, as his
youthful assistant, is pleasant but inaudible,
and, at least in this inconspicuous part,
affords no real evidence why he should be
“presented ” to Australia by the Firm. Ger-
trude Boswell, as the wife of a counterfeiter,
“THE MAYOR OF TONI Of’ AT
SYDNEY ADELPHI.
Bobby H'oolsey helps to fire the bomb.
shows beneath her necessary restraint a
subtle sense of dramatic values. Sylvia
Bremer plays the part of Mr. Argyle’s
adopted daughter on Murielstarry lines, but
with the right degree of dramatic tense-
ness. Her work is highly promising, but
she should exploit her own individuality.
Enid Bennett is infantilely pleasing in an
ingenue part. E. W. Morrison, the pro-
ducer, has a part that, in theatrical jargon,
plays, itself, but he seizes every opportunity
to build up a thoroughly satisfactory charac-
terisation. Arthur Styan, as a voluble
lawyer, shares with Gertrude Boswell the two
most difficult parts in the melodrama, lie
makes of his character a success that almost
overshadows that of the detective. During
the first act on the first night the gallery
stood on its head, or something, when Maggie
Moore entered as a colored cook.
Shaw’s scintillating comedy, “Fanny's
First Play,” was successfully revived at Syd-
ney Little Theatre last Monday evening. This
satire on middle-age sentimentalism, in which
farce mixes with tragedy, can stand revival,
In the part of Juggins, the ducal valet, and
that of the girl who has done time for assault-
ing the police, Hugh Buckler and Violet
Paget revel in characterisations that show
their capacities at their best. The cast is
practically the same as in the first produc-
tion, with the exception of Eileen Robinson,
whose Dora Delaney is an exceedingly pro-
mising first attempt. Reynolds Denniston
is another exception. He has deserted Fanny
and is Under Sealed Order? for J.C.W.
Eileen Robinson needs to weld her con-
ception of the part more firmly together,
On Monday night she was Darling Dora
only in patches, but the patches were admir-
able. The ran is to be a brief one.

After artistically holding the fort for a
week as Myles-na-Coppaleen, in “The Colleen
Bawn,” for Allen Doone, at the Palace (Syd-
ney), Harry Roberts handed over the com-
mission untarnished to Doone on Saturday
night. The latter will hold it down until
Friday when the Colleen will be forsaken.
The following night the first performance
in Australia of “The Burglar and the Lady,”
a comedy-drama by Langton McCormick,
will be given.

Another interesting dog turn is on the
National Amphitheatre (Sydney) pro-
gramme this week. It heads the bill,
and The Bulletin upholds the judg-
ment, with costs on the usual scale.
The Jap athletes; the showy Americans,
Lyons and Callum; and the musical Austra-
lian girls, Muriel Andrews and Ruby Dent,
help to drive away, for the time being,
gloomy forebodings concerning the N.S.W.
alleged Labor Party’s direful finance.
