The management of the Imperial Dancing
Salon. (Sydney) is making elaborate pre-
parations for a carnival on June 2, to be
known as “A Night in Japan.” This early
announcement is made for the ' benefit - of
dilatory citizens of the tango variety, who
have a habit of leaving their box plan ar-
rangements balancing on the verge of dis-
appointment. The dilatory classes have to
get a, rattle on these days at the Imperial.

Despite the trouble in Ulster, the ap-
proach of winter, the remarks of Rev.
Yarrington and other shepherds on the
immodesty and immorality of Australian
girls, the aeroplane flights of M. Guillaux,
of France, the tightness of monev and the
prevalence of drink, the Pantomime at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, continues. This show
has now run for over 7000 miles, and if the
nightly enthusiasm generated by it could but
be translated into terms of horse-power, the
product would suffice to drive a Manly
steamboat for a fortnight.

Ghilom were the somewhat mature pair of
heroines. The latter is specially useful in
a co., because she now takes, "with great
skill, the comic lady character, if wanted

served promotion during the season, Charles
Albert and Miss Novotna danced marvel-
iousiy, and Frank Greene established him-
'oGii nimly as (among other things) a mati-
nee idol. Julius Knight has now come in

From an Adelaide critic: —
The Society of Arts show in Adelaide this year i-
to put it frankly, the weakest for years. Lever aril
Power in England, Hevsen too busy with eomnii-
sions ; even Barnes, Collins and the ilambidge sister
all absent from various causes. And James Ashton v
numerous seashore pieces are not up to his standard.
His son Will, away exhibiting successfully in Mel
bourne, has left a few of his characteristic citv itn
pressions, London or Paris ; and, obviously under his
influence is Giles, with a bold Port Adelaide scene
Mrs. Gwen Barringer shows a lot of hollyhocks and
Heysen-inspired gum-trees from her recent pFckv
(and well-selling) one-woman exhibition, and Rose
Lowcay has some clever English bits. Edward I'avie-
is increasingly good in Kangaroo Island scenes, and
John White sound in some work done while on a
visit to Victoria. Also, the owner has lent two
landscapes, made in France by Margaret MacPherso".
She seems to have come under the spell of the Futur-
ists, and to the unaccustomed eye the first effect is
simply unpleasant. Meanwhile, the Art Gallerv
(near by the other show) has decided to open at
night. The experiment was not considered successful
when tried in Melbourne, but Adelaide fancies that -it
can show better results. The opening night was made
a formal affair, with speeches bv President Sowden,
Premier Peake and Governor Galway, also an address
on symbolism by Edward Davies, architect and
painter. Dr. Bevan and others are to follow with
addresses on the Wednesday evenings, to which, for
the present, the experiment is to be confined.
Ellen Terry, of whose personal charm and
superior talent as an actress, in the very
long ago and not so very long ago, many
writers have said the sweetest things, made
three appearances at Melbourne Town Hall
last week. Pictorially the old artist is all
that was expected of her. Her entrance
upon the platform is a success. Face,
figure and bearing are strikingly suggestive
ot the woman she used to be, and nobody
gazing for the first time upon the ruins of
Ellen Terry will doubt that her reputation
was well deserved. As for the entertain-
ment she has to offer, The Bulletin can
only call it an imposition upon the public.
She does not deliver “a discourse on Shak-
speare’s heroines, with illustrative acting.”
Seated at a ridiculous little table, or stand-
ing beside it, she reads lecturettes from a
huge note-book, the words being printed in
type an inch long. On the Thursday night
last week the poor, lady got away
from the book once or :e and recited a
line or two, but on Sa„uiday she stayed
there all the time. Ilaidly a vestige of
memory remains to her. The matter of her
lecturette is stodgy; her manner of speak-
mg it is as cramped as that of a parson
dealing with an unfamiliar sermon. She
needs to be always sucking a tabloid or
sipping a drink to keep her up to her pain-
tul task. Ellen Terry’s task is so severe
a strain upon her, so painful to the more
sympathetic of her auditors, and so exceed-
ingly unlikely to bring profit to the manage-
ment that the present writer would be glad
to hear she had abandoned all idea of tour-
ing Australasia.

the dialogue, which is poor throughout. And

makes a good attempt to dissruise the stupidity
of the husband character in the first three
acts. “The Rosary” is brightly staged.
The landscape in the opening scene is a feast
of rainbow hues.

This week’s revival of “Dorothy” at Mel-
bourne Royal supplies the want of those per-
sons, mostly very young in the sin of
theatre-going, who have not previously
heard Cellier’s old original opera, or have
not heard it much. But to them who know it
well, and retain general recollections of
brighter performances than this, the present
revival seems a rough makeshift entertain-
ment for six nights only. Except that
Dorothy Brunton is a suitable figure for the
part of the artless rustic bride who looks
forward to an everlasting honeymoon of
matrimony, the principals don’t realise their
characters. The thin libretto, so thin that
the finger of scorn pokes a hole into the tail
end of the second act, gets no color of ISth
century probability from the leading quart-
ette —Florence Yjung, Olive Godwin, Frank
Greene and Roberts. Even if they carried
conviction with their simple love-making
complications, the awful failure of Victor
Prince as Squire Bantam, the physical un-
fitness of Celia Ghiloni for an impersona-
tion' of the Squire’s housekeeper, and the
laborious absurdity of Phil Smith’s Lurcher
would spoil the picture. In two words the
latest “Dorothy” is a wholly inartistic pro-
duction. Chorus, orchestra and dressing
are on a par with the average achievement
of the principals.

other members of the well-remembered
“Wallingford” Co., are returning to the

that the joke doesn’t start before S.lO p.m.

At West’s Palace, Melbourne, the obvious
moral of “The Miracle” is still being
preached with a full accompaniment of
Humperdinckian music and choral singing.
The story of Megildis, whose vagaries upset
a religious congregation and caused various
marvels to come to pass, is only related in
the second half of the programme, however.
In the first half there are the usual topical,
comic and bellowdramatic items which fur-
nish a comparatively profane overture to the
pious instruction to follow.

The vaudeville on tap at Melbourne Bijou
on Saturday was as cheerful and enlight-
ened as other entertainments of the class.
Mantell’s Marionettes provided a neat tra-
vesty of a music-hall show, with stage
artists, orchestra and audience all acting
quaintly at the bidding of numerous strings.
The Four Lamys show that they have had
a sound education in aerial gymnastics ; and
Winifred Stewart warbles songs in a mas-
culine voice of unblemished timbre. Also
this paper can see nothing in the songan-
dance turn of Laragy and Snee to blame
them for, while the Three Vardells, the sil-
ver-ring experts, are similarly undeserving
of bricks or bad language.
* * * *
“The Worst Woman in London” is again
pursuing crime with a head on it at Mel-
bourne King’s. This time Miss Olive
Wilton plays the Baddest Female, and with
the help of red hair and a bale of dress
goods, succeeds in winning the hand of banker
Milford and dwelling temporarily in the
neighborhood of his bags of gold. Also, she
manages to be highly unpleasant to the hero
and his tart, but her schemes for aggrand-
isement come to naught, and she finally suf-
fers the painful consequences of incompetent
villainy. The course of true wickedness
finishes no more smoothly for her artful
accomplice (J. L. Lawrence), who does her
dirty work in a stiff, shopwalker manner
and a persistent belltopper. George Dawe,
as the hero, bears a load of false accusation
without disturbing a crease in his well-cut
clothes; while Tom Cannam is a blithered
wreck reduced to rags and park-dossing
through the raw beer of Old Hingland.
Edward Batteral is a whitewashed ap-
parition from the past, who nearly
brings the Dreadfullest Lady to an
anguished conclusion; and there are
others. None of the others are particu-
larly memorable, bar Miss Chetwynd and
Temple Harrison, tvho merely shout for a
hearing. . A vaudeville party that strays into
the second act is accepted as an irrelevant
interruption to the bloodstained career of
the She Limit. George Whitehead is the
best of the vaudeville team, though he
warbles “On the Road to Mandalay” with
more heroics than the subject warrants. It
is a bad road, and Mandalay isn’t worth
much when the traveller gets there.
Bv “Jean Sibi” :
W. B. Mclnnes, who supplies a portrait and some
views of Morocco, is the most interesting performer
in the present inglorious Vic. Artists’ show at Eastern
Hill, Melbourne. In “The Color Medley” Bernard
Hall has something to say about a lady without any
visible raiment; and he says it with all the rich
emphasis of his color-box. Montague Marks’s bit of
Thames scenery flaunts a red sail in a faint blue
world, while Miss Teague gives a three-quarter view
of Professor Laurie, which seems ample considering
the lady’s conventional treatment of the subject.
Leslie Wilkie shows the front elevation of a flapper.
The likeness of the boots is admirable. Miss Qumbrae
Stewart’s most ambitious effort, “Life and Roses,”
is faithfully painted, though in the face the artist
has probabiy not realised the wßble of her intention.
E. J. Shearsby’s “Impression of Colliris-street”
strikes one as a wilful and unprovoked plagiarism
of a French masterpiece in the Melbourne National
Gallery; and Miss. Yardell’s “Veil Asunder,” with
its lurid lady in ;a green head-dress, leaves little
melodrama to be desired. Alf Fischer scores with
his sun and shadow “Landscape” (No. 13) ; Crozier’s
contributions demonstrate patient endeavor; and
Percy Leason’s are well drawn and numerous.
A. E. Newbury’s portrait of an old lady is appar-
ently student’s work; G. Dunstan’s “Spanish Lady”
is poor but respectable in treatment; and Swinton
Disston’s idea of Ivan Rosenblum might have been
just as effectively rendered by machinery. Like
Bernard Hall, Archibald Collins, of South Aus.,
suspends a damsel with an impaired wardrobe,
while he strays into sentiment in the gentle study
entitled “Memories.’ Among the others the canvases
of Miss Sutherland, Miss Sweatman and Miss Traill
are especially noticeable, and the etchings of Victor
Cobb stand out prominently in the central gallery.

“Harrison O.” : Actor Walter Dalgleish, who had a
fatal seizure, at Fremantle, when about to propose to the
heroine in “The Soldier’s Wedding-,” died as he would
probably have wished to —in the limelight. He was
originally dresser to Johnny Sheridan. He had an
absolute genius for incurring stage mishaps. While
trying, on behalf of a nervous mummeress, the wire
by means of which the heroine of “The Worst Female
in the Fog” escapes from a burning building, he
had a nasty fall, which put him out of action for a
time. When Ada Lawrence rode her horse into the
orchestra, when playing in “The Squatter’s Daugh-
ter,” at Hobart, it was Walter who leaped after her
and tore out the raging beast. He was a skilled horse-
man, and delighted in bringing any nag he was
associated with on the stage aj near to the foot-
lights as possible. Again, when “The Favorite” was
being played at Perth, some years back, part of the
stage collapsed during the big;' race scene. Four
animals disappeared from view, but Dalgleish, on
the favorite, got over the gap in fine style. Two of
the nags were killed and their riders were badly
hurt. Walter last came under public notice a
year or two back while playing the vampire noble-
man in “The Power of the Cross,” at Melb. ’lppo,
when he succeeded in getting himself stabbed in the
face by Ada Guildford, who was the customary armed
adventuress.
“0.K.”: Somebody wrote to a Melbourne paper
the other day, asking: “How long are the Australian
public going to be gulled”—by managerial advts. re
“early doors” and “late doors” ? Also, the indignant
individual wished to know whether a certain theatrical
management is prepared to “sacrifice its dignity to grab
the last penny it can get”? The J. and N. Tait
management happened to be the one which the in-
dignant person was pointedly Referring to, and it
is certain that the Taits have a somewhat exagger-
ated reputation for grabbing at' the last penny, be-
cause there is no disguise of their intentions. But
all managements go for the last penny in one way
or other, and none of them hks much dignity to
sacrifice these times. “Unreserved” stalls are
quoted in the advt. at a lower price than reserved
ditto, yet, if the demand for stalls is sufficiently
urgent on a Saturday night the whole of the
ground floor seating accommodation is sold at
the higher price, and people pay just as
much as the management can get for stand-
ing room only. Dress-circle and family-circle are
interchangeable terms at Her Majesty’s, Melbourne.
A person may pay 6s. one night for a seat which
is available for 3s. on another night of the same
show. And this without there being any application
of the “early door” dodge. The “early door” is
particularly a means of getting the last possible
penny into the amphitheatre. Or perhaps it were
more correct to say that the “late door” is the
means whereby the management achieves its end.
In the case of the indignant citizen aforesaid, who
hoped to hear Harry Lauder for 2s. on a Saturday
because the advt. promised a two-bob minimum after
7.45 p.m., the “late” door never eventuated. Why?
Because, at 7.45, there was still a stream of people
prepared to pay 3s. for “early” door. Granting
that the late-door seekers were lured to the theatre
under false pretences, the Taits may fairly plead
that all showmen do it. The early door is the
apple and the late door is the core, and the mana-
gers, without exception, are the greedy boy who
says, “there ain’t goin’ to be no core this time.”
The simile is false but the effect is truthful. In
times of rush theatrical management in , front of
the house is mainly a matter of “working” many
people to pay more money than they meant to
cash up at the pay-boxes, even as management of the
box plan for reserved seats is oftentimes a matter
of “bluffing” applicants into booking bad or indiffer-
ent seats where they had intended to book good
seats only.
Oscar Asche has experienced Ijis first set-back in
the course of his strenuous managerial career. His
fine robustiousness has touched: the limit. For
some nights lately he was out of the “Kismet”
bill in London, suffering from neuritis.
Lillian Nordica, the operatic soprano who toured
Australia recently under the management of Freddie
Shipman, died on Monday at Batavia. She studied
in Milan, under Sangiovanni, and made her European
cffibut as Violetta, at Brescia. This was close on 40
years ago and she has been more or less famous ever
since. She was a warm upholder of the sacrament
of marriage, and demonstrated it by facing the
matrimonial music on four several occasions —in the
first place with a young man who presently fell out
of a .balloon and was killed ; secondly with one,
Doelime, a singer, whom she divorced ; thirdly, with
a French soldier named de la Mar ; and fourthly, with
a New Yorker by name Young. She was engaged in
a law suit with her former manager Shipman at the
time of her death —or.e of those long, dreary, mys-
terious actions which only the lawyers pretend to.
understand, and to avoid which most folks would
gladly cross the Styx.
“Boh”: Just before the curtain went up for
Alfred Buchanan’s “The Image Breaker,” at the
Repertory show, the other night, in St. Peter’s Hall,
a wild-eyed man, coal-begrimed and ragged, lurched
past the protesting doorkeeper. With a tattered
hymn-book in one shaking hand, and the remains of
a' hat that had never been much of a hat in the
other, he made his way to a seat and, bending for-
ward, appeared to engage in prayer. Presently the
right sort of stout lady in trailing velvet arrived
and, through the polite usher, demanded her seat.
The wild-eyed, prayerful intruder sat tight and mum-
bled things at the usher. At last, finding that
the new verger wouldn’t let him be, he lifted his eyes
and discovered the stout lady. He fixed his eyes
on the expanse of powdered chest which she in her
vanity was pleased to consider full costume, and
breathed hard. Then he pointed the Finger of
Scorn at the revealed flesh and, uttering the loiM,
unmirthful bray of the early (and dirty) Christian,
.dashed down the aisle, only pausing at the door to
.shout some remark about “shameless females.”
before he' could turn, he was seized by the attend-
ants atid shoved from the scandalised premises.
After which a heavy-weight patron of the Australian
drama subsided like a wave into her rightful chair
and rapidly fanned her heaving circumstance.
• “0.K.”: All the time that Lauder was drawing
crowded houses at Melbourne Princess’s (and draw-
ing more than £IOOO a week for his share of the
“houses”), the word went round the city that he
was a frost and the management was losing money.
Rumor belittled the Lauder business from the stiyt
and stuck to its misrepresentation of the case, hence
the suggestion and insinuation that rival manage-
ments were at the back of Rumor. But the man-
in-the-street who talked about “empty seats” at the
Princess’s invariably closed his statement with the
remark that he hadn’t been to hear Lauder and
didn’t mean to go. In fact, quite a lot of people
were “up against” the rugged little comedian be-
cause the Taits had fixed the price for reserved seats
at 10s. 6d. They objected to paying a fancy price
for a “music-hall show.” Similar feeling was
shown the first time Melba returned from Europe.
The people who wouldn’t pay a guinea for one of
her concerts declared that she was a general disap-
pointment. They didn’t want to hear her. They
would sooner hear Dolores. Melba went away and
returned again and again, always with the same
old guinea stamp upon her, and the bitter cry of
disparagement faded away into silence.
Harry Lauder’s talent for advertising himself shone
forth from the brief newspaper mention of his hav,-
ing, whilst in Melbourne, paid a visit to old E. W.
Cole, the Book Arcade man, at his private residence.
Whether Cole invited Lauder, or the visit waSi
arranged by the visitor, the moral is the same.
Cole is undoubtedly the best self-advertised identity-
in Australia. He recognised a worthy brother-
artist in Lauder, or the famous showman spotted a,
great kindred spirit in him. And so they met, andi
got a mutual free advt. from the press.

“Y.Z.”: Consideration of the recent Melbourne
Eight Hours’ proeesh inspires me with nothing but
contempt for the producers of the show. Away
with the garish and futile banners as a start.
Pretty pictures of impossible grafters and simpering
allegories are also out of the ideal picture. How-
much better to present picked workmen hitting it
up at their own games! Also, there should be
representative sports at the workers’ carnival. I
wouldn’t cross a road to see the ordinary, weari-
some sports programme, but I would hoof it for-
miles to witness a competition of carpenters, black-,
smiths, tinkers, bootmakers, etc., with the most,
skilled workmen hustling all they know. Wood-
cutting has provided a popular sample of athletics.
Why not stonecutting or bricklaying? Asa finali
objection, the Eight Hours’ procession runs to in-
ordinate length. The one effort at its improvement;
appears to be to stretch it. This latest march past;
was on a gusty, north-wind day, and the bearing
of banners was a protracted struggle. Ask me to,
state the show in brief, and I reply: “Linked sweati-
ness long drawn out.”
(Continued on page 36.)
At the Sydney Mayor’s lunch to Sir Hart-
mann Just, N.S.W. Gov.. Strickland said
they had taken the Colonial Office potentate
“to see the real N. S. Wales, 120 miles from
the railway.” This cheap idea wants throw-
ing out —this idea that “the real N. S.
Wales” is always farther out, and that the
people must borrow another 10 millions and
build another 100 miles of railway to reach
it. The country that stretches along the
railway lines already built is just as much
“real N. S. Wales” as any that could be
found by going out 120 miles farther; and
there is plenty more “real N. S. Wales” that
could be served without going to the sunset
to find it. More than one Government has
listened to this Micawberish yarn about “the
real N. S. Wales” and has extended rail-
ways out in the dry regions where stuff has
to be carted 400 miles to market, when the
same money spent on lines near the coast
would have opened just as much “real N. S.
Wales” and its produce wouldn’t have to be
carried half the distance.

PENFOLDS
WINES
Sydney discovered last week in the person
of dentist Stephens a most astounded
divorce petitioner. Some time ago, with
all the blithe self-confidence of a hom-
ing bird, he fired in on his wife
an order for restitution of conjugal rights.
Had the wife played the game according to
the customary rules, she would have : disre-
garded the order and been respectably divor-
ced without worry, scandal or excessive ex-
pense, within six months or so. However,
she wasn’t that sort of wife. She waited
till the last possible moment for putting in
a legal appearance had arrived, and then
brought counter-charges of cruelty and mis-
conduct. That she was not conversing
through her hat the doomed husband and
his legal advisers appear to have gathered
the moment her official reply came to hand.
As a species of forlorn hope it was decided
to admit that he had gone to Queensland
with the co-re., stayed the night at her
house, and so on; but to, at the same time,
deny that there had been frivolity. This,
doubtless, on the principle that it is futile to
contest what the other side knows and is
evidently prepared to prove. It transpired
presently, however, that the miserably few
details which the opposition had omitted to
ferret out were hardly worth discussing.
The wife’s solicitors and their detectives had
put up an absolute tour de force of investi-
gation. Uanic became plainly observable in
the petitioner’s camp. His corner took on
a worried look. At the very moment when,
under happier auspices, he might fairly have
expected to be drinking with his backers in
the pub across the road, to the health of the
newly-acquired decree nisi —at this precise
instant his alley was flung in. His chief
bottleholder skied the towel. He was urged
to give over and get out of the ring. Un-
willingly, the game fellow obeyed the order
of his advisers, pausing only as he clam-
bered through the ropes, to address these
words to the referee: —
We are innocent, your Honor, but the evidence
looks so black that I’ve decided not to go on with it.
No more astonishing reversal of form has
been seen since that undoubted champion,
Likker, the son of Old Gin, went out at 100
to one on to win the Booligal Cup (carry-
ing 7st; Gib., less apprentice’s allowance)
and was beaten, in hollow fashion, by Wind
Galls, a spavined roarer, aged 17 years, that
had, up to and including the previous Satur-
day, been engaged drawing a local cab.
* * * *
The fourth anniversary of “Mother’s Day”
happened last Sunday. It is a Yankee craze
invented by Miss Anna M. Jervis, or Miss
Priscilla Aurelia Margaret Blinn-Tucker, or
some such person. The idea has caught on
in moderation, and it is proposed to invite
America’s noblest daughter, Miss Martha
Virginia Briggs Hominy, to visit Australia
and lecture on the subject. On Mother’s
Day everybody is expected to wear a white
flower, to do all possible honor to his feminine
parent, and to live as ma would have liked
him to live. The idea has much to recom-
mend it. Nine-tenths of the population can
remember mother as hardworking, unselfish
and long-suffering. She had no regular hours
—in fact, all hours were her hours. Out of
her scanty housekeeping allowance she gener-
ally had a few pounds saved for a day of
trouble. And, above all things, she had a
horror of debt. Mother’s Day ought to be
celebrated by the simple process of swearing
off loans and starting a sinking fund.
* * * *
Last Sunday was “Mother’s Day,” and in
Sydney the occasion gathered in one victim.
A young man in poor health went out and
suicided on his mother’s grave. Possibly in
his weak state the maudlin influences of the
date helped to get him down. There are
people who, if they are talked to at twi-
light in soft, low accents about the old
family cat will burst into tears and shoot
themselves with the furniture there and
then.

The Master Hairdressers’ Association in
Adelaide has been sitting on the Reported
case of a youth who, getting his hair cut at
a city shop, was induced to have a “face
massage,’’ and was charged 15s. for half an
houfs’ work. As it happened, the artist was

A lawyer writes: The
inordinate value at-
tached by the law to
death-bed depositions,
in spite of their being
occasionally shown up
(Bulletin 9/4/T4) as
plain perjury, is an in-
heritance from British
ideas. Judge Stephen,
in his history of the
Criminal Law, calls it
“in many ways remark-
able. It has worked, I
am informed, ill in In-
dia, into which country
it has been introduced,
together with many
other parts of the Eng-
lish law of evidence. I
have heard that in the
Punjab the effect of it
is that a person mort-
ally wounded frequently
makes' a statement
bringing all his heredi-
tary enemies on to the
scene at the time of his
receiving his wound —
thus using his last op-
portunity to do them an
injury. A remark made
on the policy of the rule
by a native of Madras
shows how differently
such matters are viewed
in different parts of the
world. ‘Such evidence,’
he said, ‘ought never to
be admitted in any case.
What motive for telling
the truth can any man
possibly have when he
is at the point of
death’ ?”, Our forefathers,
as a matter of fact,
gambled on the theory
that everybody must
naturally have (1) An
intense dread of death ;
(2) a firm belief in Hell. In these modern
times, one or both of those may easily be
lacking, and then the (unsupported) dying-
declaration becomes positively dangerous.
“John P.”: A weird looking object that
answered to the name of Maurice Vancam
stepped off the Remuera at Wellington
(M.L.) the other day attired in khaki sweater
and pants and bearing round his hat the
ancient legend : “Walking Round the World.”
He was promptly escorted to the ice chest.
His yarn is that he hails from Canada, and
is hoofing it round the planet in pursuit of
the usual, indefinite old wager of £lOOO made
by some alleged individual or animal whose
address isn’t specified, except that he or its
grandfather was a monkey and lived in an
unknown and wholly unreliable tree. Van-
cam says he has walked 18,170 miles to date.
(The last lunatic of this type present scribe
interviewed swore he had walked
miles, and looked as if he might have walked
the last half-mile.) The tale is a very
rambly sort of narrative. It is to the effect
that Vancam started from Vancouver, walked
across Canada, through Uncle Samland,
Central America, Brazil and Chili. From
Chili he took steamer for Queensland. That
looks peculiar ; so does his assertion that he
crossed “The Great Australian Desert.”
When he was questioned as to the exact
locality of the Great Australian Desert he
showed a desire to shove it away up some-
where near the North Pole. Perhaps that’s
where it is.

From Sydney Telegraph last Monday :
The city of Sydney and the harbor are very paltry
affairs. The former is a jumble of pigmy dwellings,
picked out here and there with a narrow laneway,
while the latter is.an aggregation of lagoons linked
together with slender threads of water.
“The Town Crier” :
One, Andrew Cosgrove, of Brunswick (Vic.), lately
took a gun, in a manner of speaking, and projected
a bomb at the local councillors and other interested
parties, and this writer sincerely believes he' is
acting on sound lines and will continue the good
work. In effect, he notified the councillors that under
the Local Government Act they, were entitled to make
an overdraft only up to half the amount of the year s
revenue from rates, and as they had exceeded that
sum this year by £lBl7 13s. he proposed to sue
them collectively and severally for penalties for
breaching the law. He also notified the accommodat-
ing bank that the limit of borrowing had been passed
and if it loaned any more moneys, something would
happen. Reference was lately made in The Bulletin'
to the neighboring Northeote’s tremendous loan liabil-
ity of £BB,OOO. Now it appears that Brunswick,
possessing among its citizens 8000 or 9000 adult
males, has a total, indebtedness of £134,600, and an
overdraft of £18,835 9s. Bd. Thus the blessed
councillors, on a rate revenue of £34,035 13s. 4d.,
have not only borrowed the above-named compara-
tively huge sum, but have also “touched” the ban.c
for the utmost available penny. I don’t know what
the penalty is for this sort of thing, but I know
posterity will have to stand the bulk of it as usual.

On the best of authority we declare that many of
these contests are mere exhibitions of sanguinary
brutality, and we have to thank the police and some
of the promoters that some of them have not ended
in actual slaughter.— Presbyterian parsons on boxing
in Sydney.
There might abin a murder at the boxin’ match
to-night;
A ’omieide or murder as a finish to the fight;
There mighta bin a sorry corpse a-layin’ on the
floor,
Unless one bloke ’ad signified ’e didn’t want no
more:
’ls eyes was black, ’is nose was red, likewise ’is
ear was burst;
’E mighta slung ’is alley—but they slung ’is towel
first.
When Chewey Dargan swung ’is right to Boyo Jack-
son’s ’ead,"
’Ow did we know but Boyo mighta laid there stiff
and dead ?
And wot was there to guarantee, when Boyo played
’is tune,
That Chewey’s funeral wouldn’t come the foll’rin’
afternoon ?
So ’elp me bob, two dismal stiffs they mighta wal-
lered there,
With that tremenjus ’ittin’ —if they ’adn’t ’it the
air.
There’s blokes like Patrick Bradley; and it’s easy for
to guess
That if Bradley ’it a parson, then there’d be one
parson less;
Did ye see them thund’rin’ wallops that the little
bloke let fly?
But Ed McGoorty ducked ’em, so ’e didn’t ’ave to
die:
There mighta bin a Oshkosh kid that laid all cold
an’ stiff
If Eddie ’adn’t blocked the blow; yes, that’s the
question, if.
Suppose a parson, trained on tea, or fed on milk
and buns,
Was standin’ up to Saylor—why, he’d git the count at
once!
And friendly little curates, with a ’eart of lemonade,
If Mehegan crooked ’is finger, they’d be tremblin’
and afraid ;
But blokes that fight are trained to stop a blow
with either ’and ;
And, strike me pink ! them smasliin’ ’its —ow often
do they land?
Bless me, the ’armless football field can ’ave its
’undred dead,
And toffs git strained at tennis, but there’s never
nothing said!
But let a ’ard-worked boxer lose a bit o’ blood an’
skin
(No more than wot a ’ockey-stick might do upon
a shin),
And, strike me red-and-yaller, all the parsons ketch
their breath,
And start to ’owl of murder, and to preach of
sudden death.
I’d like to get inside the ring, to show them parson
lot
'One little tap won’t kill a cove, not while ’is blood
is ’ot;
.I’d like to eddicate these blokes (I don’t know arf
their names),
But nowadays I ain’t much good at any sort of
games:
For, long ago, when I was young, and more than
arf a fool,
I broke an arm and leg while on my way to Sun-
day-school.

THE BRUISED REED.


DISORDERS OF THE MIDDLE-AGED MAN.
After the age of fifty even the most healthy
of men find it necessary to make some changes
in their methods of life. They can no longer
eat and drink whatever fancy dictates, and
they require regular hours of rest and sleep.
Some little weakness begins to develop. It-
may be dyspepsia in one of its multifarious
forms ; gout, or some uric acid disorder ; or,
worst of all, a nervous breakdown. What-
ever form the disorder assumes, it comes so
gradually that it is oftentimes well advanced
before the,busy man realises its significance.
In all modern works on medicine, one fact
is made abundantly clear —that nearly all
disorders of this kind have their origin in the
digestive area. It is too wide a subject to
discuss here, but broadly speaking, we poison
ourselves by absorption from the intestinal

About the last place where one would
look for the Bad Man out of a cowboy film
is the decorous Adelaide suburb, Medindie;
but he was there t’other day all right, horse,
“gun” and everything but the woolly leg-
gings. Having fired casually at a passer-by,
whom he accused of laughing at him, he was
locked up over-night, and in the police-court
he put up the record “thin” excuse that his
horse was a buckjumper, and he carried the
revolver to put it out of pain in case of
accident! The Court smiled tolerantly,
named a small fine, and said it would keep
the weapon. It ought also to have kept
the horse. It isn’t dead certain that it
shouldn’t have kept the man. A person who
goes buckjumping and cowboying in an Adel-
aide suburb is liable to smoke in church, and
do other profound horrors.

Historical Fragment from “10-2-4” :
As an example of the sickening unoriginality of
History, it is worth noting that a row, identical in
many respects with that which caused the present
flare-up in Ulster, raged in Ireland 114 years ago.
The existing trouble arose through General Paget,
who was at the Curragh, getting an order from the
War Office to move troops to Ulster. The order
was qualified by the feeble intimation that he need
not call upon Ulster officers to go. General Paget,
who seems to be a maladroit sort of warrior, sum-
moned his officers together and told the Ulsterites
that “they might quietly disappear.” This was on
a Thursday. He mentioned casually that “by Satur-
day the whole country would be in a blaze.” What
more natural than that General Gough and the others
should take his words to mean that civil war was
imminent ? The vast ma.joritv of British officers are
Unionists—Unionism being the fashionable political
doctrine, and officers being men of fashion first and
political thinkers a long wav afterwards, as a
general thing. Many of them resigned their com-
missions; signed anti-Home Rule petitions; and did
various other unsoldierly acts. (They had precedent
to go on, by the way. At the time of the War of
Independence all those officers who were Whigs—or,
as we would say nowadays, Liberals —resigned their
commissions rather than fight against the Colonials.
Chatham’s own son led the way, acting under his
father’s orders.) Then came explanations from
Asquith, Colonel Seely (speaking for the War Office),
General Paget a; b others —none of which have any-
thing to do % ; <ii i'-g' q”estion under discussion.
And new for i e 114-year-old analogy. The
Union v s i nmin t. The Articles had been
agreed to i:i Loth the Lords and the Commons. The
alleged Irish Parliament—mostly English nominees—
had_ been prorogued never to meet, again. Public
feeling was as embittered on the Irish question as it
is to-day. At this stage an express arrived in Ire-
land from the Marquis of Downshire, urging the offi-
cers of ike Downshire Regiment to oppose the Union
by every means in their power. In other words, he
asked them to disregard the War Office on a .point
of political principle; just as prominent Unionist
noblemen have been urging Unionist officers to do of
late. _By a misunderstanding very similar to that
by which Generals Paget and Gough were beguiled,
the contents of the letter became public property.
Also, an anti-Union petition, which had been sent
for signature by those officers of the Downshire Regi-
ment alone who were freeholders of the County of
Down, was exhibited coram populo, and was signed
by practically everybody that saw it. According
to a correspondent of Lord Castlereagh ( vide “Mem-
oirs of Viscount Castlereagh”) “all signed that were
present, but one —-freeholders, non-freeholders, chil-
dren, etc., etc., and as for the men, drum-boys, fifers,
etc., all signed, or were signed for, as your Lordship
wiU see by comparing it with the muster-roll.”
(This performance bears a humorous resemblance, by
the way, to the sort of thing that occurs in the case
of all petitions.) The Government of the day acted
with far more stronuousness than the Asquith Gov-
ernment did. Lord Downshire was removed from
the command of his regiment, from the Governor-
ship of the County, from the office of Registrar 'in
the Court of Chancery, and his name was erased from
the list of the Privy Council. Also, the officers
associated with him were punished in various ways.
In the end, as we know, after illimitable talk of
bloodshed, the Act of Union was consummated fairly
peacefully; and so, in all likelihood, will it be pres-
ently when the Act of Federation has to be accom-
plished.

In Sydney, a few days ago, the usual old
band of masked criminals walked into a
suburban house where an evening party was
raging, and effectively cleaned up the 20 and
odd men who were present. But for solid nerve
their effort did not approach that of a couple
of malefactors who operated in the city, a
night or so later. According to a scandalised
daily paper reporter :- \
They stood under the glare of an electric light, and
remained for quite ten minutes trying to open the
front door of Mr. James Gourlay’s tailoring estab-
lishment, 424 Pitt Street, city. The door is
only a few feet back from the footpath, and the
affair occurred at 8 o’clock at night, at a time
when people were passing in dozens.
This journal would be in no wise surprised
to learn that a cute firm of robbers had put
one of the local cathedrals up to auction,
and knocked it down, for cash, to the highest
bidder. And if the buyer turned out
to be a policeman there would be no occasion
for excessive amazement.
* * * *
The Sydney Scotchbyterians assembled
last week and “the religion and public morals
committee” produced a report which started
off with the old lament that plate-money
was as scarce as ever. But it wasn’t put
as plainly as that. According to the re-
ligious reporter of the evening paper, “re-
gret was expressed that there was no observ-
able diminution in the volume of the human
stream that set towards recreational resorts
and away from the places of worship.” Which
was a characteristically windy and parsoni-
cal way of putting it. But the draughty,
sunless, dusty, germ-haunted church or cathe-
dral never makes any serious effort to com-
pete with the healthy, sunlit beach and the
harbor-side picnic ground. The holy people
could hardly put the choir ladies into pink
tights and mix ragtime with the hymns,
while the curate ran a shooting-gallery in
the chancel and the verger looked after
swing-boats and a soft-drink fountain in the
vestry; but at least the place of worship
should be made sanitary and structurally
safe. Last week the regular customers of
All Saints’ Cathedral, Bathurst, were in-
formed that, during 1913, a beam which was
the chief support of the building (of which
the walls were cracked and the foundations
defective) was held in position by only three
nails! Who Would take refuge from a par-
ticularly hospitable Devil in such a place?
The complicated and long-winded Catts
matrimonial case, tried in the N.S.W. courts,
ended in the delivery of an order that Eva
Alice Catts should return to the roof of
James Howard Catts, Federal politician, and
there live happily ever after. Of course,
such an order doesn’t count for very much.
It is never really enforced. When the Smiths
are commanded to reunite the police don’t tie
Mrs. Smith on a wheelbarrow, and wheel her
to the house of Smith, and hand her over
amid cheers. She is simply bidden to go
home, and, if she doesn’t go, Smith can get
a very sudden divorce. In fact, the order for
the restitution of conjugal rights is a good
heap of a failure. It would be much more
successful if the Judge could decide
what the parties are to say when they meet,
and how they are to say it. The rule
ought to be laid down that Mrs. Smith, re-
turning by the order of the Court after the
separation, should arrive about dusk on a
beautiful evening, wearing a shy, happy
smile, a tinge of color in her cheeks and
very becoming clothes. The room should
be spotless, tidy and brightly lighted, and
the table—with chrysanthemums* in the
middle of it —should be nicely laid' for tea.
The infant, if any, should be good and have
on its best pinny. The cat should occupy
a contented and homelike attitude on the
couch, even if a 201 b. weight had to be tied
to it to prevent it bolting. Mrs. Smith
should come in timidly, and Smith, instead
of recriminating, should get up and hold
out his arms as in the honeymoon days, and
say that this reunion made up for every-
thing. If the Court could fix up a restitu-
tion of conjugal rights on that basis it
would do good work. But if the wife plods
home through the rain, in a shoddy hat and
a defiant mood, and finds a dim light, and
nothing on the table but dirty dishes and
the remains of a fish, and the gentleman
says briefly, “Is that you again, you brute?”
and the lady says briefly, “I see you’re
still drinking”—then nothing very good can
come of the Court’s decision. Some wealthy
philanthropist who is on his death-bed might
do worse than leave a big sum to found a
special Order of Sisters of Mercy to deal
with cases of this sort. The Sisters would
look up the parties when the order was
made, and arrange the hour of reconcilia-
tion. Certain of them would clean and
scour and tidy up the house till it looked
like a good place to go back to. The
eldest Sister would speak like a mother to
Smith and pei'suade him to wear his best
clothes and his cleanest shave for the occa-
sion. Two young Sisters would dress Mrs.
Smith, and do her hair beautifully, and
bring her along. A really picturesque
Sister would stay for an hour, if the parties
wished, and serve the tea, in order to bridge
over the first awkwardness of the new honey-
moon. Then it is several chances to one
that the restitution of conjugal rights would
be a success. But when the Court, so to
speak, simply opens the door and shoves the
lady in, and says “Here she is,” the business
is sordid, and comes to no good.
“ Jeames,” reminiscently :
According- to a cable of last week:—
“The<.directors of the Astor Night Club in Leices-
ter Square have been fined £7OO for allowing the
sale of, liquor after hours to people who were ille-
gally introduced to the premises.”
.. Right poignantly does it bring back to memory
bursts, of long ago. A. William-Cann, Martin Luther
and I had just embarked on the sea of London life
as gentlemen’s gents. We were three of the smartest
young , valets in the metropolis. Dressed in our
masters’ clothes we cut impressive figures at the
Alsatians; the Percy Supper Club, off the Totten-
ham C&urt Road ; the Pretoria and other resorts of
that class. "Clearly across the bridge of years can
1 see,Martin posing to an edified fille de foie in the
Percy,7as a millionaire silver miner from the Upper
Amazon, while William-Cann and I "figured as
' guardsmen or something similar. Quite a lot of
> thjs" sort of thing is done at night-clubs,
v The Alsatians was perhaps the best of the lot —
though all night-clubs are really the same night-
club, when everything is said and done. It was
in Oxford-street, and was run in those days by an
aged,, gentleman of U.S. origin, who called himself
“the Colonel.” I believe he was really an honorary
admiral from Bolivia. I had the good fortune to be in
the ballroom of the club when he was shot in the
leg by a fellow-Yankee whom he had annoyed in
some way. Game as a pebble the old warrior drew
a large revolver and just missed blowing an adjacent
waiter’s brains out. He also put a bullet into
a looking-glass and another into the piano. At this
stage I left, briskly, for the Guards’ Club —that is
to say, for my master’s domicile.
To become a member of the Alsatians one needed;
to be proposed by the porter and seconded by a,
waiter, and to ante up the annual subscription fee-
on the spot. One then gave some such name as,-
William Makepeace Thackeray or Lord Byron, andl
was forthwith admitted to the freedom of the entire-
bagnio.
A night-club is the best place in the world to learn*
to dance. The ladies who frequent such spots will!
dance for ever with the veriest beginners, in return
for drinks and supper. The floor is always good
and the band likewise, till the early hours of the
morning, when the performers become blithered or
stunned. It is also the most amusing club in the
world from the point of view of very young men
about town, and lads from one or other of the Uni-
versities. Wherever else youths of this type may
feel lonely, they are perfectly sure of encountering
kindred spirits, male and female, at a night-club.
Leicester Square is an ideal place for, a night-club.
In fact, there was one somewhere between Cavour’s
and the Alhambra in my own hot youth. Patrons
just reeled down the steps of the Empire and across
the road; and there they were till daybreak, if so
disposed. An inadequately squared body of police
raided that particular establishment in the first
flush of its lusty youth, and it fell dead for ever.
I expect that the Astor made a similar mistake in
regard to the police. The only way to achieve, in
a night-club, that peace which passes the under-
standing of social purity enthusiasts, is to thoroughly
fix the police.
The night-club movement hasn’t got properly
going in Sydney yet, but there are indications that
it is coming. When it does, may the names of Oliver
Cromwell Jeames and Martin Luther Heep be the
first on the roll of membership. If we fail, between
us, to show the locals a point or two in the way of
uproar, then are we unworthy to be called English-
men.
“Women don’t mind being laughed at,”
says Miss Adela Pankhurst in Melbourne.
Miss Pankhurst may know a great deal about
suffragettes ; she knows little about women.
The average woman would rather be slapped
in the face than sniggered at. Her eternal
vigilance with regard to the niceties of dress
springs largely from fear of the guns of the
other slie-cat—the Cheshire cat. Hence the
never-failing query, “Is my hat on straight?”
* * * *
Miss Adela Pankhurst, talking off the plat- -
form, charges the London police with making ;
a bit out of the Suffragette Cause by annex- .
ing all the valuables they have picked up in,
their raids. When the police broke into the*
W.P.U. rooms some 12 months ago they car-
ried off a mass of papers, including
containing postal notes, etc. In fact they,-
captured funds amounting to £3OO, the Panki-
burst lady alleges, and though they returned]
all the documents in due course, they kepift
every bean of the boodle. All applicatio«s
for restitution of the stolen property are a
mere waste of time, according to the Suf-
fragette statement. But is a Suffragette state-
ment, in its probably reckless untruthfulness,
worth any more than a police statement, in
its probably audacious mendacity? And con-
sidering that the Woman Suffrage Cause has
the sympathy of every intelligent man, as
Ellen Terry claims for it, is it likely that an
audacious theft of W.P.U. cash by a body
of official marauders could be permitted?
The male champions of the Cause in Parlia-
ment, and out of it, must be a poor lot if
they couldn’t raise a hullabaloo over such an
outrage against the sacred rights of proputty.

The cable mentioned casually the other
day that Nona Thomspn, a Maoriland artist,
had had some canvas and paint accepted for
exhibition at the New Salon in Paris. Miss
Thomson hails from Invercargill, the little
Prohibition town away at the Ice and Mud
Oyster end of the Borrowing Isles. EUr
people are of considerable tonnage, having
taken up big lumps of the sub-Antartic por-
tion of M.L. in the very early days. The re-
sult is that they own about half the business
part of the “dry” town.
The Oldest and Most Venerable Inhabi-
tant :
An old Sydneyite recently crossed the Divide
at Hartlands, Cranford, Essex (Eng.)—Bessie, relict
of Sigmond Hoffnung, at the age of 74 years’. Sig-
mond Hoffnung commenced business in Sydney in
1853, in one of two shops which stood in George-
street, between the Bank of New South Wales and
the Commercial Bank. Later on the banks annexed
the shops—one each —and extended. Mrs. Hoff-
nung was a Miss Marks, her brothers being partners
in the Hoffnung business. She left Svdney about
30 years ago.
“0.T.” writes: —
On a recent Saturday about 4000 people gathered
round the grave of John Batman (who shares, with
Jonn Pascoe Fawkner, the honor of having founded the
city of Melbourne) in the old cemetery to pay a
tribute to the memory of this Australian-born ex-
plorer and adventurer. The ceremony, which was
arranged by the Early Pioneers’ Association of Aus-
tralia, was the first of its kind, though John has
been dead 75 years. He was a Parramatta native,
born in 1800, some say the son of a missionary
who mished at Tahiti; but no such name appears in
a list of missionaries. The father was a soldier,
Bateman, by name. John went to Van Diemen’s
Land in 1820 “over a girl” arid farmed there.
He was, too, the agent of a land syndicate which
sought to annex Port Phillip.
A bogus Lord Stauton Plope, masquerading
as a member of the Hopetoun (Linlithgow)
family, has been having a good time in Eng-
land at the expense of confiding tradesmen
and the suburban snobocracy. The Hope
clan is very numerous, and the collapse of
the bogus lordling has revived the old story
of the parson who was preaching to a crowd
of Hopetoun relatives in the wayback times
when noble clansmen were expected to gather
once a year at the chieftain’s ancestral seat.
They were sufficiently numerous to fill the
jocal kirk on the Sawbath. The new meen-
ister, by way of offering an apologetic sug-
gestion that disappointments come to even
the best families, said, “Alas, my friends!
this world is full of blasted Hopes.”
The early fathers of the Eight Hours move-
ment in Melbourne have just heard of an
old-time carpenter (aged 94) who claims to
have marched in the first Eight Hours’ pro-
cession. Joe Webster, the ancient aforesaid,
abandoned his. trade and left town to take up
land at Wahgunyah by the Murray River.
Whilst his mates were passing out in Mel-
bourne- he flourished on the land and grew
vines, so the “Early Pioneers’ Association”
knew him not. Next vear (d.v.) the execu-
tive will get him for an exhibit in the proces-
sion, though a healthy nonogenarian advt.
for the simple life at Wahgunyah district
seems hardly fitted for the part of grand old
man in a city festival.
“Vic” writes:—
This State wakes up over its obligation to Mitchell,
the explorer. There are several obelisks on what is
called the Major’s line. One is just dedicated at
Expedition Pass, near Castlemaine. Pyramid Hill
took the lead with a memorial on the peak, so
named by Mitchell. There is another cairn on Mount
Arapiles. At Portland they are laying the founda-
tions of an obelisk where Mitchell met the Hentys.
Similar movements are raging at Swan Hill, Hor-
sham, Hamilton, Nagambie, Dookie and Wangaratta.
Is not this getting a trifle ridiculous? ITovell and
Hume, and the rest, would need to be similarly scat-
tered about the country. Is there to be “Forrest” on
every milestone of the Westralian railway like those
annoying advertisements of tea all along the
lines to Melbourne and Sydney (“One Hun-
dred Miles to Swigger’s,” “Ninety-nine and
three-quarter Miles to Swigger’s”) ? School-
inspector Long started the Mitchell furore in
Victoria, and it is getting much to furious. Mitchell
went through Expedition Pass on the return journey.
His bullock waggon broke down, which led him to
the discovery of Macedon from Mount Alexander.
He was of classical bias, thinking of Alexander the
Great and Philip of Macedon.
“B.B.”: I loathe to apply the acid of mere
fact to glowing romance, but “B.’s” remarks
(30/4/’l4) about Pat Campbell, his wife and
her new husband, are over the odds. F’rin-
stance: —
The late Pat Campbell was a dashing, daredevil
officer, the kind of man who didn’t care a damn
Handsome, fascinating, and a real good sort, he was
out for death or glory. His remains were interred
m the 0.R.C., near Kimberley.
I knew Campbell well in S’Africa. He was
not an officer, dashing, daredevil or other-
wise. As a master of fact he was a humble
Yeomanry sergeant. He didn’t dash worth
tuppence. He was a bitterly disap-
pointed man, and it was notorious
that, like many another, he had selected
the war as a golden opportunity to
commit suicide creditably. He wasn’t buried in
Kimberley, but at a small cemetery in a dorp,
by name Boshoff, in the Orange River State,
When I last saw his grave, which was some
time after his death, it was surmounted by a
superb wooden cross, made from a biscuit
box. “B.” might do worse than tell Mrs.
Cornwallis West 11. about that S’African
grave.

Grief G for a Sam Gillott memorial, and a

Meyer undertook the financing of Webb Gil-

will be his first experience of freedom from
the inartistic cares of a cook’s life at a caf<§.
up to almost the time of his departure, the
sculptor stuck to his position as chef at
larer Brothers’ feedery, in Bourke-street.
lime was when he despaired of being able to

The professional purveyor of the super-
natural brand of Terewth is notoriously in-
accurate when it comes to the affairs of this
world ; and American Sunday journalism is
a frantic business. But the wildest sort of
journalism would hardly dare to brand a re-
ligious tradesman as a liar by publishing,
without authority of any kind, a detailed
story such as appears in the New York
World (Sunday, 28/12/’l3). It is an inter-
view with Ballington Booth (who used to
boss the Salvation Army in Australia), and
lie tells (or is made to tell) how he escaped
by the skin of his immortal soul from a wild
bear of the grizzly species in the Australian
forest. It wasn’t a chance circus creature,
but one of the thousands of man-eaters that
artist Norman Lindsay has unaccountably
missed when out after bears. Some slabs
from the romance :
One of the party, who was a crack shot and a
huntsman of some ability, had taken his rifle wi h
him, intending to protect us from wild beasts. We
travelled through the heart of the scrub In thoie
days there were plenty of bears in the scrub. They
kept near to the gum trees, and wherever there wi s
a grove of gum trees there were sure to be plenty
of bears around. The Australian bears in those dais
were a factor to the man who went out into the
bush. They fed around the gum trees and pursued
the policy of letting men alone as long as they were
let alone. Sometimes the female bears would plav
tricks on the traveller. They would be playful and
act almost like tame bears until they came close to
a man, when they would suddenly become aggressive
and attack him. A man had to be agile to get away
from such an attack and had to be quick with his
gun to keep from being killed.
Then a female bear, overcome, no doubt, by
the beautiful Salvation uniform, tried to
mash Ballington, was shot in the collarbone
by the ass with the gun, and became
annoyed. “With foam on its mouth, its fur
bristling with anger, it rushed at us.” How-
ever, Ballington had an inspiration, and got
behind a tree. The bear shot past, and,
when it was ready for another charge, the
mule or perjurer with the gun shot it. And
probably the only thing the purely legendary
beast wanted to do was to pull Ballington’s
Blood-and-Fire leg. This is a good sample
of what the inflamed religious imagination
can do with such unpromising material as
the playful little vegetarian koala, who sits
un aloft eating gum-leaves and posing for
Norman Lindsay.
Apparently without intending to reflect on
his law, the S. M. Herald describes H. M.
Hamilton, the newest addition to the N, S.
Wales District Court bench, as “a capable
judge of pure-bred poultry and dogs.” Still,
even among livestock, Hamilton has his
limitations. Though a confirmed dabbler in
shares, he is a poor judge of wild-cats.
Gone aloft at last, from Walkerville, an
Adelaide suburb, George Dove, vicar of that
parish under every bishop S. Australia has
had. He was a fighting High Churchman
professionally, and the gentlest, most ami-
able of rose-growers at all other times. As
an outback, archdeacon and bush missionary
in the ’7o’s lie used to do thousand-mile
journeys, riding one horse and leading
another. But it was with Walkerville, where
he lived close on 50 years in one house,
that he was chiefly connected. He was 81
when the end came.
“Professor” W. T. Mills, formerly of Mil-
waukee (U.S.), has decided to leave M.L.,
where he has spent a stormy two years as
a Labor agitator, and he returns to the
Hand of the Free (at odd times). The career
of this imported hustler has been varied.
When he first arrived in Maoriland he un-
loosed all his long-range talking apparatus
and was accepted as a Heaven-sent organ-
iser. He was appointed editor of the speci-
ally-purchased Labor page of the Welling-
ton Times, at a salary of £6OO per annum.
Ihe main plank of the United Labor Party
(Mills’s employer) was support of the Arbi-
tration Act. Up against this policy were
“Red-Feds.” These latter invited the
U.L.P. to link forces. When Mills saw
the big showing the anti-Arbitrationists were
putting up he climbed into their ’bus and
headed straight for strikes and glory. Then
the big waterside strike burst on the coun-
try* Some of the “Feds” talked about mak-
ing the streets “run with blood.” The Pro-
fessor from Milwaukee had never poured out
blood in his life, and the way some of those
people shot Paul Kruger with their mouths
anguished him. His position as a peaceful
bloodshedder became untenable, and he is
taking the long track for home.
ARTHUR STANLEY, Victoria's new State
Governor.
MY RAGTIME FUNERAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I saw a funeral to-day,
A trailing line of woe,
That passed along the sunlit way
With black plumes drooping low,
And tear-stained folk that crept behind
The cold thing ’neath the pall—
-5 could not ’rase it from my mind
That doleful funeral.
Now, for myself, when I must start
That journey to the place
Where one is emptied from the cart,
And no mere may retrace
Ilis steps along the sunny-side,
Nor ever may recall
The joyousness of youth’s warm tide—
Here is my funeral:
I want the boys to come for me
Upon a sky-blue morn,
Wearing the same solemnity
That they have always worn
Since first we met and friendship grew
To be our all in all —
I want the gang to see me through
A ragtime funeral.
Say, roll me down to Jordan’s shore
Behind a two-step band,
All care-free as we rolled before
Old Gabriel took a hand.
We 11 pull up at the pubs we know.
And give the girls a call.
We’ll make this proposition go—
This ragtime funeral.
I see us swinging through the gates,
The Gates of Fare-you-well,
A lot of happy reprobates
Who sound the passing knell
With lobsters’ hoofs on dead marines
While I slide to my stall—
Yes, sir, I’m saving up some beans
For that ragtime funeral.
N.S.W.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
A whiff from the gallant old Melbourne
boom-time period drifted into this oitice last week
in the shape of a prospectus of “Laluma Plantations
Proprietary Ltd.” The idea is to acquire land in
the Solomons, and work it as co'coanut plantations,
a lie germ of the scheme, whose birthplace is the
offices of Snowball and Kaufmann, Melbourne, is one
Mr. Cuthbert Kaufmann, who “has secured offers of
two blocks of suitable land—ore of abo. t COO acres
and another of from 500 to 1000 acres ’ (a wide
margin here). He has also “lodged an application for
a third block of 500 acres, on which are a large
number of native cocoanut trees in full bearing ”
(Incidentally, he is willing to be manager fer live
years presumably at an adequate salary.) These
assets Kaufmann is prepared to assign to the com-
pany, on terms The terms are that out of 30,000
£1 shares (12,000 preference and 18,000 ordinary)
he shall be handed 12,000 ordinaries fully paid up to
20s. (He will also lave a steady job for live years.)
Ihe first question that pushes itself mdel.y to the
' lc die properties which Mr. Kaufmann is
offering freehold or leasehold? Taking into consid-
eration the fact that the prospectus preserves a
murky silence on this essential point, one may sus-
pect, more or less, that the odds are in favor of the
blocks with which the investing public is to be dow-
ered, being leaseholds, and unimproved leaseholds at
that. 1 lie estimated expenditure provides that in
the first year .1150 shall be outlaid on “land, etc.,”
■and various other suits on the wages of 50 boys, the
purchase of their food, tools and a recruiting boat—
'total, £2400. For the ensuing six years the annual
‘disbursement is expected never to get far past the
£lOOO mark. And in the eighth year the trees will
tie in full bearing and splendid affluence within the
igrip of all concerned. The theory is seemingly that,
in eight years’ time, the whole at present largely un-
improved estate will be a solid mass of full-grown
trees. And all by means of the labor of 50, at
present unobtained, niggers. Also, incidentally, no
provision has been made on the estimates for the
expenses of paying white men to supervise the said
niggers, or for the cost of paying other niggers to
build quarters for the said white men It is about as
visionary a dream of financial splendor as this paper
“? s . to date. The one redeeming feature
of it is the spacious, Elizabethan nerve of the vendor
In consideration of nearly half the capital of the
co., he is prepared (it is time to get back to the
mam body of the proposition) to hand over: (1) An
offer of 600 acres (nothing said about title, about
the names or status of the persons offering, about the
price of the land, or about the amount of work done
on it) ; (2) an offer of from 500 to 1000 acres
(again nothing said about title, about the names or
status of the persons who make the offer, about the
Price, or about the amount of improvement done oil
the land, and in this case even the area seems un-
certain) ; and (3) an application for 500 acres made
to parties unspecified, on terms unspecified, and with
the same old obscurity re title. In his younger and
more stormy days this writer often applied for a
lob, .but he never once thought of floating his job-
application into a company.

Optimism brought a good many Australian banks into
the gutter 20-odd years ago, but a more broken-
down brand of the same spirit afterwards stood well
to the National of Australasia. When its position
was only moderately good it decided to put on a bold
front and started to pay dividends in excess of what
the visible profits seemed to justify. The public
took it at its own valuation and supplied it with
far more funds than most likely would have come
in had its dividend been smaller—a very foolish
thing, no doubt, for the public to do, but as past
experience has shown, the public very often is
foolish when it has deposits to make, and considers
not the size of the profits which are being laid by,
but only of those that are being paid out. Had
there been a sudden change from national prosperity
back to national drought and drudgery, this bank
might have had a hard row to hoe. As things
turned out, it has had years of affluence and high
prices to help it, and now everything in the garden
is blooming. This table shows how the face of the
balance-sheet has changed :

A striking feature is the big bulk of the ’free
deposit money. At a time when monev has been
earning big interest the National has added £548 211
to its previous total of deposits, for which it navs
nothing at all, bringing up the total to £4,622,885
It looks as though a good many people were waiting
f f°™ d R y to day, hoping for more stable industrial
.conditions to turn up. In cash and liquid assets
■this bank holds now 38.8 p.c.' of all its liahiliHp,
to the public. The figure is more satisfactory S
It once was; still it is no more than it ought Vbe
considering the very large size of the free deposits
which are liable to be called for at anj' moment.
Even after it had professedly cleaned up the mess
that was left on the premises when the old boom
exploded, the National was understood to have a
pretty large holding of over-valued assets. By this
time there is probably little or none of the ancient
wreckage left. As late as 1904 there was an
item “Ileal estate other than premises, £277,921.”
All that is left of this little lot is £37,444. And
the chances are that there has been a bit of profit
on the sales. The Colonial under similar circum-
stances disclosed some accumulated surpluses this
year. Whether the National has also a hidden
stocking in the manager s room remains to be seen.
The late J. G. Addison, who was general manager:—
or chief manager, as this bank always puts it—-
was a cautions Scot with an affection for keeping a
bit in his hinder tail pocket. Judging by the
rate of profit calculated on capital and reserves com-
bined, tlierc is nothing much wrong with the assets
nowadays. This shows recent results: —

about the class of security on which it lends money.
But the chief officers of this bank have for some,
years past been rather prominent in casting doubts
upon the permanency of recent high values, and the
fairly clean sweep they have made of old properties
in their hands show us that in one line at least they
backed their own expressed opinions.

It has been a hot and dry summer in Sydney, and
tens of thousands of people never before had so much
money to wet themselves with. Yet even so, it is
very doubtful whether Tooth and Co. could have
shown such a large increase in profits without shak-
ing a few cards out of their sleeve. No doubt
they had put some of them there in view of the local-
option poll which was taken late lust year. There
was no telling how that vote would go: had some-
thing happened a few days before to turn the electors
temporarily wowser, Tooth and Co. might have lost a
few dozen hotel licenses, and with them perhaps
two-thirds of the value put upon the premises would
have gone smash. Nothing at all serious happened,
however, and the co. breathes freely again for three
years. No doubt, as hinted just now, it is some of
the provision made out of past profits against a
contingency which did not arise that has dropped
into the past half-year’s booming figures.
And having had a bit of luck, the shareholders
naturally expect something, and the directors have
almost acquired the habit of obliging' them;
£137,500 is to be capitalised, which means one new
share for every eight of the old ordinaries. This will
make the total of accumulated profits- transferred to
capital within four years £887,500. This total is
inclusive of £550,000 of 6 p.c. preference capital.
When this fine plum was handed to the shareholders
at the end of 1912 most of them seemed to think
that the preferential 6 p.c. was all that could be
reasonably expected from this issue. Bi t for the
second term the div. sprang to 8 p.c., ai.d for the
past half-year, the third, to 10 p.c. Between them
the two dividends for the half-year absorb £82,500,
and looking back it will be noticed that prior to
1910 the net profits never amounted to anything like
that figure. For some half-years, in fact, they did
not amount to one-half the total required by the
present dividend of 10 p.c. all round. And it has
to be remembered that though the capital has grown
from £900,000, at which it stood for many years,
to £1,650.000, no money whatever has been paid in
during that time by the shareholders. Certainly it
it quite probable that the net profits a few years
ago were diminished by very large appropriations
towards internal reserves. But even so, and taking
the much larger figures of the past few terms as a
basis, it seems possible that under the adverse trade
conditions which must inevitably arrive in New South
Wales sooner or later, the directors may have all
their work cut out to pay 10 p.c. all round on the
new capital of £1,787,500.
Of course, the co.’s financial position is wonder-
fully good. See how it has improved even in the
past 10 years:—
Mn 1 .. ’O4. Mar.. ’l4.
Liabilities £28,129 £51,403

Thus besides making all the very large additions to
plants and properties, including purchases of other
breweries, the co. has added about £225,000 net to
its liquid assets. Breweries, properties, etc., it will
be seen, have been more than doubled, but more or
less of the accretion is due to the revaluation of
assets in 1912. Whether those assets now stand at
their true value or not it is impossible to sav. On
that point all that is plain is that there is no infla-
tion on account of goodwill, which has disappeared
entirely from the balance-sheet, although the good-
will is worth more than ever it was, seeing that three
breweries have now practically the whole beer trade
of the city of Sydney in their hands.

This society’s capital is divided amongst paid-up
and subscribing shares, but out of the total of
,£<0,900, £72,857 is of the paid up or fixed variety.
In addition to this money and the reserve funds, the
co. has the use of a fair amount of deposits. The
total of the latter on February 28 was £104,741, a
small increase notwithstanding that the money market
was pretty hard during the early part of the year.
The deposits in proportion to share capital are not
excessive, and given anything like sane management
the security should be good enough for the most
timid. The reserves are represented by £17,884 of
Treasury bills, fixed deposits, and such like securities,
along with some till money. Apart from there assets
and £7OOO in freehold premises, everything is lent
out, all of it apparently on building-society terms
The repayments seem to be fairly quick ; at any rate,
though it is mentioned that £44,680 was advanced
to borrowers during the year, the total outstanding
increased by only £594. This is a good feature. It
is when repayments are slow that a slump in values
is most likely to leave a building society stranded
high and dry, like a ship outside the Federal port at
Jervis Bay. The dividend, it will be seen, has been
put up to 6 p.c. notwithstanding which a larger
addition has teen made to reserves than for 14 years
past. Not that profits have sprung along very fast,
but because the society seems at last to have got rid
of its old load of over-valued securities, and now
reckons that it has no mere writing down to do.
That trouble is probably to come ; it isn’t when
values are high that you see bad debts in a business
like this, but it is the .time when you mostly make
them. For that reason an addition to reserves
amounting to only one-third of the dividend is none
top large-, especially considering that the reserves
amount to only about one-fifteenth of the money out
at risk. In a city liable to violent fluctuations the
brake should now be put hard down on dividends
until the profits swell much beyond their present
size.

This company,, which is the successor to a financial
undertakery, is so much in love witli the business
which it dn oped into in 1903 that during the past
year it issued a few more shares, bringing the capital
up to £300,000, and it now has under consideration
a proposal to increase this to £500,000, besides
issuing further debenture stock. To begin with, in
the rather depressed times of 1903 1,366,666 shares
of 4s. each and £392,485 worth of 4 pc. debenture
stock were issued to the shareholders and creditors
of the old busted banks in exchange for their in-
terests. Subsequently, in 1909, the Mercantile Bank
winding-up department was absorbed, and in order
to complete the transaction it became necessary to
levalue all the assets so as to make a fair division of
shares between the two. In all, £173,987 was added
to the book value of the Melbourne Trust’s assets, in-
cluding £28,782 book profit on the purchase of the
Mercantile Bank assets. Previously, £148.709, the
accumulated profits on the sale of assets, had been
added to the reserves. Combining this actual profit
and the profit expected to be realised on account
° f . the higher valuation, this co. felt justified in dis-
tributine: £246,559 in cash and 4 p.c. debentures,
equal altogether to 3s. 4d. per 4s. share. The Mer-
cantile Bank transaction involved also the increase
of shares to £1,479,355. Very soon after 1903 —
probably even before that date—a strong financial
crowd in Melbourne, scenting a highly probable in-
crease in values, had seen large possibilities in this
company and heavy purchases of shares were made
from John Bull and Alexander Mcßull. While the
London management was nominallv retained, a Mel-
bourne Board (consisting now of “Big Bill” Ballieu,
Riggall and J. H. Riley) has acquired more extensive
powers. It is not “Big Bill” Ballieu’s way to hide
his co.’s light under a bushel, and no doubt this
has something: to do with the issue of shares and
debentures to represent not only actually realised
but also expected profits. It has certainly been a
very sweet thing for those who got on to it in
1903 and thereabouts.
As properties have been sold, debentures, mostly
held on the other side, have been repurchased usually
at a profit to the company. The next table shows
both lots of transactions:—-

A third profit has of course arisen from rents and
the working of properties, which include a sheep
station and a sugar estate in Fiji. The co. cannot,
however, eat its cake and have it. Hence, as pro-
perties have been sold, rents, etc., have shrunk. Cer-
tainly the co. has had the proceeds, but, as shown
above, most of the money has gone to pay off cheap
debentures, or has been put out on deposit and in
other gilt-edged securities. At the end of the year
there was still £326,326 represented by “properties
and investments” as valued in 1910, £124,761 of
Purchasers’ balances and £60,176 in cash and short
loans. On the other side, liabilities of all kinds
amounted to £100,246. Assuming that the valua-
tion of 1910 represents present values, the net assets,
after paying the current dividend, are worth Is. 3d
per share over par (4s ). On the past history of the
co. shares should, of- course, be worth a great deal
more, but the shareholders have eaten the pie they
had the luck to get in 1903. Some dav, perhaps,
they may have assets thrown at them at a similarly
low price. But there will have to be trouble in Vic-
toria first. And this co. is just as liable to be hit
by that trouble as any other co.

This co., now just upon 40 years old, has its head
office in London and its general manager’s quarters in
Dunedin. Agencies are scattered over the country
from Auckland to the Bluff, but the bulk of the busi-
ness is from Dunedin southwards to Invercargill.
It is moist country where they grow great crops
of oats and fatten sheep for the freezers, which have
broken out like freckles all over the land. The eo.’s
business is to act as agent in pastoral transactions
and to finance the deals. The paid share capital
is only £ 200,000. The balance of the money re-
quired and not supplied by the reserves is raised
chiefly on debentures, and the practice is to fix them
on a five years’ currency. In addition to the paid capital
and reserves, the creditors, who have claims alto-
gether for £911,871, have the protection of an un-
called capital ot £803,000, and the shares are
understood to be in the hands mostly of people of
tremendous financial tonnage. An easy position is
always maintained. Thus at the end of the last
year a total of over £278,000 was invested in Gov-
ernment and other securities or held in cash. The
balance of the resources is chiefly out on mortgage
or employed in financing clients on current accounts.

Besides the miserable reserve shown in the table,
there is a “Repairs, suspense, public risk and con-
tingency account” of £4900. The South Coast boats
never carry a very large number of passengers, but
it is a particularly small wreck that calls for only
£4900, the ship and all the produce and pigs
are reckoned in. Moreover, the co.'s other reserves
amount to onlv £l7O. Yet even when the co. does
earn a little above the average, as it did this year,
instead of dropping the extra bit into reserves, it
hands out a bonus. Since 1906, when that table
began to take an interest in things, the co. has, on
nine occasions only, paid a dividend that would allow
of an addition to reserves; and of those nine addi-
tions four were £3, £ll, £lB and £22. The
remaining times it paid dividends which necessi-
tated a bite being taken out of reserves—on one
occasion, in 1911. when the co. made a loss of £3993,
it paid the usual dividend, with the result that the
reserves dropped from £6781 to £163, and they
have been more or less asleep ever since.
The co. still gives its profit “after providing for
depreciation, etc.” ; and it is there that the one ray
of hope lies. Take this comparison:—
March, March, Sept., March,
1905. 1913. 1913. 1914.
Liabilities .. £1,165 £5,506 £5,582 £7,098
Ship property 40,393 70,375 72,191 75,044
Liquid assets 45,863 10,531 9,957 9,125
Other assets.. 2,607 5,815 4,630 5,999
The ship property is only £2845 more than it was
six months ago, and there is one new steamer in
commission and another expected shortly, though no
doubt the £72,191 at September, 1913, included
something paid on account of the new ships. Still,
the life of an ark is very limited —especially if it
be a coaster—and a fair amount must be allowed for
depreciation. Nevertheless it appears that more than
sufficient has been written off in this case, so that
the co. has no doubt something in the shape of an
inner reserve. The co. is in a strong position finan
cially, and has £9957 of liquid assets against £5582
of liabilities. In March, 1905, shins were worth
£40,393 and' liquid assets £45,863 ; now the
steamers stand at £72,191 and liquid assets at orb
£9957. Yet the profit is £3099 as compared with
£3562 at March, 1905. It certainly looks like a
hopeless case of tired feeling: and the South Coa t.
being ruled by the Goddess Cow, isn’t the kind of
place that is likely to break a co. of its sleeping/
habit.

Capital is £lO,OOO in £5 shares paid to £l, leaving
a liability of £4 per share, or £40,000 in all, as a
guarantee that the co. won’t bolt with the cash or
put its clients’ funds on the wrong horse and lose the
lot. Of that £4 the co. can call in only £1 10s.
for ordinary business purposes. The other £2 10s.
acts as a “reserve liability for the protection of
clients.” Besides that there are reserves of £11,055,
as a further inducement to the public to trust it. Of
the co.’s funds, £5OOO is in premises, another
£5OOO is in the hands of the State Treasurer as a
surety for sobriety and good behaviour, £13,419 is
lent out on mortgage, £13,310 is in cash or in the
bank, and there are a few sundries. Twelve months
ago the loans on mortgage stood at £13,237, so
evidently the co. isn’t forcing that branch, which
always contains a certain element of risk. From a
shareholders’ point of view also, things are very
sweet. As in most of the newer trustee cos., the
paid capital is comparatively small, so the 10 p.c.
dividend and income tax call for only £550 or there-
abouts per half-year. And as the ,rents and interest
for the past six months amounted to £515, practi-
cally the whole of the commission goes to strengthen-
ing the co.’s position—both for shareholders and
clients.
