That looks better. In the previous twelve months
the co. got it in both eyes at once. Store wages and
other costs were pushed up during that period, while
drought pushed down the wool coming forward. 1 roni
66,464 bales in 1911-12 the quantity put under this
company’s hammer and sold shrivelled to 44,234 ba.es
in 1912-13. in the past year there was an improvement
to the extent of 10,666 bales, and though apparently
no increase has been made in. the selling charges to
meet the higher costs an extra profit of £4142 is
shown, after allowing for the bonus paid to the staff.
The divided profit, after cutting out this small
bonus to the staff, furnishes the clients with a rebate
of 20 p.c. of their commissions, which brings the
selling charge down to 2 p.c. —a rate which is cal-
culated to make Victorian woolgrowers envious and
isn’t calculated to make other people in the business
on this side of the Murray over-pleased with this
co-operative concern. N.S.W. growers are seemingly
poor co-operators. No doubt the fact that for
three or four years in the bad times around 1902-3
the company did not feel justified in making any
rebate, didn’t tend to attract custom. With a view
to prevent the recurrence of that mishap a Clients
Bonus lieserve Fund has been started with a pay-
ment of £2OOO out of this year’s profits. The capi-
tal, fully paid, is £40,000. This and the reserves
are nearly all represented by store premises, housing
both the station produce and grain departments, at
Pyrmont. Total liabilities on March 31 were
£45,893, but against that there were sundry debtors
for £57,758. #
Broken Hill Block 10, one of the once-
big Barrier silver-lead-zinc propositions:—

Tilings don’t look very well by comparison with
the previous half-year, but the backsliding is not
as bad as it looks. As the tail piece to a discredit-
able story which need not now be repeated, old by-
products dumps which had previously been written
down were written up again. That swelled the
previous half-year’s profits by £9660. The metal
markets had also been rising, so that £5579 was
received by way of surpluses on by-products not
previously settled for finally. In the past half-
year the markets moved a trifle the other way,
with the result that there was not only nothing to
collect on account of past shipments, but a reserve
created to provide for the adjustment of any over-
payments was drawn upon to the extent of about
£3BOO. Deducting this amount, the profit for the
half-year is really less than shown above, which is
pretty miserable for a mine with Block 10’s past
record.
Block 10 is, however, a very striking warning to
speculators and people who “invent” in mines that
all mines are mortal and most--mines very short-
lived. In some of its upper pipes Block 10 was
wonderfully rich. But instead of lasting down
indefinitely the lode refused to be worth anything
below about 1615 ft. Over 12 months ago diamond
drilling was done at the 1815 ft. level, and the
result of that and a good deal of driving was the
development of small ore bodies worth about three
to five oz. silver and 3 p.c. to 6 p.c. lead and
almost barren of zinc. The 1715 ft. level was not
much better. Twelve months ago ore reserves were
estimated at 220,800 tons. Since then 97,913 tons
of ore have been broken and sent to the mill. A
development on what is known as the western ore
body has stood to the dying co., and in sight on
March 31 it was estimated that there were still
228,900 tons, so the year’s development has rather
more than supplied the year’s ore. The western
ore body is small and its area ascertained. The
only hope now seems to lie in the discovery of other
western patches. From the bottom of the mine
nothing further can reasonably be expected. From
parallel bodies, on the other hand, it is quite
possible that something substantial may be got,
though it will cost money to look for them.
Without making deduction for depreciation of
plant the profit for the half-year was £23,879, or
at the rate of 9s. 9d. per ton. This includes pay-
ments made on account of tailings and slimes. '
Assuming a similar profit for the future, there is
£111,500 exposed in the mine. The surplus of
liquid assets on March 31 was £97,614. Total of
the two, £209,114. There are 100,000 shares, so
the liquid surplus plus the visible profit is equal
to a shade under £2 2s. per share. But probably
the price based on the last two years’ metal prices
is too favorable. Zinc, at any rate, has tumbled
during that period.
* * * *
J. E. Scantlebury was up again last week
with an application to suspend work at the Hill End
Amalgamated (N.S.W.). After talking of the money
that had been spent, he mentioned incidentally, in
proof that the game wasn’t up yet, that the co. still
had £BOOO to play with. Whereupon the warden
said his course as laid down by the law was quite
plain—he couldn’t grant suspension to a co. that
was in funds, even though those funds might not be
sufficient for the programme it had mapped out for
itself. In that case, said Scantlebury, the game, he
was afraid, was up, so far as Hill End is concerned.
There is money waiting in the Mines Department to
assist in exploring the deep ground at Hill End,
but Scantlebury’s plan for doing it doesn’t find
favor. Probably Scantlebury himself doesn’t find
much favor with the Mines Department either. The
story told now is that if Scantlebury can’t get his
own way, he will recommend that the money in
hand be either divided among shareholders or
invested elsewhere. This looks like bluff, even if it
isn’t. At any rate, shareholders will recognise that
if either course is adopted all their money spent so far
will be lost for ever; whereas if the Mines Depart-
ment’s proposals for testing the deep ground are
accepted, and the Government subsidy be thereby
secured, the money may possibly be all recovered.
Scantlebury may know more about Hill End than all
the officers of the Mines Department put together;
but the loss of the capital hitherto spent, in addition
to the Government subsidy and the chances of making
a “pot,” will be a pretty stiff price to pay for say-
ing that it shall be either Scantlebury’s plan or none
at all.

From Ardlethan: Mining-engineer J. M.
Newman arrived here to-day (Tuesday) from Syd-
ney, and if current rumor is anything to go by, he
has been brought up to report on the Carpathia. It
is just 12 months since he reported on this mine, and
Logan Jack has been there since then. Newman’s
next report should be instructive.

Another appointment has been made to the
staff which the Broken Hill Prop, is getting together
tor is new iron and steel works at Newcastle: J. H. F.
Hill, a Sydney graduate, who for 10 or 12 years past
has been doing solid work on the surface plants at
the mine, has been put in charge of the coke and by-
products plant.
During April the mineral despatches from
Broken Hill totalled 72,039 tons valued at £381,987.
The most valuable shipments were 28,268 tons of
lead concentrates ( £267,643), 40,082 tons of zinc
concentrates ( £105,599) ; 2481 tons of crude lead
ore (£5038); 1109 tons of lead slimes (£3513)
and 99 tons of zinc slimes ( £194). Lead slimes
were valued at over 625. ; zinc slimes at 395. ; lead
concentrates at over £9; and zinc concentrates at
525. per ton.

in a distribution of a Is. 6d. dividend ( £67,412),
bringing the total disbursements from the sale of
silver, zinc and lead produced last year to £183,963.
Dividends credited to 1913 are 7s. to prefs.
( £85,992) and 3s. to ordinaries ( £97,971) equal
to 35 p.c. on pref. and 15 p.c. on ordinary paid-up
capital (20s. per share). To what extent the slump
in spelter will affect profits for the current year
cannot be told, for the exact return on zinc concen-
trates isn’t known for six months or more from the
time they are shipped; but this concern publishes
a monthly return showing' revenue, expenditure andl
estimated profits with metals taken at conservative
prices.
The Grafton Copper (N.S.W.) appears to
be confining its attention to the eastern side of the
leases and the long-neglected Melbourne section, some
nice patches of ore having been struck. Nothing is;
being done, however, toward proving the continua-
tion of the ore body downward below “A” level on
the western side. This latter being the deepest por-
tion worked by the co., its. existence or otherwise at
a depth should be a point of extreme interest.
Sufficient ore is being obtained to keep one of the'
four furnaces in commission, a very small perform-
ance in comparison with past records.
Bourke’s Hill Tin Sluicing (Emmaville,.
N.S.W.), will, like many similar propositions, feel',
keenly the severe drop in the price of the metal.
Latest returns show a marked falling off in output,,
the reason assigned being the increased blasting re-
quired for the harder ground encountered during the 1
period. The probability of an improvement in this,
direction being remote, the combination of circum-
stances point to reduced profits.
* * * *
That aristocratic being, the Adelong Gold'
Estates, looks particularly anaemic just now, but
anxious inquirers can get no satisfaction. When;
J. S Henry departed from the scene to build dredges:
for the Austral-Malay the presumption was that a
betterment was to take place, but the clay has been:
just as sticky to the new man as to the old, and'
the yields very little different. Apparently too much
was promised when Henry moved out, and as there-
is a blister of £12,000 of debentures on the lease:
the trouble with the clay brings worries all round.
Meanwhile, Davies at the one end of the property
and the Adelong Dredging at the other make headway..
* * * *
The Melbourne papers are giving Jack
Nortbey as bad a time over the Lode Hill clean up l
as they gave brother Paul over the Briseis Extended.
The trouble is that Jack Northey shovelled only 15-
tons 2cwt. out of the boxes while he had cleaned up-
21i tons from a much shorter first run. Then-
-40,000 cub. yards averaged ljlb., whereas now
30,000 cub. yards go lib. This doesn’t seem a big
difference, but Cain has been raised by people who-
got in at boom prices. Of course, Northey, being a
mining man, was entitled to regard an average yield 1
as satisfactory, and to telegraph so, but the time-
taken to stream the tin and the costs factor are-
what he is up against. It will take a lot to wipe out
the belief that someone down south had a shrewd'
idea that there would be a smaller return than the-
first clean up. It’s just to clear this point that
manager Northey will have to remove any ideas that
on the mine is someone who knows what’s what:
just as well as the manager does.
* * * *
Tin-dredging in the Stanthorpe (Queens-
land) district is making some headway. The modus'
operandi is the suction dredge without the barge.
A plant consists, as a rule, of two 20-h.p. portables:
driving two lOin. centrifugal pumps, one supplying
water under pressure to the breaking-down nozzles
and the other pumping the tin-bearing sludge-
over the sluice-box. Such an outfit treats:
30 to 40 cubic yards per hour, and cost about £2OOO-
- install. It is being realised that it doesn’t re-
quire a big yardage to pay for this small capital
outlay, and make a few thousand pounds profit. The-
shifting of the plant costs about £5O, and as each-,
remove accounts for at least 10,000 cubic yards, the-
cost thereof is not more than Id. per yard, making
the total treatment cost 4d. to sd. Half a million;
yards of pound ground on these figures leaves a.
nice profit. At present, there are a dozen plants;
at work, and more in contemplation.
* * * *
Hampden Cloncurry is recovering slightly
under 10 p.c. copper—s27B tons of ore gave 524 tons:
of metal. The 463 tons of customs ore smelted dur-
ing the month averaged 12.3 p.c., the caleite fluxes;
1 p.c., and the ore from the co.’s own mines 9J p.c.
Fluxes that carry 1 p.c. copper to the ton are a big
help, although in this case the cost of mining and
transport must be fairly high. This concern carries:
350,000 shares and a debenture debt. Assuming it
can produce 7000 tons of copper a year it requires:
a clear profit of £lO per ton to pay £70,000, or 4s.
per share.
* * * *
Mills’ United (Charters Towers) closed'
its half-year on April 14 with a credit balance of'
£lO,OBl. There is also £3372, due from the Bur-
dekin mill account, bringing the total to £13,453.
This weakening is due to the payment of a dividend!
of Is. per share ( £l5 000) during last half-year.
Mills’ United hasn’t been successful in its recent pros-
pecting operations, but the luck may change. Tribu-
tors’ crushings will be a new feature in the returns..
The last crushing was 106 tons for £393.
Mungana (N.Q.) is sinking its Giroflia.
main shaft another lift below 710 ft. From that level
contour drives proved something like 40,000 tons of'
sulphide ore, so there is sufficient inducement to sink
the shaft another lift. In this mine lead seems to-
preponderate over copper in the deepest levels. At
the Lady Jane mine the rise of the water in the shaft
is very slow, and nothing but a supply from the
neighboring creek will flood the mine sufficiently to
put the fire out. The ore is heavily charged with
sulphur, so the conflagration has a good hold.
The old Queensland Exploration Co.’s
freehold property at Cloncurry, which includes the
Great Australian copper mine, was knocked down at
auction for £21,000, and the Lady leases for £7OOO.
The Great Australian mine will now be under the
management of the Mount Elliott board.
Word comes that a £50,000 co. has been
formed to work Mount Amos (Q.) properties. A
party of tributors has done very well on the
Phoenician during the past two years, and their re-
turns probably had a good deal to do with the
success of the float. A Cooktown syndicate has
erected a five-head battery to treat a big low-grade
proposition lower down the mountain.
* * * *
Cundy, of Victoria, and Fawcett, of N. S.
Wales and the rest of the world, called in to advise
the Government what to do about the Tasmania, give
cold comfort in return for their fee. It was some-
thing like a goldmine in the upper levels; but the
deeper it got the heavier was the water, and the
cost of keeping the mire dry ate up all the dimin-
ishing gold. The old co. paid £772,000 in divs. ;
J. Bull bought it in 1903, and has never got a bean
from it. “Not absolutely hopeless,” is Cundy and
Fawcett’s report in effect; but they wouldn’t advise
anybody to spend £30,000 or more in putting the
shaft down another 100 ft. and opening up the mine
there. The co. is dead-tired of it and the Govern-
ment can hardly do anything in the face of this
report; so the old mine, which started to astonish
the natives in the late ’7o’s, will probably be handed
over to tributors.
* * * #
Mount Bisclioff (Tas.) reports 75 tons
of tin oxide for April, which, coming with a drop
in the price of tin to £l5O per ton, makes the out-
look less bright. The average yield is from 101 b.
to 111 b. of black tin per ton of ore, so the mine has
to raise 20,000 tons a month to produce 100 tons
of tin concentrates. This proportion would be
unprofitable with high costs, but Bischoff can mine
and crush its low-grade stuff for less than ss. per
ton, which makes the concentrate cost £5O.
* * * *
Greenfinch (W.A.) has taken another
dive into the speculative whirlpool and acquired an
■option on the old Haddon Consolidated at Southern
Cross. This show has had a varied career. About
18 years ago it was worked as the Golden Pig by
an English co., with a nominal capital of £250,000.
Having plenty of money to burn, the co. decided to
overcome the water difficulty by railing the ore to
Seabrook, some 180 miles nearer the coast, and
erected an 80-head battery and cyanide plant to
extract the gold. The grade, however, was too low
to cover the cost of railage and treatment costs, and
after a few parcels had been put through, the co.
went bung. Eight years later a local syndicate tried,
■and failed, to make a do of things, and the pro-
perty was left alone until 1911, when a couple of
prospectors came along and located a new reef.
From this over 1000 tons have been raised and treated
for an average, including cyanide returns, of loz.
per ton. There are a few thousands of tons of ore
in sight which should yield a fair profit, but below
water level the mine, so far as The Bulletin can
learn, isn’t \yorth two bob. If, therefore, a market
Tise is engineered in Greenfinch shares on the
strength of crushings or reports, the public would
■do well to stand clear.
* * * *
Insurance against miners’ complaints is
now being considered by the Kalgoorlie Chamber of
Mines and the Workers’ Union. A combined deputa-
tion recently waited upon the Government with a
request for assistance, and was told that the Govern-
ment subsidy previously offered would be increased
from £5OOO to £7500. Government also intimated
that it was thinking over a land-settlement scheme,
to be run in conjunction with the fund that is pro-
posed to be raised conjointly by the State, mine- -
■owners and miners.
***•*•
Associated (W.A.) has bucked up a bit
■as a result of the discovery of 4oz. telluride ore just
■across the boundary in the adjoining North Boulder
Block. The shoot is dipping into Associated ground
between the Nos. 3 and 4 levels, and if it lives down-
ward 50ft. it must pass into that property. A cross-
cut is now being extended to settle the point. Real-
ising the futility of sinking any deeper in search of
payable ore, the management has for some time past
been devoting considerable attention to the top
levels and the opening up of a large quantity ,of fair
:grade ore has been the result.
* * * *
Chaffer’s (W.A.) is busy fixing its new
£26,000 ore treater in position, and hopes to get
■it going by the end of August. About 7000 tons is
■expected to be handled monthly, but as yet the
■grade does not appear to have been fixed. Total
■costs, with allowance for loss in residues, are esti-
mated at about 255. 6d., and as the average recover-
able value of the ore in the mine cannot be much
■over 305., the co. will be lucky if it earns sufficient
to pay a small yearly dividend, and if only one of
the many unexpecteds that happen in mining occur
it may find itself in the unfortunate position of
■being incapable of even doing that.
Great Fingall (W.A.) has been having
■some trouble with the walls of its deep level stopes,
■and has stopped ore-breaking for a few weeks in
■order to do some timbering and filling.
Sons of Gwalia (W.A.) has started to
■drive at No. 20 level, but estimates that it has 200 ft.
; to go before reaching values. At No. 19 level the
■shoot, including a few short breaks, is 1100 ft. long,
■and worth about 375. per ton. A winze recently
■started at 680 ft. south is going down in good ore.
There are tricks in every trade. Recently
The Kalgoorlie and Boulder Miners’ Union announced
That its members weren’t going to work with non-'
unionists after May 1. This drew a retort from the
Chamber of Mines that rather than get rid of non-
unionists to please unionists the mine-owners would
■shut down altogether. It now transpires that the
union’s threat was only a move to get more mem-
bers. So far. it has succeeded in enrolling a few
hundred, and is still adding to the list.
* * * *
The old, old story. In November last
Hurbanks Main Lode (W.A.) lulled the suspicions
■of uneasy shareholders by an official report express-
ing confidence at the prospect of meeting with “fur-
ther extensive lenses of ore,” and the hope to
present a “highly satisfactory report at the end of
the financial year.” A more cheerful feeling was
■also imparted by the Board’s statement that “the
continuance of the present improved outlook, and the
fact that the mine is now equipped with machinery
■of a thoroughly up-to-date nature gives reassuring
prospects of an early resumption of returns to
shareholders.” Now, only a few months later, comes
•an official circular blighting these hopes. It reads-
“In consequence of the persistent falling off in the-
width of reefs and the value of the .ore in the stopes-
the monthly yield has proved insufficient to carry
the cost of the necessary development work. The'
Board has, therefore, decided to temporarily cease
operations.” Arrangements have since been tjffide irt
the West for financial assistance to keep the mine-
unwatered, and, as the Financial Times cuttingly re-
marks. a call upon shareholders’ pockets, instead of
their being replenished with a dividend, appears.to
be the probable early sequel to the official predic-
tions at the time of the annual report.

WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, May 11, 1914.
iMr Dear Arini, —
Guiilaux flew over the Government House
party late in the afternoon. This
made an exciting interlude and diverted the
/attention of Society and others from the
frocks of their best-hated friends. The hydro-
aeroplane which swooped over everybody’s
tlfedsft feat on this occasion is a large, amphibi-
i-dus/ifttfrifthbeast. It is a sort of sky whale. It
Uooks more boat-footed than web-toed, and is
:a small terrace of houses compared to the
:slim, neat-waisted monoplane. I suppose it
isucks up water with its boat-shaped feet,
.•and stows it away like a camel in some in-
ferior tank for dry weather.
Governor Strickland and Lady Edeline
llaid in wait for their innumerable visitors on
ithe sheltered lawn, where Miss Rawson’s
white pigeons used to softly peck the hand
(that fed them at tea-time. The long, thin,
Hniman stream, a Noah’s Ark procession
which has descended from the Flood to the
maodern garden party, moved forward at a
smail’s-pace. It wore the self-conscious ex-
pression inevitable to a gathering in which
a gaping throng is huddled together to censor
/he clothes of its acquaintances.
The tall figure of Papua’s Lieut.-Governor
towered above minor atoms. Papua
is a i. ''Oimtainous country, and it requires a
tall Go\ si'nor to keep an eye on things. The
C. Justice' Cullens stepped into the place of
importance the sub-royal court, where a
blue-robed b; 'djeuard of Strickland Light In-
fantry surrouL wl the throne or garden chairs
of State. His Anglican Legs of Sydney
(Archbishop Wi Vfet) had a reception to
himself, and made a special official entry
through the hall. This landed him at his
Ex.’s coat-tails, wher “ he was pounced on
by General Finn, and led to the vice-regal
hand. Large, fat ladi a -S in the procession
who were addicted to t.he almost obsolete
knee-bob or curtsey got so hurried that they
broke into a kind of tango £tep, and came
near falling over the feet of the Strickland
ladyship. This queer salaam, ft poor rela-
tion of the Court curtsey, is one of the recent
fooleries in the presence of a State Governor
and his wife. It became a ludic'WW# habit
or jump in the Chelmsfords’ reign.
The party brought all the new" sea-
son’s goods out of their moth-balls, where
they’ve been in hiding till the belated winter
puts in an appearance. Mrs. Tom Watson
wore one of the Burgundy-tinted gowns which
are It —so it creased up in front in the truly
latest mode. Mrs. Spencer Brunton chal-
lenged competition in a complicated French
garment that was black silk or something
in its sleeveless jacket, and velvet half an
inch thick in its skirt. The tall, good-look-
ing George Fuller trio —Papa, Mamma and
daughter Gladys—were fine landmarks, not
only by reason of their height, but because
of the imposing gear or top-dressing on the
head of the juvenile member of the party.
This was a blue turban toque that shot a long
thin frond into the air in front, at an alti-
tude of about half a yard above hair-level.
A gasp of respectful admiration followed this
daring skyscraper, till it vanished in the
crowd. Mrs. Trefld, tailored in a blue suit
that fitted like wallpaper, was shepherding—
or shepherdessing—a nice-looking Temora
girl, Miss McCrone. And amongst those pre-
sent were many pale-lavender complexions
and white-washed Society faces with lips
the shade of a ripe pomegranate—guaranteed
not to run in cool weather.
Captain Wallis, the new Help, is a mild-
Tooking young man, with a neat moustache
and a decided waist. This was firmly tied
up the other afternoon in its best party or
full-dress red sash. Just now the recent im-
portation wears the worried expression of
being so brand-new that he’s afraid to
approach even that elderly topic, the weather,
without taking expert advice.
Mr. d’Apice, of the old Italian family that
; settled at Hunter’s Hill in the long ago, has
developed into a useful hon. Help, who rushes
. to the aid of sub-royalty in times of social
• stress. He is something tremendous in the
militia besides. His small but gorgeous per-
■ son, hung with ropes of gold, was one of the
i most extensive upholstered apparitions at
1 Friday’s garden party.
The Antill Pockleys have taken a house in
' the vicinity of Woollahra Point, an elegant
; suburb where those who don’t own a car
t themselves can at least cultivate the correct
i motor manner. Daughter Phyllis, who left
f for England and matrimony the other day,
1 has a creaih-and-roses complexion which the
] British maiden will find hard to beat. An
rex-Flag-Lieutenant (who ought to have had
1 his eyes fixed on his hymn book) once said
Ithat, at a Town Hall prayer meeting to
which he went with the Admirable King-Hall,
tthere was only one complexion in sight that
eould stand the hideous glare of light from
the municipal orbs of electricity. The owner
of it was Miss Pockley. The young man who
is going to claim this pretty Australian is
Mr. Danby, R.N., who used to 100-tenant out
here—l think on the Powerful.
Miss Armstrong, a sister of the Speaker
Armstrong who says “Order” in Brisbane’s
legislative halls, is lingering hereabouts and
gadding gaily round amongst troops of Syd-
ney acquaintances.
Last Saturday’s inter-State tennis match
between Victoria and N.S.W. provided various
of the upper seven or eight with a very good
excuse for appearing in the very latest. But
the gown that flaps and bulges in the wind,
the large check skirt that - carries no duty
stamp, and the blouse-sleeve that begins at
the waist and ends nowhere in particular all
faded away when the new half-way veil
happened along. When the crowd wasn’t
being delighted at the way the genial Alf
Jones was ill-treating Heath, of Vic., it
derived much amusement from the Mrs.
Ford-Miss Stewart match. These two
were at it for 2$ hours, and two more weary
and untidy females I’ve never seen. Then,
as if that wasn’t enough, they both played
in the doubles immediately after. How-
ever, if this isn’t trespassing on the sporting
page, the N.S.W. gentleman players treated
their visitors scandalously and sent them
home with almost as bad a beating as it was
possible to give them. The ladies were more
polite, and allowed the Victorians to go back
with a win, but it was a microscopic one.
Pianist Leonard Borwick has again put
his feet on Australian soil, and will presently
place both his sinewy expert hands on the
keys of the concert grand in Melbourne.
Borwick’s last visit to this part of the globe
was also his first. By some unknown streak
of bad luck it wasn’t the financial success it
ought to have been. This time he ought to
capture large audiences. Anyone with an
ear drum in good working order should rush
the Borwick recitals.
Ella Caspers has returned to her native
land with a really fine contralto, which bears
out the predictions of the enthusiasts who
industriously scraped together the funds for
the lady’s musical education. She sings
very well indeed, but misses the grip on her
audience that comes with the warmer tem-
perament of Kirkby Lunn or Ada Crossley.
The melancholy Italian tenor, Martinelli,
who is included in the Caspers tour, is by
no means the most uninteresting singer we
have heard in the Town Hall. Pianist John
Carlton Fay made agreeable interludes in the
Sydney concerts.
Violinist Raimund Pechotscli, heard again
after nine years absence, has brought home
the well-fed outline of a healthy alderman.
He-fiddles in the highly-respectable manner
that befits his comfortable circumference,
and knocked so many spots out of the Musin
Mazurka the other evening, that it went off
“bang” like a fusillade of penny crackers.
Organist Lilian Frost lent her aid to the
recent Caspers concerts in this town, to loud
and vociferous applause.
Agnes Caspers, younger sister of the con-
falto, is a budding composer. Two ditties
in the lighter ballad style, and a more impos-
ing effort, “Morn,” hint that the Australian
girl is going to make a splash in the musical
pond.
The young and rather inexperienced mana-
ger Philip Potts, who is steering Ella Caspers
through her present tour, has a good deal to
learn about his billet. For instance, when
he makes appointments with the press he
should see that his minor star either keeps or
cancels them. Even the humble press, which
is used as a doormat by the present-day
platform performer, objects to cool its in-
dividual heels in draughty hotel corridors,
while Miss Caspers—who now takes an out-
size in hats —sweeps haughtily past and
ignores her appointment. Also, manager
Potts ought to cut down the programme so
that it doesn’t drag its weary length across
the night. Practically every concert in Syd-
ney is at the mercy of gangs of schoolgirls,
who furiously encore every item. There is
nothing flattering about this clamorous de-
mand for more, as it is absolutely inevitable.
On Thursday night and Saturday afternoon
the Goulburn contralto finally sang to an
audience which was stamping out of the hall
to catch its trains and trams and yawning
with the interminable weariness of senseless
encores for every item.
The twin Wade girleens, daughters of C.
G. Wade, M.L.A., are taking to the light,
fantastic toe —in an amateur way—with con-
siderable alacrity. They hop round the
Imperial Salon twice or thrice a week, in a
most nimble and feathersome style. On May
28 they are going to hostess a party of about
100 souls and 200 feet in that decorative
haunt of the Very Best.
The National Council of Women sat in
great majesty at Government House the
other day, with the Strickland ladyship in
the chair. It is worth a line to note that
whatever may come in the next thousand
years or so of the Nat. Council’s desire to
reform the world, just now it isn’t doing any-
thing very glaring on this side of the earth.
On the other end of the cables it is quarrel-
ling in its best hat, at the International Con-
gress in Rome. The lady who produced a
tome called, “Genesis of the International
Council of Women,” apparently intended to
speed the exodus of Lady Aberdeen. The
latter is known as a practical philanthropist
who has nothing to gain by the cheap ad-
vertisement of having been president of the
overseas council for 20 years. But the
only tangible result one can see in oceans
of chatter, is the unswerving desire of half
the National Council to beat the other half
with its stoutest umbrella.
The veteran charmer, Ellen Terry, is to
have a special reception here by the pro-
fession. If her once melodious speaking
voice retains its former sweetness, her lec-
tures should be easily overheard in the
most remote crevices of the Town Hall. It
used to have a bell-like modulation that
“carried” without any apparent effort, for
Ellen belongs to the old school of actors
which sedulously practised elocution.
Mrs. Bertie Sheldon, a niece of the very
handsome Julius Caro dame, left Sydney in
the Orvieto for the Thames village. A good
engagement in the Margaret Cooper line of
business lures her to that vast antheap.
Artist Tliea Proctor’s beautiful decorative
work —mostly fan designs—is attracting that
eminently useful person, the Buyer. The
Buyer may be thick and plain and ordinary
and sordid. But he has his uses. The
show, which still hangs on the walls
of Anthony Horderns’ attractive little
gallery, is worth taking more than two
trams to find before it is packed up for
flight. Some of it—a big percentage—will
stay in Sydney, for a promising epidemic in
little red blobs is scattered over the collec-
tion. There is a reminisicence of Manly in
the fanciful treatment of some betowelled
figures, “The Bathers,” with the seabreeze
blowing through their garments. But it is
the Dancer, with her strong right leg and
her athletic left limb that has the kicking
power of an ostrich, poised in action, which
artist Thea depicts with such virile life.
The “Souvenir of Genee as La Carmargo,” is
amongst the sold, and the owner is likely
to be envied with enthusiasm.
Mrs. Reg L. Baker is on board the Otway,
now off the Australian coast. She is hurry-
ing home to see her little girl, who very
nearly flickered out with appendicitis during
her parents’ absence on the great wallaby
in foreign parts.
The council of the Royal Colonial Insti-
tute, which has its headquarters in London,
recently elected a number of Australian
Fellows. What a Fellow of the Institute
(who can be of either sex) actually does to
blaze the Commonwealth trail is not known
to those outside the sacred circle. But the
names of Master in Equity, H. I*. Owen,
several members of the Minter family (Syd-
ney), Major Wilkinson (Perth), and mer-
chant John Myers (M.L.) are mentioned as
now wearing a fellowship of this august as-
sembly. Some day I hope to attain the dignity
of fellowess myself.
Dr. and Mrs. Ashburton Thompson are
back again after a year or so on the other
side. He is the ex-medical officer of this
port, who got such a firm grip of the first
big edition of the plague, that it has been
successfully pushed out of town and chased
back to its old haunts in Asia.
The Olympic Ball on May 20 ought to be
a lively event, as the “sports” sets will be a
tremendous draw for the galleryites. The
Town Hall, which stands in its usual place,
expects your presence and superfluous cash
on that evening. Hon. secs. Mrs. Arthur
Deery and Miss May Melliday and hon.
treds. Mrs. Vincent McCauley are in the
forefront of this dash towards the Olympic
funds.
Sir Hartmann Just, who was persistently
stalked by lion-hunters at the Stricklands’
garden-party, is a very mild-looking prey.
He is a smallish, weary-looking Under-Sec.
of State for the “Cawlinies,” with an abbre-
viated appearance of retiring under the brim
of his top hat when the public gaze is glued
on to his unassuming person.
While Rupert Clarke was packing his
toothbrush and a drop of medical comfort
for the expedition in the Fly River country
of Papua, his decorative lady went to
Medlow and took the air in a funeral-coach
motor-car under the escort of a Pomeranian
poodle. Medlow—the Foy-Smith part of it
—is, by the way, to be semi-shut for several
winter months while something extra in
renovations is made.
The British Australasian records the
fuss that persons learned in wood-carving are
making over Svdney’s Ruth Bannister, now
in London. The late Sir Hubert von Her-
komer said that there is nothing Miss Ban-
nister could not do, so far as wood and tools
are concerned. (What a pity such a gener-
ous critic should have to be invoiced as “the
late,” by the, way!_) The Sydney girl owes
her first push forward to the discriminating
eye of Lady Denman, which saw a streak
of genius in Ruth’s wood-sculpture. Her Ex.
pounced on the young sculptress, and for-
warded her on to England to chisel Aus-
tralian fruit and flowers on her British
chimney-pieces. Something that looks like a
career (in consequence) is threatening in the
Thames city.
The long and picturesque personality of
Hugh McCrae —writer and artist —will
shortly be removed from this city. The mov-
ing will be done by a steamer bound for
London, which leaves this port on May 23.
McCrae, who is addicted to handsome millin-
ery of the Spanish sombrero species, is not
going to Mexico to lend his pen to the thump-
ing of Huerta. He’s bound for ’Frisco and
Noo Yark via Tilbury Docks, to place his
literary and artistic wares on the U.S.
market. A stack of friends in this town
wish tall Hugh the best of luck.
Black-and-white check in any sized pat-
tern from the pepper-and-white fleck to the
large draught-board square is becoming a
wide-spread epidemic. Mrs. R. D. Hall wore
one of the tailored checks of moderate dimen-
sions the other day, and looked very well in
the now übiquitous garb. Even the most
daring Rag Prophet, however, didn’t foresee
the frenzied popularity of this harmless pat-
tern. Caps, trousers, waistcoats, even bags,
are now taking on the garb that used to be
called shepherd’s plaid. If Harry Lauder
will only wear a kilt of the black-and-white
family we shall all be happy. Skipper Cum-
berlege, of the Warrego—a naval dandy who
is “frightfully rushed, don’t you know,” by
local Society—used to dazzle Maltese belles
in a suit of this design, but lie has tamed
down to more sad-colored earb these days.
According to “Snowy” Baker, who writes
from Paris, the big boxing contest between
Adonis Carpentier and the mulatto Jeannette
looked like a first-night at the Opera House,
Frenchwomen dressed in the last scream of
fashion (scream is the only word fof the
1914 styles) and glittering with gems made
up a big percentage of the audience at the
ringside.
Percy Grainger has written what he is
pleased to call a “Colonial Song”—presu-
mably a representation of “Colonial” yearn-
ings and a struggle to voice the deeper feel-
ings of Dungog and the soul-wail of Kangaroo
Island. As the golden-haired pianist is a
South Australian himself, it is curious that
he should have forgotten that the Australian
doesn’t glory in the old, contemptuous term
of “colonial.” And even in the bad old days
S. Australia was never a “colony.” Its official
designation, from its first beginning till the
day when it became a State, was “province.”
In that matter it stood alone. However, the
latest effort of this person who doesn’t even
know that “colonial” is out of date is a com-
position for an orchestra and two voices, and
it ought to be harmonious. Percy Grainger
docs understand the art of constructing pleas-
ing noises. He explains the germ of his
song thus : “I tried to voice my personal feel-
ings about my own country and people, and
a certain kind of emotion that seems to me
not untypical of native-born colonials in
general.” This is rather high-falutin’. A
“colonial” must be native-born unless he just
sprang up like Topsy and wasn’t born any-
where at all. Everybody is a native of the
country he was born in unless he was born,
somewhere else, and nobody ever is.
Canberra is getting into print as a place
that contains a Commonwealth Public Ser-
vice all to itself. A Mr. Maurice Hyde, now
in London, is alluded to by an overseas’ jour-
nal as having been a recent member of the
service in Canberra. There is no visible
join between the bush village and Esperanto,
but I understand that Mr. Hyde found the
latter polyglot tongue a great help during
his travels on the continent.
Journalist George C. Craig, who is re-
ported to be still busy with his pen at the
age of 72, was recently interviewed in Lon-
don. He has behind him a strenuous 47
years of the inky way in Australia, mostly
divided between N.S.W. and Queensland.
PI is present proposition is to settle down in
smoky Glasgow (his crowded native hamlet)
barring a sudden desire to flee back to the
land of sunshine and great spaces.
The Delmar Cavendish-Smith wedding
occurred in London on March 30. The bride
is the well-dowered Ruth Smith, who hailed
from the Glen Innes district of N.S.W.
Delmar Cavendish was the dapper purser
on the Mooltan who was so popular with
the flanper on the high seas.
The harem veil, which is still hanging on
the outskirts of local Society, gives the im-
pression that the lady who is behind it
wears a young beard that sprouts all over
her chin in staccato spots. It looks a good
deal like a blind half-pulled up from below.
Everything (or nearly everything) has some
good point about it. The harem veil is a
godsend to women who have ugly mouths
that snap together like the long thin sides of
a meat-safe.
Kubelik and his dressy wife were recently
snapped walking along Fifth Avenue, New
York. The lady’s clothes, which can he
taken at face value as the ultimate note of
Fashion, . include a white-fcatherd hat of
monstrous tufts arranged in the Choctaw
Indian (not Christian Israelite) style, and
a pair of plain, undisguised white trousers.
A dark winter coat is cut up in front to
show these gentlemanly garments, which are
topdressed by something that looks like a
white tunic to the knees. The United States
alderman has been so agitated about women’s
clothes of late that I suppose he has been
meeting trousered ladies in his walks abroad.
London Daily Mirror recently illustrated
the three stages of the feminine cranium.
First the lady of rueful countenance and
straight, lank locks. Next, the same woman
with her head surrounded by something that
looks like an infernal machine. She is going
through a curl pilgrimage, and her hair is
having its pores opened in a new way by
science and damp flannel twisted round metal
cylinders. A few more things are done to.
bully the lankiness into curls, then the re-
sults are dried by electricity or struck by
lightning or something of the sort. The
third stage shows the lady again. She is
surrounded by a mop of curls, and appears
to have fainted dead away for joy. The
Swiss who thought out this scheme of getting
even with hair that falls as straight as a
shower bath is a most daring and inventive
person. But I don't agree with the London
scribe who calls it “the simplest thing in the
world.”
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, May 11, 1914.
My I)eab Yarrie, —
The Repertory Ball started with a crash
of music, at 9 o’clock last Wednesday night,
and rolled along until 2 o’clock next morning,
when the last lingering revellers were blown
down the stone steps with a charge of
Anthem. The balcony gazers were enter-
tained by a procession and tableaux, with
interludes for the arrival of the reigning
stars. The tableaux were generally effective ;
but when the component parts got loose on
the dancing space, the right-thinkers present
breathed hard through their noses, pulled
down their mouths at the corners, and gave
other signs that they were Shocked to the
Marrow. Clarence Weber, as Herakles, in
the Greek group, was mostly leopard skin and
beautiful pink muscles. Aubrey Hoad, as
Joseph, inhabited the short white garment of
a blameless life and had his bare toes well
trodden on, until, in sheer agony, he Up-
ended himself and walked out on his hands,
meanwhile frowning terribly with his feet.
(No! that’s what he would have done had he
been a comedy acrobat. The gruesome fact
is that he had to stand and bear it.) Then the
Wagnerian gods ! Well, really, Yarrie, if it
hadn’t been a charity occasion I would have
launched myself from the balcony and at-
tended to them personally with my umbrella.
Tenor Kirby came along in his bath-robe and
a full set of whiskers. The full set looked
like Ned Kelly, but a silver cross proclaimed
him Lohengrin—a five-feet something Lohen-
grin. The Governor-General and the State
Ex. attempted no disguise—which was just
as well. The part of Henry VIII., which
was thought to be the G.-G.’s disguise, was
filled by Colin Templeton. Lady Denman, as
Queen Katherine, was magnificently clothed
in green and gold brocade over a red petti-
coat, with a stomacher of gems and a gold
inesh veil held out by a wide lace collar. A
diamond tiara twinkled overhead, and gems
gleamed here and there about her splendid
duds. Lady Stanley chose the part of one of
Henry’s most respectable wives—or was it a
sister she impersonated? (I must get out of
the habit of using pages of my dog’s-eared
history book as curlpapers!) Anyhow, the
black velvet dress had bold lines of fat white
pearls and fine sketch lines of seed pearls,
while a fine white veil floated over her
starched collar. The under-garment was of
terra-cotta and gold. Help Wilson was a
gorgeous Earl of Surrey, and Curwen looked
as if he had purloined some girl’s fox furs.
It clashed somewhat with his announced role
of “nobleman.” Help Pollok, in everyday
rig, attended* his chief with Victor Hood.
Gregan McMahon was Shakspeare or Hall
Caine or somebody. Mrs. McMahon wasn’t
even herself. She had tired herself out ar-
ranging the show and looked pale.
The beauty girl, Purchas (she is a cousin of
Melba, my dear —you remember that, of
course!) made a magnificent Zuleika, dressed
in tawny clothes and jewels. She was carried
round the room by four bronze suburban
slaves, with a canopy of chiffon over her
head so that the gallery folk could see her,
and if she didn’t look sufficiently cold and
proud to suit the must bitter critic, it wasn’t
her fault. If Pharaoh himself had called round
for a bit of supper she would have told him
to go out to the kitchen and fossick out the
cold meat for himself. Little “Bundles”
Lincoln (son of the late actor of that name)
was Benjamin. Young Eddy, dressed in
bronze paint and some purple chiffon, helped
to carry Zuleika. Mrs. Hilliard (Cleopatra)
was also to have been carried; but something
happened to the tickets of her four brawny
carriers and those young men were abashed
by the remarks addressed to them by a door-
keeper and went away home to wash; so
Cleopatra walked, which was much better
for her. The competition for good-looking
men was something horrible. Men were
stolen at the eleventh hour and screams of
“He’s mine! He’s mine!” were heard in all
direction. Tonithjr Cochran, who wears side-
boards and has a perfect genius for clothes,
disguised himself as Svengali and put some
black face-fungus on his chin. Farmer
Oswald Syme was a beautiful sight in a
purple coat as one of the Quality-street Set.
Dr. Syme was a farmer in a smock, and his
wife wore one of the broadest crinolines I
have ever seen. Mrs. Geoffrey Syme put in
a brief appearance in her “Kismet” dress;
but she is still on the sick-list and a nurse
was handy amongst the onlookers waiting
to take her home. She and her husband are
shortly to take a trip to Colombo. Mrs.
Willie Newbisrin was one of the reception
committee. She filled the bill (and part of
the doorway) in blue brocade. Muriel Starr
came along as she had on the stage
when she was Madame X. She resurrected
cheerfully as a tango butterfly with tulle
“poufings” on side panniers clamped by ropes
of theatrical pearls. The jewel-chain gave
way in the middle of a dance and she had
to be hurriedly pinned together.
The tableaux at the Repertory Ball were
managed by Eric Maxon, of the Royal, Reg
Carey, of Her Majesty’s, and Gregan
McMahon. At midnight the whole enter-
tainment was held up for the Ellen Terry
welcome by the theatre people. Ellen looked
dreamy and pale. Her hair was bound with
rose-velvet ribbons and diamonds, and she
wore an ivory brocade that could have stood
alone. She replied to Bert Bailey’s address
in a few words, spoken in a deep, rich voice.
And then the gyrations were resumed, while
the star sat on the dais with the vice-regal
group and chatted briskly with friends who
converged on her from all directions. The
theatrical folk swarmed in after the shows
closed up. The management of Her Majesty’s
allowed the Persian ballet girls to attend in
their stage costumes. Ada Reeve led a set
of her own. Daisy Jerome lost a leopard-
skin coat during the evening. All the furriers
in town seemed to have hired out their skins,
so it must have been comparatively easy to
smuggle the precious thing away. Daisy’s
mother was last seen in consultation with
the lady of the cloakroom. That severe and
important official smiled grimly at the tur-
moil, for she is used to such scenes. It is
i surprising how many people lose valuable
property while they are dancing in allegedly
good society. Sometimes one might as well
have been with Chinese pirates.
The Gaud Mayoress arrived, all spread-
ing brocade and lace collar, as the
Empress Josephine. She was so splen-
did and made such a fine entrance
that I had to hurry out and run
three times round the block to work off my
excitement. Jenny Brenan disguised herself
in a powdered wig as Lady Teazle. She and
the Misses Hyams looked on approvingly at
the sets they had been drilling. Docie Sharp,
a Toorak girl of fine physique, paraded an
“As You Like It” set, for which she had made
the dresses. She herself was a strapping
Rosalind. Mrs. Glenloth Peter (her Celia)
wears that name in real life. A brace (or
should it be a covey?) of good-looking men
completed the group. Maggie Stirling made
an imposing Rrunhilde in the weighty Wag-
ner gang. Black-eyed McCubbin youngsters
acted as heralds and blew trumpets whenever
anything was going to happen. Someone
with a "useless megaphone then barked at
the crowd and clouded the issue. One partic-
ular two-step tune was encored seven times
till the conductor laid down his fiddle-bow
and thereby intimated that he would rather cut
up his violin for kindling-wood than saw it
off again. The financial result —which should
be handsome —won’t be known for a week or
so. Repertorians say that “it is not so much
the money as the advertisement.” Philis-
tines who never knew of the existence of a
Repertory Theatre are now enlightened.
But, with a ball as an introduction, their
impression probably is that it is a new kind
of vaudeville shop.
If you happen to be in St. Kilda, my dear,
on the night of June 2, you will discover
the O’Donnell ball in progress. (St. Kilda is
the O’Donnell’s mayor’s nest.)
Lady Denman last week gave a theatre
and supper party to the hard-working com-
mittee of the successful Loan Exhibition, by
way of recognising its exertions.
The Austral Salonites (God bless them!)
had doubly bad luck with their Ellen Terry
excitements. On the morning of the boat’s
arrival they rose early, put on their most
staggering duds and hats, and proceeded in
state, armed with bouquets, to gritty, lump-
erish Port Melbourne—the very last place
on earth for any well-dressed female to be
abroad in on a working morning. Arrived
there, they waited patiently on a foggy, chilly
pier till Ellen Terry sent word that she could
not possibly rise for another hour, and after
that would require a dressing interval.
Shakspearian fiends and all sorts of literary
bodies and carcases were also sitting on gang-
ways, and old, weatherbeaten boxes with
other bouquets and speeches ; so the wearied
Salonites gave it best, and arranged a cosy
indoor welcome for the next day. It was a
party-concert or concert-party at the rooms,
and the club colors twined affectionately
newer and fresher bouquets; but, alas, all
they got was manager Blascheck and a doc-
tor’s letter to the effect that it was physically
impossible for his distinguished patient to
attend welcoming parties and keep her public
engagements as well. She had been so ill-
used by the motion of the ocean that rest
was imperative. The Salon swallowed its
disappointment, denounced the ocean in high-
pitched voices, and sent the bouquets along
to the hotel.
A gentle friend who witnessed the Salon
retreat from Moscow down that long, chilly
pier said that it was terrible to see the infuri-
ated old chooks returning with all their gush
uncorked. A female doctor who drives her
own machine wrenched the organ handle in
front, so madly that she tore the whole con-
traption to pieces, and had to take the frag-
ments home in her peggy-bag. Then, after
all these gibbering people had departed, the
famous star made a. sort of triumphal pro-
gress down the pier in a flower-decked chair
carried by four sailors. The brilliant, blue-
eyed smile she gave her patient admirers
made most of them forget the long wait.
Someone who knew her years ago in London
commented smilingly, “That’s just like Ellen
Terry! She always did exactly as she
pleased, and no one could scold when she
came coaxing.”
The Gaud Mayoral Hennessys’ reception to
Ellen Terry , was quite satisfactory. People
who do not trouble about rushed and over-
crowded society parades came out of their,
exclusive rabbit burrows to welcome the
stage pet of 40 years. The crowded Council
room was so full of food that it looked like
a famine relief depot, and the Mayoress’s
apartment was heaoed with flowers. Mrs.
Hennessy was impressive in black panne vel-
vet and a black feathered hat. She ushered
local peeresses into the Presence, and great
was her rush. I don’t know if I have men-
tioned it before, but here goes: Ellen has the
most wonderful eyes ; perpetual youth shines
in their depths and intelligence twinkles.
She can never look really old while her eyes
are open. The cunning lady had a lace
veil tied round her hat and neck so that a
youthful glimpse of shoulder was visible.
The hat and the side veil made an ideal
setting for the famous profile, and a classical
cloak of black moire hung over a deep blue
dress. She certainly looked a picture—
and not an old lady picture either.
The Cresswell dame, Mrs. Gurner and
Lesly Maddeji sped round the idol. The
Pinschof dame, who was accompanied by her
one remaining girl, threw up a spray of com-
pliments on the outskirts. Mrs. Godfrey, a
grandmother to many infants, stood up
straightly in black furs and agreed with
some critic that years need not a'ge. Some-
body else concurred with a friend that a
stitch in time need not save nine, and that
it is absurd not to have more than one cook
when making broth. The Terry was motored
away from these moralisings after a brief
stay. She* vowed that she had not got a
real night’s rest during the long voyage and
had to make it up somehow.
When she made her appearance on the
Town Hall platform the illustrious Ellen
was assailed with a blizzard of applause.
When it had passed over, she returned .a
rich, resonant “Thank you!” (it sounded
like a hearty and unaffected “How-do-you-
do?”), and got to business. She was in
front of a shadowy moss-green curtain which
assisted thf imaginative listener’s eye and
made use of the trick of turning her back
to the house. In a heavy Juliet scene, she
Vbiled her fgce as she moved about the stage,
and from the glimpses one might have taken
her for a crouching girl. The Denman lady,
wrapped in white and gold brocade and at-
tended by Gentleman Helps, led a flock of
the Best fetoales, most of whom seemed to
be half asleep after their Repertory dancing
of the previous night (and morning). They
simply had to go to the first Terry night,
else they would have made a brief stay-at
home evening of it with a magazine and a
fire. After a scene had been completed, the
star departed from the platform, vice-royalty
made an undecided move which caused a
flurried pianist to thump out the Anthem,
and the audience were stampeded for the
night, though the show was only partly
through. Manager Blascheck got on to the
platform in time to tell the last coat-tails
that it was all a mistake and that Miss
Terry would give a- complete programme on
Saturday.' ■
Like the newest corset, the motor-car is a
great hand at reducing the girth of things.
The Yon Richens, well-known Adela’de
people, just jumped into theirs, along with a
niece, Miss Englehart, and joined in Syd-
ney’s Easter parade. -On the way over they
paid an afternoon call or so upon friends in
Melbourne, and en route , and on the return
journey took' along the track that runs
through Cobar and Broken Hill, planning to
strike the latter silver dustheap about the
time of its annual race-week, frivolities. By
way of an- advt. for sunny" Australia they
mention that they hadn’t unstrapped the hood
of the vehicle since they left Adelaide. Still,
as they bumped out of Sydney along Parra-
matta-road —a far worse track, they say,
than the Coorong desert that you read the
thrilling stories about —they probably re-
flected that a motor-track can have too many
days of sunshine.
The eldest of the sporting Bill Leonards
group of sons has married Miss Doris Rey-
nolds, from the Ma State. They met on
the wet track to the Smoke, and were tied
up in the Cold Country last Wednesday very
quietly, the religions being mixed. He is
head of a big wool business here, and the
bride is said to be well dowered. The pair
will trip back here in time for the spring
season, if not sooner.
Miss Gertrude Johnson, a University Con-
servatorium girl, is the latest aspirant for
concert and grand-opera applause. The
Gaud Mayoresses interesting herself in the
deserving case, and is rallying the Best Fed
to help with a benefit concert. Melba has
given Gertrude a letter to Ponsot, who at-
tends to promising top-notes ;. also an encour-
aging pat on the back and her artistic bless-
ing. Now Gertrude’s many friends want to
gather the coin that will enable her to de-
liver the letter in person. Dolores, Clara
Butt, Kirkby Lunn and other persons of
eminence have supplied hearty encourage-
ment, so it seems up to the public.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
Dr. A. E. Wigg, who left here for Europe
some months ago, died • in London on Sun-
day. He went away with Mrs. Wigg, his younger
daughter and Miss Mary Murray. They had been for
some time on the European continent before striking
London —and disaster. He was one of the Wigg, Son
and Co. clan, and a brother of Dr. Higham Wigg, of
North Adelaide.
The Society of Arts annual show has been opened
in the Society’s rooms on North Terrace, where about
140 pictures are displayed. Incidentally, about 60
had to be refused wall-space through no demerit on
their part. The inadequate wall in question has been
covered with hessian—an artistic improvement. Mrs.
Barringer has some attractive panels, and Miss Rose
Lowcay, the two Ashtons and Miss Bosworth all score
more or less heavily.
Miss Cissie Hughes is here on a six-weeks visit
from Queensland. She is staying with her brother,
Mr. Herbert Hughes, at Athelney.
Gradually Government House is working through
its list of first appearances. On Saturday it looked
in at both the football match on the Adelaide Oval
and the military sports on the Jubilee Oval. On
Monday the Victoria League took the floor —or to be
exact, hired part of the Grand Central Hotel —and
invited Lady Galway to tea-drinking. She duly
drank, in a frock of dark-blue crgpe de Chine. She then
stood at the head of the stairs, and met everybody—•
who is anybody in a female sense—and ultimately
went home in the awed state of one who has been
shown Mt. Chimborazo for the first time.
On Thursday a few of the London-bound passengers
came up from the mailboat for lunch at the Queen
Adelaide Club. Amongst them were Mrs. Byron
Moore and Mrs. Atkinson-Wood, from Melbourne;
Miss Grace Noble and Captain Carr, recently of the
Psyche. On the same evening Mrs. Dalton Green
gave a dinner at the club in honor of Mr. Ernest
Williamson, the new manager of the Executor and
Trustees Company, and his wife. The John Shiels
and their daughter, Dr. Hayward and Mr W. G.
Gaveson were of the party, which wandered along
to the Tivoli after dinner.
Still the exodus continues. The following are now
leaving in the usual olu direction: The Lendons, ex-
Senator Symon, and Dr. and Mrs. M. J. Symons (for
pity's sake, don’t let the comp, confuse them. One
family has no final “s” and the other has, and mis-
understanding on the point may lead to international
complications, or worse).
The Galways gave a dinner on Tuesday and a
luncheon on Thursday. The hungry and the indigent
who were fed at the former entertainment included
the Chief Justice Ways, the J. H. Gordons, the
Bishop Thomases, the Downers, Judge Murray and his
sister, the Alick Murrays, the T. E. Barr Smiths,
Dr. and Mrs. Martin, the C. H. Angases, and the
John Shiels. The luncheon was mostly a Parlia-
mentary affair. Members of both parties fed in per-
fect amity—at any rate, they refrained from throw-
ing the plates at one another. Men can do this sort
of thing where women can’t. What perfect ladv
could tear food in company with one whom she had
declared publicly to be the worst kind of villain and
harpy ?
Archdeacon Dove, who was 82 when he died, had
been in S.A. for 53 years. And for 52 of them
he had lived at Walkerville. This was perhaps the
most remarkable thing about him. Incidentally, he
was a good old man, who had worked hard in his vast
Bush vineyard in his day, and who grew roses as
well as any professional or other gardener in Aus-
tralia.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

“Aesculapius”: Tn ancient Greece they used to
erect temples over natural springs and give to each
a titular diety. l\e moderns are no less appreciative
of the healthful virtues of a mineral water —only our
methods differ somewhat. For instance, up at the
famous Helidon Spring, the water is secured just as
it bubbles up from the earth, and hermetically sealed
until the process of aeration and bottling is com-
plete. You don’t need a Greek fineness of palate to
appreciate the cool charm of Helidon Spa—it has
the same delicious refreshingness to Billjirn as to the
Earl of Hopetoun. As a blender with wines and
spirits, too, it gives just that added snap and
sparkle that counts so much in the sum of pleasant-
ness. You’ll be wise to always make it a practice to
“Figaro - ’: Read a par. here the other week about
Nature planting the giant stinging nettle in N.Q.,
but close beside it the tree whose leaves serve as an
antidote to its sting. This provision of Nature is
in no way unique, for, just as she curses us with
indigestion so she blesses us with Helidon Spa.
After dining out you’ll have no next morning head-
ache if you take a bottle of “lielidon” before turning
in—this delicious mineral water is Nature’s gift to
those who have dined not wisely but too well. A single
glass will dissolve the undigested food and at the
same time ensure a perfect night’s rest. Quite apart
from its health aspect, “Helidon” greatly enhances
the flavor of wines and spirits—so truly the way of
wisdom is always to
SPLIT IT WITH
HELIDON.

“Machete”: Rupert Clarke, in his Sydney
newspaper interview's, given before starting on his
Papuan expedition, unloosed quite a sheaf of inac-
curacies. He discoursed, for instance, on the pos-
sible dangers of the trip. As a fact the dangers are
hardly worthy of mention. Neither Macgregor nor
Albertis, both of whom explored the Fly River, had
any special trouble with local natives. Albertis
did the trip in ordinary boats, and found the current
a big handicap. Macgrcgor’s mode of transit was
a sciapheap old steam-launch, the Ruby, which had
to bum wood in a coal firebox. Even then, and in
spite of his craft’s excessive draught, he got to where
the Fly River runs into Dutch territory. Reasons
of State, or something, forbade him to go farther.
Clarke, with his very much up-to-date gasoline
launch, should be able to go anywhere the law
permits. But if he decides to follow up the
meandering Fly through Dutch territory, until it
wanders back again, to finally dive through British
into German territory, he will of course need a
sheaf of permits. No one knows the Fly’s origin,
though its volume and sudden and tremendous floods
point to sources in snow-clad mountains. Now
no one has had an authenticated glimpse of per-
manent snow in Papua, so the expedition has a chance
of discovering a Papuan Everest. Rupert also made
the rather amazing statement that he hoped ,to emu-
late Sir William Macgregor, who “never had to pull
a trigger on his many expeditions.” As a matter of
fact. Sir Wullyum dealt out more stoush to the
heathen than any traveller before or since his reign.
One notable occasion was the smashing up of the
dreaded Tugeri raiders west of the Fly. For this
action the very rare Papuan medal was granted to
the Armed Native Constabulary. Another notable
scrap took place on the Baum River, where he was
attacked on leaving one of the huge swamp villages.
Again, he cut off a great cannibal raiding partv
fiom Collingwood Bay, on the N.E. coast, catching
them in the Musa River. On all these occasions
the local undertakers, the crocodiles, were kept
working overtime.

“Arnheim”: “Wallaby” (B. 15/1/’l4)
contradicts “Mil,” who says the north coast of
the Territory isn’t scratched as far as pearl-shell and
trepang is concerned. Well, “Mil” is right. “Mil”
knows better than “Wallaby,” and I know better
than either of them. “Wallaby” talks of the “coral
reefs” between Thursday Island and Darwin. There
aren’t any coral reefs except a few off the Vernons.
They are all rock. Trepang is never found on reefs
of any sort, and shell only in very small quantities.
Also, it is never “dived” for on reefs. There is no
white company operating. Edwards, Jolly, Witherden
and Cooper are the only pearlers on this coast, and
every one of them employs Jap divers. The crews
are Filipinos, Timor men, South Sea .men and Japs.
There is not one Macassar man working in pearl or
trepang on the whole N.T. coast. Brother Binghi
does all the trepang diving that is done at present on
fho coast, and I defy “Wallaby” to find or nominate
any man of any other race that goes down into the
water after a fish. Some of the trepang bosses help
to pick up when the tide recedes, but they never
dive. The gins are the best divers, and get the
most fish. They’ve got to or their bucks would waddv
them. In days past when the proas had a.
corner on the trepang industry all the diving
for trepang was done by Macassar men. They did
not work Binghi in the water; his methods were too
deliberate for them. Macassar captains arrived with
a proa full of men (slaves practically), and worked
them day and night. Frequently the poor devils
were marooned to make room for cargo, and that
was the last of them. There never was an “art” in
“lobbing” on pearls, and as marfv pearls are found
on the average now as ever there were.

“E.”: For quite a time I was haunted by
the ghost at Heeley’s. We were prospectors in
Gippsland, and Heeley was an adjacent settler whose
wife did our washing and provided milk and butter,
t he first we heard of the ghost at Ileeley’s was when
Tim appeared in our tent at about 11 o’clock one
night after the whole clutch had turned in. Tim
was leading Honora by the hand, and Tim was
deathly white. So was Honora. “Will one of yeh,
’r two maybe, come ’n’ see to th’ ghost be me dure?”
said Tim. “Ghost be th’ dure,” echoed Honora.
Ghost be damned!” said Tang'ie. “Go home, you
idiots.” “I will never go home alone this blessed
night,” said Heeley, “wid the ghost he me dure.”
“A long, terrible white ghost,” piped Honora. I
went with Heeley, convinced that at any rate the
man had a deathly fear on him. At his house he
pointed out where the ghost had stood against a
big tree about 50yds. from his door. The tree was
hollow. I examined it thoroughly, and found no
trace of a ghost. Three times after that Heeley
and Honora came creeping to our tent, hand in hand,
with a piteous plea for a “fine, sound man” to go
with him and lay the ghost. We declined for a
month. Then I consented to sleep at Heeley’s a
night or two in the hope of seeing the ghost. I
saw it. It came after 10 o’clock, and was a fair-
dinkum ghost outlined in pale light against the tree.
It had luminous eyes, and a long, unwavering stare,
most disconcerting to a nervous man like myself.
I was probably as white as Heeley when we went
forward, shoulder to shoulder, to investigate. But
the ghost faded out at 20 paces, and a close exami-
nation revealed nothing. I looked the tree over
next day without result. On the following night I
saw the ghost again. On the third night I elected
to lie in ambush on the farther side, and caught
Tracker red-handed. I did nothing to Tracker, but
Heeley, with the help of heaven and Ilonora’s able
assistance, nearly killed him. It was all a matter of
a lost cow, and right of access to Tleeley’s “crick.”
Tracker called in supernatural forces to avenge him-
self. He did it ingeniously. On the side of the
hollow tree facing Heeley’s home there was lit tie
left but bark. On the inside of this Tracker had
scraped the outline of a man till the bark was so
thin as to be transparent, hiding his work with
charcoal dust. A lighted candle attached to a
long string did the rest. When anybody approached.
Tracker, hidden among the saplings, violently jerked
the string attached to the candle, and the thing was
extinguished among the debris.
'‘Stargazer”: He was sitting near me at
Melb. Theatre Royal the other night. Plain to be-
hold was he. She was pretty and smartly gowned.
That he was a country cousin fresh from the pasture
and cow yards of Billybingle was my sudden conclu-
sion. I saw no subsequent reason to revise it. His
evening suit was much too copious, his hair had hail
someone at it with a hatchet. The operative must
have been a bad axeman. His hands were large and
red, so was his face, which was decorated with too
few whiskers, such as the nice girl with him simply
loathed. Then his feet were too big for the theatre,
and were cased in boots that were more suitable for
the quest of cows than the pursuit of art. The poor
girl was suffering from him, suffering horribly. It
is all very well to abuse as caddish, snobbish and
despicable those poignant emotions that arise within
us when we have to pilot a lumbering bush brother
round town, but why should we bear such trials
patiently, and resent being run over by a motor-’bus ?
Rube walked on the feet of most of the people getting
to his seat, and they all cursed and scoffed at him.
while the girl burned red. In the seat he made
stiange noises with his teeth, and put his tect in
conspicuous places, and talked about cows, just as if
cows were something to be proud of. During Act 1
lie applauded loudly, and uttered appreciative com-
ment, like “Ryebuck!” and “Gripes, lie’s a bit of orl
light 1 When the curtain fell again he turned to the
dying girl, and said he in a loud voice: “Scot, the
feller with the cigger done his bit good!” Cruel
people in the vicinity laughed. I felt like snatching
UP suffering sister, and escaping with her from
the building. No man missed a nobler chance of
figuring as one girl’s true hero. Presently Rube had
an idea. “1 know,’ he cried, “I’ll get yell some
lollies.” “Oh, no, please, don’t.” She loathed
lollies. My oath, I will!” said Rube, going over
the people’s feet again. It was useless to protest,
lie went out. I followed him in thought. He was
dubious what to buy. “Gimme one of those,” he said
pointing to a highly-colored packet. Anyhow, the
packet he brought back was extremely decorative on
Chinese lines. He disarranged patrons’ feet again.
He offered the distressed damsel the packet. She
look it to cover her confusion. She tried to open it.
She tried with too much effort. It came open unex-
pectedly, and half its contents flew into her hair
H was a packet of confetti! Immediately the cur-
iam rose the girl fell ill, and had to go out. She
never returned.

A BALLADE O’ BUNYIPS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A\ hen rhymers rhyme of g'riffins grey
And stale romancers spin their lies
Of dragons lurking in the clay,
And breathing death in maidens’ eyes;
lien out of stiff Siberian snow
They grub the mammoth huge and tall,
1 pay no heed—for well I know
A Festive Bunyip beats them all.
Uncertain yet is’t where he dwells,
Mis length of claw, his breadth of bill;
And though his form each bushman tells
He’s just a trifle shapeless still.
Me know he lurks in misty gloam
Sometimes a bulk in low lagoon,
And that he’s rather prone to roam
And prowl for prey beneath the moon.
But still he keeps incognito
In dark stand-offish attitude,
Avoids the giddy crowd, and so
Brings up his curious little brood.
He has been known to bolt a black
Who disappeared without a call,
Without a single tell-tale track—
The Grisly Bunyip beats ’em all !
When yarns are scant and lies run out
(A thing that seldom happens now)
You'll hear the camp romancers spout,
And deck him out from tail to pow.
A varied garb of feathers, fur,
They lend him with a casual fin,
And send him with a squeal and purr
to sally forth his prey to win.
They tell how once he sneaked along
Behind a roysterer rolling down
The track—with many a jolly song—•
And hypnotised him far from town ;
And though they found the victim coiled
Asleep, and sound in wind and limb,
Yet was the beast not wholly foiled—
For lo! he had canoodled him!
And none the picture may contest
Or doubt its grim fidelity,
For none can bring the parlous pest
And show him plain for all to see;
And so each artist takes the brush
And paints the beast at evenfall
As suits his fancy—or his lush—
A Festive Bunyip beats them all!
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER F.


Why You Should Buy a BEALE Piano
NINETEEN thousand five hundred previous buyers have been satisfied. We have
made and sold that number of pianos, and more Beale Pianos are used in
Australia than of any other make—British or foreign. They withstand alike the
desiccation of our torrid summers in Kalgoorhe, Broken Hill and Cobar, or the melting
moisture of sugar lands in North Queensland and Fiji. You are safe with a Beale
Piano in any climate.
The most perishable part of all other pianos is admittedly the tuning system or
wrest-plank. The Beale tuning-system being solely of iron and steel is warranted to last
for generations, it cannot fail, and every buyer will confirm this. A wooden plank will
gradually crush, as all makers have to admit, under frequent tuning and twisting of the
pins. No matter how often tuned —and with Beale pianos tuning is seldom required -
the Beale system is not affected.
Beale Pianos contain the choicest Action in the world, by the most expensive makers
of Pans, specially prepared for the Beale Pianos. Similar actions are also used by the
leading piano builders of America, France, England and Germany, but only in the
higher class.
Beale Pianos although made particularly for exacting Australian conditions have a reputation abroad of which we
possess abundant proofs. They have won the Grand Prix and three gold medals at the great Pranco-Biitish Exhibition,
London, 1908. Our pianos are solely used in the splendid Strand Palace Hotel, London, and at an aristociatic Fiench sea-
side resort. They are used by the European manager of the Chinese railways and also in Africa, whilst recently we have
received an unsolicited order for Beale Horizontal Grands to be prepared ror the largest hotel in Europe, which is now
approaching completion. Of this latter, further particulars will be advertised, with illustrations.
Quality for quality you will make a big saving in price.
Buying direct from the makers you save customs duty and middlemen’s profits.
You get a longer-lasting piano and twenty five years’ actual guarantee.
Your - Beale Piano will have a better touch consequent upon the high-class Action.
The sound boards are warranted not to crack or lose their camber, hence a violin-like results, producing the
tone for which our pianos are everywhere famous.
If your Beale Piano should at any time meet with an accident or misfortune we have all parts always ready so that
you can rely upon its perfect restoration.. You can only be sure of this with the actual makers.
Where easy terms are required we issue a EREE EIEE POLICY, so that in case of death the instrument becomes
the property of the customer without any further payment, thus removing anxiety :u time of trouble.
The Beale Factories are the largest of their kind in the British Empire and are under the direction of one of the
world’s greatest experts, Major Geopge D. Rose.

“John P.”: A steeplechase, which as an
hilarious spectacle far out-did anything ever imag-
ined by bush bards, happened at Invercargill (M.L.)
on April 2, during the Autumn Meeting of the local
racing club. It was a 2J miles drunk, and there
were seven starters. At the finish Northern was
the only horse standing up, so he cantered leisurely
home and captured first money. That was Act 1.
One of the fallen horses, Forget-me-not, that had
tumbled in front of the stand the last time round
and half killed its rider and lost its bridle, was
peacefully eating grass on the course ; so its trainer,
J. Kirkwood, dashed out of the crowd, jumped over
the fence, and, springing on to his nag’s back, rode
it without a bridle over the last four fences. This
fine feat of horsemanship was loudly cheered, and
as, by the blessing of Providence, the impromptu
jockey managed to draw the exact weight required,
Forget-me-not landed second money. Two other
horses, Corso and Kelp (also eating grass), were
then caught by their partly-damaged riders, and
rushed once round the course, getting past the post
third and fourth. That was about the end of Act
11. When the excitement subsided and the judge
and officials began to get things sorted out, they
reckoned that the third and fourth horses had dropped
a round in the general mix-up and therefore hadn’t
completed the course. In the bird-cage, where by
that time both horses had been unsaddled, there was
a sultry argument between jockeys and owners, and
charges of embezzlement and bigamy flew around.
Kelp’s jockey, with a view to completing the course
and grabbing third money, began frantically to re-
saddle his moke. But McKenzie, the damaged pilot
of Corso, had better sense. He grabbed his saddle,
sprang on to his undressed steed, rushed out on to
the course and gave another dashing display of
horesmanship by racing round the course over six
stiff fences, carrying his saddle on his left arm. He
was closely pursued by Kelp, whose rider, on a
partly girthed saddle, was sliding about all over his
mount. Corso had too big a start, however, and
finally landed third money by four lengths. That
was Act 111. The owner of Northern could, of
course, have claimed all the stake money, but he was
a good sport, and said he reckoned Kirkland and
McKenzie had more than earned the cash for second
and third places. Then everyone went and had a
drink, or bought a new pair of boots, or something.
“Bakblox”: Re “Hamer’s” par anent the
duties of station bookkeepers (B. 2/4/T4). Here
are some of 'the jobs that I take on, as a matter of
course: Driving an engine, sawing timber, black-
smithing, carpentering, keeping drays, poison carts,
etc., in order, looking after windmills and pumps,
also telephones and lines, making and hanging gates,
cleaning out bore drains, cooking for musterers and
assisting at lamb-marking. I also look after the
store. But my real job, inasmuch as that is the only
one for which I get paid, is to keep the station
books. There is no bookkeepers’ union, so it comes
cheaper for the boss to call me that than to pay on
the basis of an engine-driver, or
telephone expert or cook.

“Boro-Belle”: A profitable use has been
found for the lawyer vine, which grows in such
abundance in North Queensland scrubs. A Townsville
furniture maker, having observed the large quantity
of bamboo furniture, wicker and cane ware imported
from China and Japan, decided to experiment with
the Queensland article, and the result is good. The
solicitor vine, through being solid, is stronger than
the bamboo, will not split on being put together is
equally pliable, takes on beautiful stains, and is more
lasting than the bamboo. It lends itself to all sorts
of light, artistic furniture, at a price much below the
imported bamboo goods. Thus a new industry is
opened up in North Queensland. Previously' the
only use made of the barrister or judge vine was in
the making of ships’ fenders.
