“Lagani-Namo”: A residence can be built
in the tropics out of coral nearly as rapidly as with
galvanised iron. The densest of it can be hewn with
a tomahawk, or sawn. Such a house is delightfully
cool and hygienic, and will outlast many renewals
of the iron. There is a chaste little coral chapel in
these Southern Isles that charms even the ungodly.
The walls are grey-white blocks 12in. x 12in. x 36in.
Seats have been hewn in sections and exquisitely in-
laid with iridescent shell. The floor is a cement of
the calcined coral. The crisp, umber, sago-leaf thatch
picked out in stars with silvery pandanus leaf, gives
a spick and span daintiness impossible to describe.
Go there some Sunday morning at 10 and you’ll see
the native pastor, in surplice and stole, expounding
the gospel to his brown-skinned flock from a neat
needlewood-and-cedar pulpit bracketed on one side
of the spacious chancel, where is also a fairy altar
of opalescent mother-of-pearl. This architectural gem
is set in a cocoanut grove just back of a glistening
beach.

“J. 0.8.” : There is not sufficient material
available at present to settle the argument about the
date of the prickly pear’s first appearance in Aus-
tralia. Before 1840 people began to scowl at it. It
was disrespectfully known in and about Sydney many
years previously. There is no evidence that it came
here with the First Fleet, though
that is possible. Phillip tried
to take with him a bit of everything
he found at any port where he touched,
and as he touched at Rio, where the
pest abounded, though not regarded
there as a pest, he perhaps carried a
sample of it along. Its history, so far
as records have been deciphered, be-
gins in 1839. In that year a Dr.
Carlisle took to Scone (N.S.W.) a
prickly-pear plant in a pot as a
garden ornament. Mr. J. 11. Maiden
agrees that Scone is the spot from
which the pest has radiated to all
parts of Australia. Dr. Carlisle’s pot
plant spread noticeably from there to
northern N.S.W. and over the south
and west of Queensland. It is reason-
able to suppose that had it been in-
troduced by Phillip it would have
proved its baleful propagating powers in the 50
years preceding Dr. Carlisle’s experiment. The
truth may be that some American ship brought it
along in the ’2o’s or ’3o’s. The overhauling of old
manuscripts now going on may throw new light on
the matter.
“Cork”: While wending my way home the
other evening, and in the vicinity of the New-
market (Vic.) cattle yards, I came upon a crowd
of excited people and stopped to investigate. I was
informed that a bovine of the male religion had
become suddenly insane, and after making charges in
various directions had taken up a strong position
in the yard of a neighboring hotel. Observing a
coatless man running in haste, and carrying a rifle
in his hand, I ran after him into the hotel yard
in time for the following tragedy. Standing in an
open shed, and facing the crowd, was brother Bull,
as harmless a looking quadruped as ever I beheld.
The man with the gun, after a hurried consultation,
crept forward to a pile of wood about 20 yards
from the alleged lunatic beef, and resting his weapon
on a log, took aim and fired. A wild scamper by
the crowd (including the man with the gun), but
Brother Bull remained in the same position, with a
look of quiet contemplation beneath his horns. The
rifleman, after taking breath, again crept forward
and repeated the dose. The bull remained as inno-
cent looking as ever. Twioe again the assault was
repeated, and on the fourth round the victim wagged
a broken leg and stumbled forward. Another sport
then came forward and offered to try his skill, but
the ammunition was exhausted. The scene ended
by a man dressed like a waiter arriving with a
knife, and surgically operating at the base of the
animal’s horns. I am informed that the shootist
is a prominent member of a rifle club.' Surely even
the most prominent member should be able to hit a
bull, however insane, at 20yds.
EFFICACY OF PRAYER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Good Brother Veal down on his bones
Put up a mighty prayer.
His quaint, insistent thunder tones
Smote on the summer air
Like great drums smit with mammoth shanks—
Drums big as thousand-gallon tanks.
A Cornish Wesleyan was Veal,
Who prayed before the Lord
With eloquence and sweating zeal,
And all day long he roared.
The Methodists of Teddy’s Plain
Had put him on to pray for rain.
He prayed that day, and all the next
With valor fine prayed he.
The silence of the sky was vexed
With his audacious plea.
“O Lord!” he cried, and cried again
“Ah Lord, send down Thy precious rain!”
“It is no good,” an elder sighed.
“Come up, man, and away.”
But Veal he shook his head and cried:
“If rain don’t come to-day,
To prove m,v theories are true
I'll pray and pray until she do!”
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.
The Real Lumper and the Dubious One.
Dear Bulletin, — An element of the chronic
wharf-labor trouble appears to lie in the
superabundance of wharf laborers. The pay
has now been fixed by Justice Higgins at
Is. 9d. per hour during the day ; time-and-a-
half (which is apparently 2s. 7id. per hour)
between 5 p.m. and midnight; and double
time (apparently 3s. 6d. per hour) between
midnight and the date when the rooster gets
up and tears the silence apart with its melody.
This remuneration seems something above
the average for toil that can hardly be called
skilled, and it is probably a world’s record.
The work is as abundant as a very inefficient
tariff and a crowd of hard-borrowing Govern-
ments can make it. No country in the world
provides more wharf-labor in proportion to
population than Australia does. At times it
is a very laborious way of making a crust, but
it is no harder than a lot of agricultural em-
ployment. It isn’t so uncertain as it used to
be, for the windjammer and the casual steam
tramp are less in evidence than of old, and
the ship that runs to a time-table (and keeps
to its time-table a great deal better than the
Sydney suburban train or the cattle truck
that brings cows from Monaro) becomes
more and more frequent. Also it isn’t half
so uncertain as shearing or harvesting or
Murrumbidgee whaling. But there are too
many men to share the profits, and every in-
crease of wages seems to make the competitors
more numerous, consequently there is never
enough remuneration to go round. One explan-
ation is that the honest professional wharf-
laborer is swamped to a considerable extent
by the dishonest amateur one. The hulking
loafer or semi-criminal, who feels that the
eye of the police is always liable to fall on
him, can’t carry round, in rebuttal of a
charge of having “no lawful visble,” a certifi-
cate of membership in the Shearers’ Union or
Seamen’s Union, or the Rural Workers, be-
cause people don’t shear or pervade the sea
or do rural work in the city. He can’t pass
himself off as a bishop unless he has more
than average intelligence, for a bishop has to
bisli with a certain amount of publicity. Nor
can he exhibit a ticket as a union carpenter
or bootmaker or bricklayer or stonemason
or even as a member of the Australian
Journalists’ Association as proof of his bona
fides. In all these walks of industry men get
jobs of some continuance, and if a man can’t
show that he ever acquired a job of continu-
ance and had sufficient skill to keep it, his
claim to be a working citizen is blown
out. But a man can be a nominal wharf-
laborer even though ho observes no regular
hours, has no steady billet, has no desire for
a steady billet, and only wharf-labors for a
while when he feels that his status is be-
coming precarious in the eyes of John Hopp.
The hulking loafer must cease to hulk now
and then in self-defence, and as wharf-labor
is the least provable of lawful occupations he
affects that. Therefore the honest, legitimate
wharf toiler has to share his industry with
far too many casuals or occasionals. The
alleged “wharfie” who yearns for a strike as
an explanation of why he is back at his old
corner and leaning against his old post is a
constant source of trouble to the union. It is
quite possible for the bogus lumper who
doesn’t lump to hold a meeting and start a
violent industrial dispute while the real
lumper is away lumping like a good citizen.
Nobody has yet devised an adequate remedy
for these troubles, but presumably one will be
found in course of time. The wharf-men may
console themselves with the knowledge that
other industries also have their “fringe,”
though it may not be quite sb wide a one.
If a. derelict owns a faded tail coat and
some traces of grammar he is always liable
to call himself “journalist.” And when
Flossie has been roped in after a good up-
standing fight with three policemen she has a
lamentable habit of setting down her occupa-
tion as “dressmaker.” —Yours etc., Solomon
Malaprop.
COMPULSORY SATISFACTION.
It may be interesting to state that every day in
every gaol in New South Wales one prisoner is ap-
pointed a delegate to test the bread, meat, vegetables,
tobacco, or any other supplies which come in. He
tests the weights and has to be satisfied as to the
quality of the supplies.— Sydney. D. T.

satisfied as to the quality of the supplies?”
PRISONER: “Well, it's this way, Mister, I
dashed well HAS to be!”

a he water waggon is still leading the way
m Maoriland. and the procession of the
boozy and the blithered swells prodigiously.
The drink bill for 1913 amounted to
£4,137,653, which is ahead of the previous
year’s figures by £56,491. There is a de-
creased expenditure of llld. per head of
population compared with 1912, but, all con-
sidered, the no-license system of inculcating
sobriety in Maoriland is about due to take
the count. It was started in 1896, when
Clutha went “dry,” and that year, on paper,
each of the inhabitants was credited with
£2 19s. Sid. worth of liquor. Last year,
when the books were made up, although there
were no less than 12 so-called “dry” dis-
tricts, the cost of alcoholic irrigation per
throat was £3 14s. Similarly, the year be-
tnre the Great Fraud was commenced, the
convicted “drunk” was merely 6.60 per 1000
of the community. Now he is 11.88 numer-
ous. Since the swindle got going the known
absorption of beer has leaped from 5,138,170
gallons to 10,012,000 gallons, and the addi-
tional disappearance of whisky is the differ-
ence of 441.338 gallons. Still, the people
who support the system profess to be satis-
fied with it, and if the brewers and spirit
merchants have any bricks to hurl at it this
paper hasn’t heard any sounds of the fray.
“Whitehall” about a man of girth
The measure of High Commissioner man's maun
m the United Kingdom can best be gained, not. hy
amiable blither at banquets and the like, but by a
consideration of the attitude towards him of those
in authority. Judged by that standard Reid is
a superlative comedian ; but a person who is by no
means to be taken seriously when weighty discus-
sion is afoot. Recent developments in connection
with the New Hebrides question serve to put the
Commonwealth’s deplorable representative precisely
where he belongs in the opinion of British statesmen.
France and Britain recently appointed Commissioners
to discuss and settle the future of the New Hebrides.
It is possible that, as the outcome of their delibera-
tions, France may take the New Hebrides outright;
or the islands may be handed over, for a considera-
tion, to the United Kingdom ; or the Condominium
may be abolished and a totally different form of
government substituted ; or any one of a dozen other
vital changes may be made. Whatever happens in
this connection is of profound importance to Aus-
tralia. Practically the whole of the New Hebrides
trade is Australian; and many of the local settlers,
business men and missionaries are Australians. Yet
the other day, when Sir ICinloch Cooke asked in
the House of Commons if Australia was to be repre-
sented on the Commission the Minister said “No.”
And no surprise appears to have been evinced at his
answer either. If there is one man in London who,
by virtue of his position, should have a powerful
say in the disposition of the New Hebrides, that man
is the High Commissioner for the Commonwealth.
But Australia’s High Commissioner is quite useless
at that sort of job. He is a notable flaneur, but
work is out of his line. They know it in London
and carefully refrain, accordingly, from asking of
him duties which he is notoriously unwilling to
attempt, and incompetent to perform if he did
attempt them. So Reid goes placidly on, making
after-dinner speeches about the size of iiis waist, and
Australia remains, in all the real essentials, without
a representative in London.
—■
A cablegram from London states that a
certain Sir George Macalpine lately ad-
dressed the Baptist Missionary Society on
the fading of the caste system in India. He
reckons that when caste is finished there
will be a great boom among the Christian
Churches all over old. mouldy, mysterious
Hindostan. Certainly caste is disappear-
ing, and when it goes there will probably he
exciting events. For many centuries a great,
strange, miserable country has been divided
into social or religious grades of the most
wonderful kind. The highest-caste man,
who was few, would sooner see all Mind
trampled under foot by foreign enemies
than drink out of the pot that had been
drunk out of by the lowest-cast" man, who
was numerous. The lowest-caste man didn’t
care much what foreign enemy came along,
provided he knocked the head off the highest-
caste man. The highest-caste man didn’t
fight, as a rule. He was too darned import-
ant. The lowest-caste man wasn't allowed to
tight, and didn’t want to. The two men
who are of no value in war are Dives and
Lazarus —Dives because he is too fat and
Lazarus because he is too thin and depressed
and covered with too many sores and licked
by too many dogs. India, in the days
when it struggled for itself, had a separate
warrior caste. It was characteristic of
the place that a man wasn’t even allowed
to defend his country unless he belonged to
the clan or race or sect or religion or church
of hereditary defenders. The warriors were
too numerous in time of peace and not suffi-
ciently plentiful in time of war. So the
Mohammedans, and after them the Europeans,
among whom any man was a soldier when
required, got these Hindus down and sat on
their numerous brown ear. Now caste is
getting on to its death-bed, and no one knows
for certain what will happen, hut everybody
is afraid of what will happen, when that
crippling influence is no more. But it
isn’t the Christian religion that is killing it,
nor is there the slightest reason to suppose
that its finish will boom Christianity, any
more than the extinction of the feudal sys-
tem in France boomed Buddhism. Caste
is passing out by reason of railways and
cheap, third- and fourth-class tickets. The
Hindu lias a craving after travel. For the
sake of going somewhere, and seeing the
place, and coming back, he will pollute him-
self by sitting beside the Unclean and sleep-
ing beside the Unspeakable. When caste
is finished the locomotive will be the god of
India. And the nigger is learning to drive
it.
'“Jeames” —his tale:—
According to the cable:—
“Landless cottars have seized the Isle of Taransay,
west of Lewis, in the Hebrides, belonging to Lord
Dunmore.”
This is a popular trick with crofters on the west
coast of Scotland. In fact, it is (he only hope that
most of them have of getting hold of a bit of land,
and so evading pauperdom. I was in Skye once, in
my youth, when a band of crofters arrived and
settled down doggedly on a sort of peninsula. It
looked capable of carrying about one Australian sheep
to five acres. Yet they started building huts and
tilling the soil. The Oovernment sent a gunboat up
to remonstrate, but the interlopers refused to be
shifted, and, so far as I know, they are there to this
day.
The rural toiler of Australia would get the shock of
his life were he to spend a week as a Western
Hebrides agriculturist. Taransay, for example, is
inhabited by people who live not in houses, but in
cabins, which are in no respect more comfortable
than caves. There is not a tree on the island—-a
characteristic which it shares with Nortii and South
Uist and other places thereabouts. On the reclaimed
hogland of the lower lying parts the natives raise
a few weedy fowls and cultivate a little corn and
potatoes. On the hills they run a sheep or two, and
illicitly distil whisky for the purposes of Sabbath
revel rv. At one town near which 1 abided every
summer for some years the whole of the inhabitants
got unobtrusively blithered every Sunday. On the
other hand, bad any Southron dared to fish on that
holy day he would certairdy have been boycotted, and
possibly assaulted, as a cynical atheist.
The heather-clad hills of Taransay abound with
grouse and snipe; and duck, teal, Brent and Bernicle
geese are plentiful in the lochs which one encounters
at everv turn. However, the natives aren’t allowed
in kill them, nor could they if they wanted to, having
no guns Tiicr hands and feet are gnarled with
rheumatism to an incredible extent, and they lock
very, very old at 50. For these troubles the climate and
hard work are to blame. These people have always
impressed me as the verv poorest white folks or.
earth—in the sense that‘they have fewest of the
amenities of life. The white man has a much better
time at Cairns or Port Darwin.
The Earl of Dunmore, who owns the island, .was
Lord Fincastle till a few years ago. He won the
V;C. while acting as war correspondent in the Afghan-
istan Frontier War in 1897. Hi 3 father was the
eccentric, one-eyed nobleman who speculated a good
deal in Australia a couple of decades back.

I rom London : W. IT. G. Nation is dead.
lle was a wealthy Devonshire squire who
came to London for three or four months of
the year, took any theatre that happened to
be vacant, got together a scratch company
and put on one of his own numerous plays.
He never gave any other dramatist a chance.

at intervals. After the name of almost
every character were the words in paren-
theses. “with song by W. H. G. Nation.”
In this way his name frequently figured a
dozen or fifteen times on each playbill. He

Government Notices.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, 30th April, 1914.
Appointment of Junior Clerks to Govern-
ment Small Arms Factory, Lithgow,
Under Section 63 (1) and (2), Defence
Act, 1903-1912.
APPLICATIONS are invited for appoint-
ment as Junior Clerks, Small Arms
Factory, Lithgow, New South Wales. Appli-
cants must not be less than 16 nor more than
IS years of age. Salary on appointment, 160
per annum, but a successful applicant ap-
pointed from a locality outside Lithgow may,
in addition to the salary provided, be granted
a living allowance to bring the total remun-
eration up to £126 per annum. Such allow-
ance to cease when, by subdivisional incre-
ments, the actual salary has reached £126
per annum.
Application forms and full particulars
may be obtained by applying to the Secre-
tary, Department of Defence, Melbourne ; the
Military Commandant, Victoria Barracks,
Paddington, New South Wales; or the Man-
ager, Small Arms Factory, Lithgow, New
South Wales. Applications must be lodged
with the Secretary, Department of Defence,
Melbourne, not later than 25th May, 1914.
S. A. PETHEBRIDGE.
Secretary.

THE IRISH QUESTION. (Re-
printed from the December number ot
The Round Table.) With a fore-
word by the Rt. Hon. Sir Horace
Plunkett, K.C.V.O. Svo, Sewed, Gd.
“I think it will be agreed that the writer
has done a real service in making a compre-
hensive survey of the political events which
have led np to the present crisis, and in giving
English and Irish readers alike a calm and
dispassionate picture of the opposite points ot
view from which these events are regarded. It
matters not whether we agree or disagree with
the conclusions at which he ultimately arrives;
and his constructive helpfulness is not lessened
by the fact that we may conjecture how he
would vote if he were in active politics, me
value of his work lies mainly in the fairness
with which he presents the opinions which he
does not approve, and makes us feel that he
has honestly faced the facts and hidden none
of them from his readers... .It seems to me
that this essay has succeeded in presenting the
older and the newer Home Rule questions in
true perspective, and I am glad of an oppor-
tunity of saying so.” Horace 1 lunkett.

THE CONNOISSEUR’S FAVORITE.
POLITICAL
POINTS
A Washerwomen’s Union is about to be
formed in Melbourne. It is to be under the
guidance of Willie Hughes, as boss of the
Waterside Workers.
The Home Ride majority in Ulster doesn’t
Uiake so much fuss as the Tory minority, but
it occasionally does things. On a recent day
it wandered like a person walking in his
sleep to one of Carson’s drill-halls, and
knocked all the stock of illegal rifles into
pieces, and threw the fragments into the
road. There were no speeches and no ad-
vertisement only smash and departure.
Good news has arrived for the poor old
country cow which dies in the truck on the
long railway trip to Sydney. The express
train lias first right to the railroad ; then the
mail train; then the ordinary way-side pas-
senger train; then the mixed train; then
certain fast goods trains; and then conies the
tram with the hungry, thirsty cow. So there
is still hope for the animal, and if it is so.
ungrateful as to be dead on arrival the other
animal will fetch so much the more, and the
price of beef will go up again,.
LATEST' NATIONAL PASTIME.
It is gratifying to know that the Sydney
bakers strike is “off.” It is also humorous
to remember that the Arbitration Court had
given a judgment in the matter, and that
nobody cared one solitary half-cuss about
the Court or the judgment. In fact the
poor, obsolete Court wasn’t even mentioned.
Mexico is thankful these days—as it has
been often before—that it didn’t place any
of its great cities on the coast after the
Sydney and Melbourne fashion. When the
guns of the U.S. warships had battered down
the defences of Yera Cruz 1000 marines were
able to occupy the place. But it is a long
walk from there to Puebla and Mexico city
&nd Guadalajara and San Luis Potosi, and
the Yankee ships can’t climb hills.
It is always pleasant to hear that Uncle
►Sam and Father Bull are brothers, and that
mud (or is it blood?) is thicker than water,
and so on. But seeing that Bull and Samuel
have been banging around in an arbitration
court about the matter of a vessel which, it
is said, was illegally seized 102 years ago,
the friendship appears to be worn pretty thin.
Cordial acquaintances who can remember a
twopenny grievance all that time are almost
as bad as enemies.
One. more row has happened in never-quiet
Albania, where there is no compulsory mili-
tary training and no reliable army. Certain
angry f«lk of Epirus, where Pyrrhus used to
hang out, crucified 200 Albanians in a church
and cut off the fingers or hands of all the
children they captured. According to the
theories of our anti-Defence people, nobody
will ever molest us if we are courteous, .iust
and inoffensive. So doubtless these children
had hit the bulky Epirotes in the stomach,
or something of the sort.
ACCIDENTAL.
Mr. Cook: It seems to me we will yet have to take
over your railways altogether.
—Prime Minister Cook to State Premiers.
AUSTRALIA : "That's a good note, Joe; must
have been a fluke surely."
“The Crying Need for Population” was the
heading of a recent article in Sydney Tele-
graph. The need is visible to the naked eye.
-But unhappily: (1) Australia, by reason of
its silly semi-Freetrade ass of a tariff, is, in
proportion to population, the greatest import-
ing country in the world, and as things can’t
be both made and imported, there isn’t much
woik for artisans; (2) so much money is
being_ borrowed to pay for imports that the
debt is the biggest per head on earth, and
the population is half-inclined to get away
to avoid the interest bill; (3) the farmer has
to sell his wheat to artisans in Europe in-
stead of to artisans right at his door, and
the freight charges make him feel discour-
aged.
Gilruth, the horse-doctor who runs the
Northern Territory, urges that the place
should get anew name, and
proposes “Kingsland.” It is
a very poor sort of name.
AVliy not call the whole place
Darwin? Still, Kingsland,
had as it is, would be better
than the present designation.
A casual curse of Australia
is the State names. A
patriot might die for England
or Ireland or France or
Spain, uttering the name of
his country with his last
breath. But if he fell shot
tn the heart on the battle-
field, he simply couldn't say
that he perished gladly for
the Northern Territory of
South Australia.
A deceased Adelaide merchant left a fund
to pay for the delivery of one lecture at the
local university every second year, which
seems a long while between drinks. This
year the lecture was on transportation, and
the lecturer mentioned that the cost of hump-
ing a ton of goods one mile on the private
railways of England was lid., on the State
lauways of S.A. nearly Id., and on the pri-
vate railways of the United States less than
2>d. It looks a good advt. for U.S pri-
vate enterprise, but then in the U.S. the fine
old capitalist used to. get such enormous land
giants and cash subsidies that in many cases
he really received his railway as a present
tiom the patient community. One company
had a surplus left after building its line out
of Government grants, and built part of
another line with what remained of the steal
f. n< l th e n . did another steal. Still it called
itself Private Enterprise.
Miss Beatrice Grimshaw writes:—
I wonder is it generally known in Australia that
free land, or what amounts to it, is not yet a
thing of the past in Papua? It is true that you
must pay survey fees amounting to £2O per 100
acres, if you take up a large holding. l!ut you can
take up soil not exceeding 100 acres absolutely free,
for 10 years, the Government surveying it at no
cost to the holder. After 10 years, a rent of Sd.
an acre may be demanded, and, after 20, it may
mount to the figure of 6d. or Is. the acre, or it
may not. But it can never be more, during the
99 years’ lease that the settler is given. Improve-
ment conditions are not heavy. Within two years
the settler must make a commencement of some sort,
and within five, if he has taken up a 100-acre area,’
he must have 20 acres cleared. It costs £5 an
acre to clear light timber, and £7 to clear heavy,
according to the rates at present prevailing. The
best land is the heavy-timber country. There is
nothing in the range of tropical productions that
Papua will not grow just a little better than other
countries will. Rainfall is constant and heavy, and
you can pick your annual fall within a very wide
range, as suits yourself, since there is land to be
liad of every kind. There are good prospects in
Papua for the small man, if he is the right kind,
but not otherwise. He must live on his place, and
oversee his own work; he must put up with a good
deal of loneliness and want of amusement, and he
must not drink. He will not suffer from fever to
any extent worth mentioning, if he feeds and lives
conformably. It is true that the qualities that make
a successful settler in Papua would also ensure suc-
cess nearer home, but New Guinea enjoys the great
am antage of a climate that can be relied on, absence
oi hurricanes and droughts, and liberal, constant
ram. The projected railway from Port Moresby
to Sapphire Creek, a distance of about 20 miles, will
make a great difference to landholders in the sur-
rounding country, much of which is still available
for taking up. The land lying eight to 10 miles
from the terminus of the railway, however, is much
better than that through which the line will run
since the former is in the wet district, and carries
the heavy timber, which the drier land near Port
Moresby cannot support. Still, there are some plan-
tations within _lO miles of the capital—growing
tobacco, fruit, sisal hemp—and some of them are pay-
ing well enough. Cocoanut land is available along a
very large portion of the coasts, though not close
to Port Moresby. A settler who can do his own
overseeing, and even part of his own building, etc.,
may make a start on a few hundred pounds, and get
through all right. Full information as to the best
products to grow, supply of labor, and so on, may
bo had in Port Moresby. Men trained to town life
would be better advised to try elsewhere. Papua
is still in the rough more or less, and the undoubted
advantages of free land and reliable climate have to
be paid for in other ways.
Last week (to be precise, Saturday, May
saw the end of the two years period
which had to elapse, under the Parliament
Act, before the King could give his assent
to the Home Rule Bill. The Home Rule Bill
is being debated for the third time, in the
Commons, at the moment of writing. It will
presently, as sure as eggs are hen-fruit, he
eontumeliously flung out of the Lords for the
third time. Since the 9th inst., nothing has
stood in the way of the King giving his
assent to it. So whatever trouble is brewing
may be expected quite shortly. Of course, by
the way, the two-years business applies not
only to the Home Rule Bill, but to all legis-
lation under the Parliament Act—Welsh Dis-
establishment, Plural Voting and all the rest
ot the contentious measures which render the
honest British Tory well nigh epileptic every
time he thinks about them.

animal in command, it says it reckons Port
Adelaide can be made one of the finest ports
in the world. Has Peake discovered that he
was wrong (in which case he should, of
course, resign), or is he merely the usual
humbug that the party system breeds?

OUR THEORETICAL SAFETY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Times, commenting on the Pacific naval ques-
tion, says that from the Admiralty’s point of view
the change from the 1909 agreement was doubtless
completely demonstrable on account of the strain of
armaments. “The abstract proposition,” adds the
journal, that the safety of the Dominions depends oh
Great Britain’s strength in the North Sea is an im-
palpable consolation contrasted with battle ship? sta-
tioned in their own waters.”
The little ships were on the sea,
We saw them all a-row,
And wondered what their game might bo,
Till they began to throw
Their shells. A score or so
Fell on us ere we wired our fear:
“John Bull, the Japanese are here.”
His answer early was and bright:
“The German Ocean is all right.”
The yellow men in swarms came down,
With rifles in their hands.
They swept us from the blighted town,
They drove us from the lands.
We fled in tattered bands.
Our leaders sent one message more:
“The Japanese are all ashore.”
And John Bull answered: “That may be,
But you’re safe—in the Northern Sea.”
Invaders bold took all we had.
They tore our flag to shreds.
Our women’s fate was rather bad,
And we who kept our heads,
Hid high in mountain sheds,
Sent out a wireless cross the seas:
“Australia now is Japanese!”
The answer was: “On the North Sea’s swell
The Empire’s safe, and all is well!”

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The return mill between Hugliie Mehegan
of Australia, arid Milburn Saylor, of the U.S.A., was
a tremendous display which on Saturday night last
drew the largest crowd Sydney Stadium has
seen for some time. Mehegan was the national hero,
and he was there to redeem his cause. Through the
dark masses of packed humanity round the ring
rolled a wave of telepathic anxiety. Would Mehegan
be once more the Mehegan of old, and with a shower
of body punches beat down and out this hard-hitting,
aggressive Yankee fighter? Immediately the fight
started it was apparent that all Nature was hostile to
such a probability, and the stars in their courses
fought against it. God had been good to Saylor
and given him a longer reach, superior hitting-
power and greater speed. Mehegan had pluck and
endurance. If all his endurance had been put on a
freight train and despatched to Melbourne it would
have blocked the line for three weeks. Both were
in perfect condition. Mehegan’s weight 9st. 91b. to
Saylor’s 9.st. 6flb. Only perfect condition couid
have kept the scrap going as long as it went, for
Saylor was in a hurry. Apparently he wanted to
finish the fight and go and get married or confirmed
or something. Mehegan kept attacking, and as he
went in Saylor met him with long-range swings.
His reach suggested a wireless telegjram. But
Hugliie broke through the barrier of natural advan-
tages and lambasted Savior about the body so that
lie was glad to hang on for surcease, repose or
philosophical reflection. But that was only occa-
sionally. For the most part Hughie was a reel-
ing force. Saylor fought what, for him, was a
remarkably fair battle. But, all the same, he
succeeded in rousing the ire of the press and the
public on more than one occasion. Punishment has
an embittering effect on Saylor. He goes on the
howl, and it is then he does those things which are
certain, if persisted in, to end his brilliant career.
In the 11th, Mehegan got to close quarters and un-
loaded a last, desperate effort. But it exhausted him,
and then Saylor came like a storm. Mehegan went
down, but rose again at eight, and once more Savior
rushed him. Down again, and the gong ended the
round. Immediately Meheean came out the aggressive
Saylor set full sail across the ring and hit in a large,
general way, and again Mehegan went down. Then
the police rose majestically, and at the stroke of
nine issued its protocol saying, “Let there be peace!”
and there was no more war.
The scribes of the Sydney Evening News,
the Sund.av Timks and The Bulletin have, from time
to time addressed Saylor severely on the subject of
his method of fighting. On Monday last these pre-
sumptuous scribes were severely castigated in Sydney
Sun by Mr. W. F. Corbett, who blew off in great
style. Mr. Corbett defended Saylor’s methods He
was expected to by all parties concerned. Saylor is
a United States boxer, and ever since he fell under
the fascinating influence of the charming Noah Brusso,
Mr. Corbett has been unable to see any wrong in a
Yankee boxer, or much good in an Australian one.
If Australian boxers are flattened out by occipital
blows delivered with the wrist or the heel of the
palm, serve the swine right. They shouldn’t have
left their necks in the road. This is the kernel of
the argument which Mr. Corbett puts forward under
many cross-headings, such as “NOT A FOUL
PUNCHER” ; “SAYLOR FALSELY ACCUSED,” and
“A GREAT FIGHTER DISCREDITED.” If Mr.
Corbett has failed to see Saylor land other blows than
the occipital one with the heel of his palm; if he
has not observed the same young man, no doubt
through carelessness, uppercut his opponents with
his bare forearm, then he is just as unable to see
as he infers his fellow scribes are unable to think.
* * * *
Although football in Sydney kicked off in
a mutilating mood it has a long way to travel
before the Yankee record is in danger of being
eclipsed. In the 13 seasons ending with 1913 the
casualty list worked out 234 mortalities and 2603
injuries. Yet they talk about the brutality of
boxing!
* * * *
Judging by Saturday’s exhibition the
Rugby League’s First-grade teams are already
divided into sheep and goats. The clubs that won,
won easily and handsomely, the ones that went under,
excepting Glebe, were hopelesslv outclassed. Just
consider the list:—-
South Sydney beat Glebe by nine to two.
Eastern Suburbs downed Western by 10 to two.
Newtown defeated Annandale by 13 to three"
North Sydney scrunched Balmain by 24 to three.
The only struggle that had much evenness about it
was the dust-up between South Sydney and Glebe
at tlie Sydney Cricket Ground in the presence of
25,000 enthusiastic barrackers. Play started bril-
liantly, and was kept so. But South Sydney was
altogether too nippy for its ancient rival. South’s
forward pack sunk its own individuality and con-
tented itself with heeling out, and then the South-
ern back machine got opportunities, and the rest
was smash and wreckage of Glebe in spite of a fero-
cious struggle. The way that superb back machine
juggled with the ball was beautiful to see. The rest
of the matches were merely the trampling down of
weak teams by stronger ones. North Sydney gave
a magnificent exposition of how to do the job with
Balmain for an illustrative subject. Still, there is
no reason why the defeated teams should not pull up
somewhat. That they were short of the practice
that makes for perfect handling of the leather at
high speed was the chief trouble with them. The
trouble is, of course, that the victorious clubs will
get so long a lead that there will not be time to
overhaul them.
* e * »
The same deplorable circumstance was
noticeable in the Union game, to a lesser degree.
University smashed up North Sydney by 22 to 15.
Randwick did for Newtown by 18 to 11—an amaz-
ing circumstance considering Randwick is a new
club and Newtown a hefty combination , with a
mighty past behind it. Glebe kicked the stuffing out
of Western Suburbs by 15 to four. Balmain wal-
loned Manly by 14 to eight Eastern Suburbs and
South Sydney struggled grimly to a draw with 1C
each. St. George had the bye. None of the games
were worthy of comment. They were all strenuous
enough, but none of them showed the bright, spark-
ling quality that catches the public bob.
The bogging in of Melbourne’s League toc-
ballers proceeded apace on Saturday, to the gratifica-
tion of several thousand disinterested spectators and
the annoyance of bigoted barrackers. The largest
congregation transported itself to the little ground
at Fitzroy to watch South perforin against the pre-
miers. Neither side had been properly tested this
season, but public opinion reckoned that “ ’Roy
would about do it.” As usual, pub. op. knew little,
as the Red-and-Whites started off at a pace that got
them a ten-point lead, and were never afterwards
headed, winning by eight points—74 to ’Roy’s 66.
On the whole, the play was too congested; both
sides were too eager to prevent the “other bloke”
from getting going to try their own systems
under way, and ’Roy made the mistake of persistently
playing to its star forward, although he was too
closely watched to allow of effective play. The
improved Essendon 18 went to St. Kilda and per-
formed a considerable and to some people aston-
ishing feat by capsizing its opponents by 69 points
to 52. After trouncing Richmond and wearing down
Carlton by sheer persistence the Saints reckoned they
“had the Dons as a gift,” but the latter set a
pace the Saints couldn't follow and kept it up till
the bell rang. Nevertheless both sides favored in-
dividual excellence, and a strong, concerted team, like
South, should wipe out both. ’Wood was at home
to Richmond and pleased its partisans—and few
others-—by demolishing it holus bolus, 90 points to
36 being the record. Carlton utterly spiflicated and
scarified the Students with a score of 102 points
to 32 ; also Geelong stamped on Melbourne with a
tally of 74 points to 58, but as the Seasiders were
on their own ground and Melbourne had to dress
while sprinting from the station to the ground—
the Railway Department’s express being late as usual
—this performance was nothing to “skite” about.
The Melbourne Ass. games likewise furnished a
couple of shocks, Essendon going out to ’Scray
and paralysing the premiers by its speed and dash.
Latter made a valiant effort to pull up towards the
close, but when the bell rang were still 12 points
to the bad —33 to ’Don’s 45. The hitherto unde-
feated ’Townites accumulated a similar handful of
points against Brunswick, which conglomeration
simultaneously raked in 54, and so raised itself a
step on the ladder. The two tony suburbs of Brigh-
ton and Haw-haw-thorn indulged in a discourteous
and undignified scramble, from which Robinson Snow-
balls’ constituents emerged with the less damage—.
101 points to Hawthorn’s 41. North took Northcote
by the slack of its deficiency, so to speak, and strewed
it over the paddock and jumoed on it and left it
demoralised and almost moribund. North 1,25 points,
’Cote 16, was the scorer's disgusted statement. Also,
P ran attacked Port with vigor and the strong boot
and notched 62 points to Port’s 34. There was a
time when Port would have wiped its feet on P’ran
with the casualness of a householder cleaning his
boots on his doormat; but those days are not.
* * * *
Through the week the League investiga-
tion committee took out its axe and buried it, in a
manner of speaking, in the person of a Carlton
bailer, for mistaking an opponent’s ribs for the
ball. It disqualified him for three long months.
It wasn’t a very great offence, so the committee is
evidently out for gore, especially as it explained to
the culprit that it had “let him down lightly because
lie was a young player and had been accidentally
kicked beforehand.” Certain ill-regulated ’bailers
will lie wise to inwardly digest these remarks.
The chief features of the N.S.W. r. Vic.
tennis match were: (1) the awful hiding Victoria
received—lB rubbers, 38 sets, 287 games, to three
rubbers, 12 sets, 172 games; and (2) the unmerci-
ful way that A. B. Jones ladled out the acid to
his _ opponents. His two singles matches were
against Rod Heath (several times Davis Cup rep.)
and A. Campbell, and on each occasion he won 6—2,
6—o, or 24 games to four. In the doubles with
Clarke, who was very weak at times, he won six
sets and lost one. If an all-round performance like
that won’t _ convince the Davis Cup selectors that
they are doing Jones an injustice, nothing ever will.
The youthful Jimmy Anderson did pretty well. He
beat Campbell 6—2, 6—2, but lost to Heath 2—6,
6 —;4, 4—6, though his win in the second set was
mainly due to several faulty decisions. In the
doubles with Marsh, , who played a splendid game, he
won one rubber and lost one. Patterson, the Vic.
colt, didn’t show up too well, but it’s pretty evi-
dent that he will make a big impression soon. In
the ladies’ section the Mrs. Ford-Miss Stewart match
lasted 21 hours, and was won by Mrs. Ford by
11—9, 9 —7. Naturally both ladies were quite
done up, so the play was anything but of a high
order. Miss Cozens put up the best show for the
Ma State. It was her first inter-State match here,
and she was the only N.S.W. lady to take both
rubbers. The Victorian ladies won the match by a
very small margin.
After waiting with a patience that knocked
Job’s record kite high, the Australian Board of
Control was roused from its slumbers last week by
the news that the guarantee demanded for the
despatch of an Australian XI to S’Africa for the
triangular contest had been accepted. The Board
demanded £2OO each for 13 players and a manager,
and the acceptance by the S’African Union throws
the Board into a state of electric activity. The
Australian team will leave here during the first week
in November, and its programme will show five
tests and 20 other fixtures. The selectors are
Clem Hill and L. O. S. Poidevin, and E. Bean, of
Melbourne, will have the charge of sifting the
players to make the trip.
J. N. Crawford, of S.A., is being dragged
to Otago (M.L.) in an awful hurry ; but there is a
reason behind the indecent and expensive haste. One
of the laws governing cricket in the Borrowing Isles
saith that a man must reside six months in a province
before he is eligible to play for it. Hence Otago’s
frantic desire to get Crawford to cast his luggage
ashore in the Scawtch metropolis without delay, so
that he’ll be available to go in pursuit of the Plunket
Shield next season. That bauble is really the whole
root of the importing mania, as far as cricketers are
concerned.
The Dunlop 100 INliles Tennis Relay Road
Test (what an awful title!) is causing excitement.
As will be remembered, North Sydney Cycling Chib’s
team beat all records and annexed the boodle. But
Granville came along with a protest. It was assever-
ated that the Northern team did not comply with the
condition that no pacing should be allowed. In fact,
it stated that all North Sydney went out on its bikes,
its motor-bike and its side attachment and its dog
and took the team along. At time of writing the
Dunlop Co. is investigating with a dazed expression
and a clouded brow. But even then the cup was not
full. Some blunder took place in cabling from
Maoriland P. O’Shea’s 10-mile relay, and his time
was gijen as 20min. 42 2-ssec. It should have been
two (minutes more, so that prize passes from O'Shea
to the well-known Sydney rider, G. H. Horder.
He covered the distance in 21min. 50sec. But as
Horder is a member of the North Sydney team, which
is accused of having been paced like the Devil, the
question whether Harder or O’Shea gets the prize
depends upon the verdict in the previous case.

as the. bunny.
Cyclist F. White, who started out to
break F. Birtles’s record of 31 days three hours 15
minutes from Fremantle to Sydney, was forced to
abandon his ride at Coleraine, owing to heavy floods
on the Victorian border. Leaving Fremantle on
March 23, White established a new record to Cool-
gardic in two days, 22£ hours, as against the pre-
vious best of three days 10 hours. Widgemooltha
was reached at 7.15 the same evening. Attempting
to take a short cut between Widgemooltha and
Fraser’s Range, he lost one day through the track
being washed away, and he trard toride right back to
Widgemooltha (what a name!), thence to Norseman
and on through heavy rains from Fraser’s Range and
Balladonia. The trip was uneventful until he
reached Denial Bay, where he lost another day fool-
ing over a short cut which proved unridable and
forced him to go back for forage. Near Ililtaba
cattle station he had to walk 25 miles, owing to
heavy sand. From Eucla to Port Augusta he ran
through an old-man drought, but had cool and
cloudy weather, which helped him a-lot. From
Port Augusta, through Adelaide, down to Miningie
the roads were good and fast times were established ;
but at Miningie horrible weather caught him and
some of the wind squalls were worse than earthquakes.
The black-soil country around Narracoorte and on to
the Western District of Victoria broke him right up,
till, at Coleraine, which he swam into, he burst his
boiler and abandoned the attempt, as he had no
hope of equalling the existing record. In the latter
stages he had to dismount every few yards to scrape
the mud from his wheels before he could progress.
But, all the same, White intends to claim the
Unpaced Record between Fremantle and Adelaide,
as the previous holder had the advantage of motor
pacing on portions of the journey and cycle pacing
on other stretches. He finished in excellent fettle
and weighed 10st. 81b. when he arrived at Cole-
raine, as against lOst. 51b. when he left Fremantle
on his Davies-Franklin bike shod with Continental
Tyres.*
When M. Guillaux steered the first hydro-
aeroplane over Sydney Harbor, with its owner, Mr.
Lebbeus Hordern, as a passenger, he laid the founda-
tions of a hew sport. Mr. Hordern himself is learn-
ing to manage his own hydro-aero, and there is not
much doubt he is the pioneer. In another year or
so they will be thick over the harbor and surround-
ing landscape on Saturdays and Sundays. The hydro
is probably just as dangerous as the land aeropiane,
but it does not seem so, because it can take refuge
in the great deep. If you find your machine doing
a perish and can choose where you will drop, it
seems safer to plunk into water than to hit hard
rock. The hydro-acro was hovering over Sydney
and the harbor most of last Sunday, and it sug-
gested a new sort of insect on a giganitc scale.
Also, it reminded one somewhat of a dragon fly.
The hydro-aero is a biplane. Soaring through the
sunlight between the blue sky and the blue harbor
it emanated an atmosphere of serenity and reposeful
power. Even its infernal clatter was the far-off
hum of a drowsy insect. During one of the flights
a newspaper man was carried over the “unpro-
tected decks’’ of our battleship. He was appalled
to think of the damage that could be done with
a bomb or two, he wrote. Rubbish! Also, Rats!
All the damage any bombs that hvdro-aero could
carry, although it is an up-to-date Maurice Farman
military machine, is about nil. The armor of
the ship is strong enough to resist great projec-
tiles, charged with high explosives hurled at the
velocity of 1500 ft. or 2000 ft. per second. But the
average man has a very exaggerated idea of the
destructiveness of the hand-thrown bomb.
That Scotchbyterian Synod, or Confer-
ence, or whatever it is that drafted a report on box-
ing and kindred evils started out with a declaration
that it recognised that the art of self-defence is “a
useful and possibly necessary accomplishment,” and
that against “boxing” as an athletic exercise practised
by amateurs the committee had nothing to say. Which
shows what an ignorant crowd the . Scotchbvterian
parsons are as regards “boxing.” If you want to
see blood splashed around, and hunks of skin and
hair knocked off, go to an amateur competition—for
preference, one run by the muscular Christians who
attend some Band of Hope or Christian Association
connected with some church. They are the boys
who put up gory scraps. And, also, they are the
boys not trained and carefully attuned to "stand pun-
ishment. The professional is rarely in the rin"
without being in proper condition, "and he knows
from considerable experience how to take good care
of himself. Occasionally a professional gets reallv
damaged, but very rarely. The amateur is always
throwing himself headlong into trouble.
The Ma State Rink Championship gath-
ered in the limelight of bowlsdom in Svdnev on
Saturday, when the brethren from Maitland lasted
long enough to overthrow Chatswood in the final
by 24 points to 20. It was a tussle worthy of the
title, and the success of the band that had travelled
over 100 miles Was a pleasant variation of the
usual order of things. Chatswood had its revenge
though, in the “pairs” when it cleaned up the
sward with Dubbo bv 20 points to seven.
The feature of Sydney Lacrosse last Satur-
day was the consistent, business-like wallopings un-
graciously presented to those who fell in battle that
awful afternoon. The great struggle was that
between Eastern Suburbs and Sydney. Last year's
premier club came out right on’ ton' of Sydney bv
eight goals to two. The other disturbance ended
with St. George falling before the rush of Mosman
b.v 12 goals to two, while in other corners of the
congested metropolis Petersham rubbed Manly in
the gutter, by 11 goals to two, and Balmain jig-
gered University by 11 goals to one.
N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union started on its long
grind of winter road-racing on Saturday last, when
three heats of the City Club’s nine-miles' open handi-
cap were whirled off on the Western Main Hoad
starting and finishing at the Sherwood Council Cham-
bers. The day was ideal, and the road, marvellous
to relate, in good order. Over 150 riders went in
pursuit of plate for the suburban sideboard, and the
first 10 riders home, and the bloque who made the
fastest time, will he allowed to take part in the
final dash for the crockery or ironmongery. This
takes place next Saturday over the same course. it
is safe to bet the course will not be in anything like
so good order as it was last week. It never is.
Eiders were in a good fettle, and the finishes were
desperate. Also, the times were fast. The absolutely
fastest time of the day went to E. W. Pedersen,
champion last year, who scuffled over the nine miles
in 22min. 28sec., which is travelling somewhat.
There’s evidently going to be an awful struggle next
Saturday.
•T. B. Clarkson, who started out from Syd-
ney to ride to Melbourne in a cycle-car, had, at time
of writing, demonstrated that the little machine was
quite capable of long-distance journeys. And there
was never any very grave doubt about it. A man
can walk from Sydney to Melbourne, and a wheel-
barrow can be pushed ; so there was no reason why
the little cycle-car should not chug-chug its way. But
the point was, of course, whether or not an enjoyable
trip could he made in such a contraption. Mr. Clark-
son says it can, notwithstanding the fact that he did
have one capsize. He may, of course, be full of con-
cealed emotion and basely trying to put a good face
on a bad job. Ono never knows.
The Adelaide Cup Carnival at Morphett-
ville (S.A.) saw a good day’s sport marred somewhat
by the death of an ex-Sydney horse, The Reckoning,
in the Welter Handicap. He collided with another
noble steed, fell, and broke his shoulder so badly that
he had to be destroyed. However, most clouds have a
silver lining, and this race was brightened immensely
by the fact that the second nag, Lady Pistol, paid
only 14s. less than did the winner Alraf—and lie
cleaned up for £4 14s. for each £1 invested. The
parties who backed the second horse in the First
Hurdles were even better off —they drew £2 Bs. to
the 325. collected by the backers of the winner.
Jockey W. 11. McLachlan came to grief
on Saturday last at Tattersall’s ltandwick meeting.
F. Hickey, rider of Maltcliester, complained that
Mack had interfered with him, and the stipendiaries,
after investigation, agreed with Hickey, and handed
McLachlan three months holiday. McLachlan was
shortly to have gone to Queensland to ride for E. J.
"Watt, and generally was regarded as being on the
very crest of the wave of prosperity. His name was
mentioned #in connection with some crowding at
Rosehill when Smith was killed, hut the witness
who charged him with crowding was careful to exon-
erate McLachlan and explain that he was merely part
of a general movement started by parties unknown
on the outside. But it was the straight griffin to
avoid even the appearance of evil, let alone the thing
itself, for some time to come. McLachlan didn’t, and
now he is in the tureen.

“Strangler”: Buttan Singh, the ancient
turkey-legged Indian who tricked Clarence Weber
and other hefty ’rastlers hereabouts, has returned to
Australia after some years in Fogland and elsewhere.
The ancient Buttan doesn’t look a day older than
when he upset Gus Eenuert at Melbourne Athletic
Club. He mentioned with an air of satisfaction that
“I come back this country.” A yarn went the
rounds of athletic circles that when Buttan left Aus-
tralia the ship he was on touched a rock off the
W.A. coast. It was evidently a serious matter, as
Buttan flopped down on his turkey trots and swore
several swores that he’d never return to Australia
if he got out of the fix. But he reached the “old
dart” safely and then commenced a grappling cam-
paign, in which he ate up arid threw down all the
best men at his weight that promoters and various
sports could unearth, and in every instance gave
years and years away. The athletic and physical-
culture journals proclaimed him equal to anjrihing of
his tonnage on earth. In a recent article written for
“Bleeds” Fitchett’s Life Storky Adams says that
Buttan Singh is the cleverest wrestler seen in action
in Australia during recent years. Adams also men-
tions that George Hackenschmidt wasn’t the greatest
wrestler of his time, hut owed his success to the
clever management of one Cochrane, a London show-
man. Hackenschmidt once threw Adams through
the cabin door of a ship, but Storky same back
and told Hackey he was a large fool who had cer-
tainly lost his nut. The Russian subsequently poured
forth regrets on Storky’s shoulder.
That now-famous 12-cylinder Sunbeam car
has broken out in a fresh place and beaten all records
in the 60-h.p. class, with 47.7-h.p. A half-mile was
achieved, from a flying start, in 15i03sec., which
means 119.6 miles per hour—a regular dazzler. This
was 10 miles faster than the previous best. Later
on, the mile was covered in 29.82-ec., equal to
120.73 miles per hour. Chassaigne, the well-known
French driver, steered the car.
THE UNION PUNCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A movement to form a Boxers' Union is on foot.
“Sparring partners’’ and “preliminary” lighters are
ttie initiators of it. —News item.
Well, strike me, ain’t it bloomin’ time?
The blankv blank top-notcher pinks
. ’ls man and gets ’is thousand; we,
As you might term it, spar for drinks.
It’s fair enough to make a bloque
Sick iv the game to see a chap
Get ’undreds for his end, when yew
Git fives an’ give a better scrap.
What is the sparring partner’s booze?
Fizz? Aw, fergit it. Tray-bit ’ock.
What does he earn ? A blanky crust,
To be some White ’Opc’s chopping block !
You see the blankers swank eround
In dandy clothes and nobby ’ats.
We is the workers at the game,
And they’re the pluto-dashed-old-crats.
An’ now we’re up against the crowd
That gits the glory and the tin:
We’ll fight ’em by Judge Higgins’s rules,
And on me oath we’ll fight all ini
No longer, down-trod workers, we
Will rest in our blanked ’umble state;
We’ve found the punch that gits the cash ;
Maybe we’ll git a piece-work rate.
Labor’s the crowd as ’as the punch,
The hit, the footwork and the length;
We’ll show the blanky blighters soon
’Tis union that is blanky strength.

The Cult of Beauty
A RARE COLLECTION OF BEAUTY
HINTS FROM NEAR AND FAR.—
HOME RECIPES
How to Have Thick and
Pretty Hair.
“Home Talents.”
Soaps and artificial shampoos ruin many beautiful
heads of hair. Few people know that a teaspoonful
of good stallax dissolved in a cup of hot water has
a natural affinity for the hair and makes the most
delightful shampoo imaginable. It leaves the hair
brilliant, soft and wavy, cleanses the scalp com-
pletely and greatly stimulates the hair growth. The
only drawback is that stallax seems rather expensive.
It comes to the chemist only in sealed packages,
which retail at half a crown. However, as this is
sufficient for 25 or 30 shampoos, it really works out
very cheaply in the end. *** For an actual hair-grower
nothing equals pure boranium. It is quite harmless,
and sets the hair roots tingling with new life. *** The
use of rouge is almost always obvious, but powdered
colliandum gives a perfectly natural color and defies
detection.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous
Hair.
“Home Science.”
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths of super-
fi.:oi.s hair wish to know not merely how to tem-
porarily remove the hair, but how to kill the hair
roots permanently. For this purpose pure powdered
pheminol may be applied directly to the objectionable
hair growth. The recommended treatment is designed
not only to instantly remove the hair, but also to
actually kill the roots so that the growth will not
return. About an ounce of pheminol, obtainable
from the chemist, should be sufficient, *** For com-
plexions inclined to be oily many smart women are
now using just the natural allaeite of orange blos-
soms. It is perfectly harmless, makes a capital
greaseless cream which holds the powder perfectly
and does not encourage the growth of hair. *** Dis-
agreeable body odors can be instantly neutralised b.v
the occasional application of a little powdered
(white) pcrgol to the affected surfaces.
Using Oxygen for the
Complexion.
“Chemistry at Home.”
The well-known characteristics of oxygen may be
effectively applied to the renovation of the com-
plexion. Mercolised wax, such as may be found at
any chemist’s, contains oxygen which is released
directly the wax comes in contact with the skin.
Oxygen consumes waste matter in the body, but does
not affect healthy tissues at all. Therefore, it
attacks and removes the deadened waste accumula-
tions on the skin which show in the disfiguring form
of sallowness, moth patches and a general appear-
ance of lifelessness. The fresh young skin which
has been obscured by this disfiguring veil of waste
matter is thus allowed to show forth in all its
healthy beauty. The mercolised wax is absolutely
harmless, and indeed very beneficial to the skin. *** To
bring a natural red color to the lips, rub them with
a soft stick of prolaetum. *** For tired, hot or per-
spiring feet use a teaspoonful of powdered onalite in
a foot-bath.
Emergency Complexions.
“Novel Recipes.”
Even a complexion which is hopelessly dull or
greasy can be made beautiful in a moment by a
perfectly harmless home recipe. If you have no
eleminite in the house, get about an ounce from
your chemist, and add only enough water to dis-
solve it. Apply a little to the face with the finger
tips. The process defies detection, and the result
is an immediate appearance of velvety, youthful
“bloom,” which every woman desires. The effect
will last all day under the most trying conditions,
indoors or out, and renders powdering quite un-
necessary. This simple substance is also very good
for the skin, and no possible harm can come from its
regular use. *** To make the eyelashes grow long,
dark and curling, apply a little mennaline with the
finger tips occasionally. It is absolutely harmless,
and beautifies the eyebrows as well. *** Pilenta soap
is the most satisfactory for all Complexions. It even
works well in cold or hard water.

Business, Bobbery, etc.
“G.V.W.”: There is more than the average amount
of acidity in the latest trade or trust scrap in Fog-
land. Eighteen months ago the vinegar factories,
with one exception, came to a horrible understand-
ing, and put up the price of vinegar from SM. to
7d. per gallon. In spite of sour remarks, the “black-
leg” acid factory continued to sell at Id. a gallon
les3 than the combine. The combine accordingly
started to dump casks of vinegar on the public door-
step at 2Jd. per gallon, and in one week dumped
2000 cask’s in London alone at that price. The
public is getting cheap acetic acid just now, and
when the “blackleg” factory is killed or surrenders
it will still be getting acetic acid, but not cheap.
It will have to pay 9d. for a while to make up the
loss.
“ll.I.O.”: When “0.K.” talks (B. 23/4/’l4) of
Madame Oaillaux’s Use of a revolver in the Calmette
murder he talks through his hat, and when he
praises the accuracy of that effete weapon —well, he
simply gibbers. In reality, the modern revolver is
only a little more dangerous than a hand-propelled
brick. As one who has used firearms of all sorts, I
say the revolver, with its appalling “windage” due
to its peculiar construction, is a fraud and a delu-
sion as an “arm of precision.” To make the thing
hit anything with certainty at a range of over 25yds.
a lifetime’s attention is needed. Madame Caillaux,
like a sensible assassin, used an automatic pistol.
With any good make of these weapons, gaslight, as
a rifle and far lighter and handier, even an utter
novice can make fair shooting at ranges far exceed-
ing the revolver’s capacity. To point a small “auto”
correctly is no more difficult than to point a fore-
finger. “Throw off,” that additional curse of revolver
shooting, is absent in the pistol, while there can be
no comparison in rapidity of fire. The “gunman”
all the world over has long ago discarded the revol-
ver in favor of the automatic, and many European
armies have followed suit.
* * * *
Trades-people in Auckland (M.L.) were rather
upset at the end of the recent Easter holidays by the
discovery that some person or persons unknown had
been scattering round forged £lO notes. The notes
were a splendid imitation, and the fact that the
banks would not be open for three days helped a
lot in keeping the scheme quiet. A fair number
of the paper frauds were passed into the totalisator
at the races, and a good many sorry punters were
heard from later in the week. About 120 notes have
so far come to light, so £l2OO, less distributing
expenses, is not a bad haul. The suggestion has
been brought forward that the bank whose name was
taken in vain should pay the notes, but the insti-
tution is preserving a bitter silence on the subject.
“Billy B.”: There’s a new business holding out
its maw in Australia, and its visiting card bears the
device, “Australian Photoplay Co.” It has been
formed “with the object of instructing people how
to write picture-plays, and to show them how to
market their work to the best advantage.” Its cir-
cular bucks up prospective clients with the informa-
tion that there are over 10.000 shows requiring 12
new pictures weekly, and that good plots with an
Australian setting are being continually called for.
Also, “there is only one correct way of writing a
picture-play, to realise its proper value. This way we
teach, and our correspondence course consists of eight
lessons.” The course costs £5 ss. Personally, I
wouldn't mind parting with a fiver in return for
being made a successful photoplay writer with a
clamoring market, but what I don’t understand is
why these “experienced photo-plavwrights” who
are running the joint are wasting their time scratch-
ing round for pupils instead of reaching for fame, and
fortune, and limelight, and Sunday-paper interviews,
and posters, and free drinks and sundries.
* * * *
A recent Melbourne conference of municipal repre-
sentatives discovered, with a sharp stab of astonish-
ment, that even if a weights-and-measures or other
inspector saw a butcher, for instance, giving light
weight to customers, he could not prosecute. So an
amendment of the law is to be sought. The idea is
to provide that all goods shall be sold by net weight
—as butter, jam, etc., under the Pure Foods Act
and that inspectors shall have power to enter, seize
and prosecute when called for. More “interference
with the liberty of the subject.”
“Croson”: I observe with sorrow that the Watt
Government is throwing away more good capital : a
comparative trifle, certainly, but it all counts. The
wastage is in connection with its fourth egg-laving
competition at Burnley. The three previous tests
have demonstrated the fact that the White Leghorn
pullet is a resolute layer—a veritable poultry-yard
bookie—but that she is of little use for anything
else. Now, man cannot live bv eggs alone. And
the White Leghorn doesn’t provide a decent meal for
a mouse. Also it is necessary that hens shall go
“ducky” occasionally, otherwise the breed is doomed
to extinction But the excitable white fowl has no
time to “cluck.” On the other hand, the Black Orping-
ton lays in moderation : sits at the proper season ; and
two or three of her will furnish a meal for a healthy
wharf-laborer. If the Agricultural Department wants
to discover really useful information, why doesn’t it
find out the market values of the eggs, the breeding
capacities and the dinner-providing possibilities of the
various breeds, and publish them? As it is, various
pens of White Leghorn pullets are herded together
for a year, fed on a fixed, regular, monotonous diet,
and encouraged to devote themselves entirely to the
production of eggs. At the end of the 12 months the
overwrought birds will be mere frameworks, bare
eggshells, so to speak. Meanwhile, as everybody is
cultivating White Leghorns, this country is liable
to lose the run of the Minorca, the Andalusian, the
Plymouth Brother, the quaint Iloudan and the old
and reliable Barndoor, all birds with established
reputations in several useful directions. I would
fain see a society or something formed, having for its
object the preservation of these grand old fowls.
The most frankly ironic charity fund in the wide
world, perhaps, is the Bentinck Benevolent Fund foi
the relief of needy racehorse trainers and jockeys.
Seventy vears ago a horse called Running Rein won
the English Derby, but was disqualified when it was
proved to be a “rung-in” 4-year-old. Lord George
Bentinck was the sport who undertook the job of
exposing the swindle, and a number of other spoils
(including grateful backers of the gee that came in
second and got the stakes) subscribed a testimonial ot
£3ooo' for the energetic turf scavenger. He refused
the money, so the Jockey Club took it for the
nucleus of the Bentinck Memorial I und aforesaid.
Indigent ex-trainers and jockeys are preserved fiom
destitution through the fact that a trainer and joc y
of yore connived in an attempt to work a lovely
swindle.

Only Electric Power
used in this Mine!
The Christopher Coal Co., Christopher,
111., U.S.A., is using Electric Power
from two 750 kw. Curtis Turbines for
every mining operation. Coal' is cut,
hauled, hoisted, screened and loaded,
water is pumped and air exhausted
entirely by power from motors fur-
nished by the General Electric Co.
(U.S.A.). The mine is lighted
electrically, and its power controlled
by a G.E. Switchboard. This is but
another instance of the appreciation
which mine owners are showing in
money-saving electrical equipments
suggested bv G.E. mine experts.
Largest Ilgner System
Hoist in America!
The double drum hoist in use by the
Christopher Coal Co., U.S.A., has 7ft.
drums designed for a useful load of
90001 b. in a car and cage weighing
11,0001 b. additional. It is guaranteed
to make 1000 trips' from 600 ft. depth
in 7 hours. The hoist is driven by
an llOOh.p. 550 v. Direct Current G.E.
Motor. Power is supplied by a fly
wheel Motor Generator set consisting
of a 650h.p. G.E. Induction Motor
and an 800 kw. G.E. Generator with
a 17k.w. G.E. Exciter. The efficiency
of this hoist is high, and an automatic
rheostat equalises the fluctuations of
the hoisting load. Our Coal Mine
Bulletin, No. 4668, will be sent you
on request.

FREE ELECTRICITY.
Once installed, THE AUTOMATIC WIND MOTOR can be run
practically for nothing: it is driven by the wind and requires
NO FUEL whatever to generate electricity. There is no
Smell or Dirt, NO RISK from Fire, Shock or Explosion.
The power generated will safely light your home in the most modern, convenient and healthy
manner.
The heating of dwelling rooms is thereby reduced to a minimum, and the soiling and air-
vitiating effects of gas, oil and other illuminants is obviated, and, further, the fire risk is nil.
Electric fans can be driven to render hot summer days more bearable, and free the home
from flies, mosquitoes and other winged pests.
The domestic problem is solved by the furnishing of cheap power for vacuum cleaners,
electric irons, washing machines, etc.
A small freezing machine, capable of turning out ten or, in the larger sizes, a proportion-
ately greater number of pounds of ice per hour, can be driven. This feature in itself should
commend the plant to those who are forced to reside in the hotter districts of Australia, and, in
combination with the furnishing of electric light, will do much to give the country dweller some
of those comforts only at present enjoyed by the city man.
The larger plants can be utilised for driving shearing-machines, chaff-cutters, churns,
pumps and other machines where moderate powers are intermittently required.
Man> pumping problems otherwise impossible of solution by the ordinary windmill pump
can be solved by the Automatic \Y ind Motor, since it can be placed in the most exposed position,
and the power generated transmitted by wires to a pump driven by an electric motor.
We shall be pleased at all times to advise prospective clients as to the size of plant best
suited to their individual requirements, or to explain any doubtful points.

Prom London: Kdith Craig, daughter of
your visitor, Mien Terry, is making ' a good
.thing out of . stage-production and ■ coaching. She
played small parts under Henry Irving from the time
she was a schoolgirl, a nd'learned'the whole trade. “1
cannot act,” she told an interviewer latelj-. “But
I can make others act.” An odd sort of memorial
is-the joint one, just unveiled by George Alexander in
Shoreditch Church (city of London), to a Latch of
old-time actors buried there: Richard Burbage arid
Tarlton, the original Hamlet and First Gravedigger:
the elder Burbage, manager "of the Globe, and others.
The tablet: is dedicated by the usual quaint conven-
tion, “To (lie Glory of God.” Among alleged revues,
the latest intelligent and refined title is “Splash Me!”
A curious, between-tlie-lines advt. issued by the
Palace management about their crack male Russian
dancer: “M. Nijinsky was unfortunately ill for three
nights. In the opinion of the management this break
was detrimental to the success of the engagement, and
consequently they exercised their option under the
contract, and terminated it.” Exit Nijinsky.
Oscar Asche’s revival of “Kismet” found one at
least of the London critics in a critical mood. The
Remrej: man wrote: ‘*l,.think JTajj and the....rest,
of the people of Baghdad might have.walked less in
public with advantage to the play, or, at any' rate,
if it were necessary., for us to witness their peram-
bulations in order that we might calm down between
each of Hajj’s succulent villainies, they might at
least have not always gone for the same walk. One
got tired of seeing the same passers-by | parading
before the same" drab wall,'with’its same, dark' arch-
way...:! note a tendency to over-elaboration. The
whole play has become distended with superfluous
‘atmosphere,’ and is too long for the small amount
of-drama that it carries. IP is rather like putting an"
airship to carry a hammock... .pictorially, too, the
freshness of the play seems to have faded a little.
Since ‘Kismet' first showed’the (lancing- world the
pietuvesqueness and freedom of the Persian ‘dress,
ladies of the harem, sheiks and caliphs have Uttered
Hie floor at every fancy-dress ball. All Suburbia
has a yashmak and every clerk his turban.”
The extension of the show girl movement in
London —due to the rise of the sort of revue which
is mostly unclothed show-girl—has brought about a
renaissance of the “masher" of-quarter of a century
hack. According to the evidence in a recent assault
case, young men in evening dress block the stage
entrances to many of the variety shows that' call'
themselves revues, musical comedies, or what not.
Daly’s in particular constitutes one of the .most re-
markable midnight free shows in London. There,
regardless of ridicule, congregate armies of. shining
youths. They are waiting to take girls somewhere .to
Supper. Apparently, there is no law to prevent this
sort of thing, although it amounts to an obstruction
of the 'traffic.' The metropolitan’ magistrates re-
gard it with disfavor. Anyhow, when one of. the
unhappy young men got punched on the nose by an
attendant for not “moving on” as directed, the Bench
declined to fine the puncher. All it made him pay
was the doctor’s bill for repairs done to the wounded
dude.
Wilding, the champion, has been teaching Maxine
Elliott how to make-the most of her splendid phy-
sique in tennistry. Playing at Cannes lately,
Wilding had the beauteous Maxine for his partner in
a mixed double. Wilding is a bachelor, and the lady
is understood to be husbandless at present, on account
of a divorce or death in the family. So the gossip-
mongers are mortgering. '
A French dramatist, one Feydeau, insists on being'
allowed to choose his own east for a play, and he has
the same system that Gregan McMahon, with nar-
rower scope for working it, applies to casting of the
Melbourne Repertory Theatre plays. Feydeau be-
lieves that physical suitability for a part should be
an actor or actress’s first recommendation, without
regard to the stage experience of the chosen one.
For a .comedy lie will produce at the Theatre Michel
he has engaged a banker’s clerk to -play a leading-
part because the novice looks like the man he wants
represented. Being quite innocent of stage experi-
ence, the bank clerk will have to be. taught to do
pretty much as the author suggests, for in Paris an
author is allowed almost as much authority as W. S.
Gilbert asserted in coaching the cast for a comic
opera. If the raw amateur has good ability he will
lie shaped into a fair representation of the’.character
the author conceived, which is just what the average
old professional “creation” of a character is not.
It is this system of picking his com pa ny on their
physical suitability for their respective jobs that
enables Gregan McMahon to get satisfactory results
from his Repertory Theatre work. Sometimes, as in
the ease of the recent “Rutherford and Soil” produc-
tion, he succeeds particularly well with a very diffi-
cult job. For that matter the Repertory perform-
ance of “Man and Superman” was more convincing,
on the male side of the cast, than Julius Knight’s
treatment of it, nor is there any professional co.
touring in Australia- that could do “The Doctor’s
Dilemma” as effectively as McMahon’s selected mem-
bers of the Repertory Cos. did it.

WHY YOUR STOMACH HURTS.
A DOCTOR’S COMMON-SENSE ADVICE.
Pain in the stomach, variously called in-
digestion, dyspepsia, flatulence, heartburn,
stomach-ache, etc., is usually attributed to
some unnatural, abnormal or diseased condi-
tion of the stomach itself. Nothing, how-
ever, could be farther from the truth. Nine-
teen times out of twenty the stomach is
absolutely healthy and normal, the pain and
discomfort being entirely due to the acidity
and fermentation of food which irritates and
distends the stomach ; although if this condi-
tion is allowed to run on. in time the con-
stant irritation of the acid is likely to eat
into the stomach walls and produce ulcers
and sometimes cancer of the stomach. Medi-
cine is useless in such a case. The acid
and fermenting food must be removed by a
stomach pump or an emetic, or you must
neutralise the acid and stop the fermentation
by talking, half a teaspoonful of bisurated
magnesia in a little w r ater. This latter is
by far the simpler and safer method. Bisur-
ated magnesia almost immediately neu-
tralises the dangerous acid and by correcting
the food contents enables even a tired, weak
stomach to digest almost any food without
any difficulty. Physicians advise that bisur-
ated magnesia should be kept in every home,
and a little taken after each meal whenever
the slightest tendency to food fermentation is
shown. Be sure to obtain bisurated mag-
nesia, as other forms of magnesia are valu-
able as tooth and mouth washes, bpt they do
not give, satisfactory results in , stomach
disorders.* ; V-
