IN BANANA LAND
“Polstan”: At Boulia (W.Q.) there are
phenomena in the natural water-supply line,
and I would like to know why. Small
basins in solid rock are full of clear water
even when the weather is as dry as the rock
that Moses struck before Moses struck it.
The basins are very small, but as they are,
like the widow who was begged for food by
Elijah, practically inexhaustible, their size
doesn t matter much. Empty one, and the
water at once begins to rise again. The
rock, is apparently solid, and the little wells
don t overflow. I think they are replenished
by subterranean loans at 4ti per cent.
“Binnacle's” ray of light:—
Wireless telegraphy, which has proved its enormous
value to shipping, received a hearty and spontaneous
curse a few days ago. The big Pacific mail liner
Siberia, trading to Japan, was calling up the Aus-
man Lloyd steamer Persia, off the coast of Formosa.
The Persia s call is M. 0.5., but a shore station, inter-
cepting the signals, read them as 5.0.5., the univer-
sal call for assistance. The news that the Siberia
was crying out for help was flashed to London, to
the consternation of the relatives of hundreds of pas-
sengers and of the insurance brokers. The first
began to develop hysterics, the second to gamble in
re-insuranees. When a breathless cable arrived to
cry that the call S.O.S. had ceased the re-insurance
rate jumped to 50 guineas per cent. It was
assumed that the big ship had gone to David Jones.
The next message was to the effect that the Siberia
had arrived at Manila amid much applause and aston-
ishment. The brokers and gamblers who burnt their
fingers over insurances are a very wild lot, and revi-
sion of the Morse code of signals is suggested. But
who can devise a code of signals that some fool won’t
mistake, or a horse that will never bolt, or a man
who will never fall off a horse, or a gun that won’t
miss fire some day, or a stair that no person will fall
down ?
The results of the foolishness of a State
of 670,000 square miles being allegedly gov-
erned by a town poked away in the south-
east corner are coming home to roost in
Bananaland. The size of the State alone
forbids capable management from one town,
but when that one town suffers as badly
from the centralisation selfishness as almost
any other town in Australia—which is say-
ing much—any remote chance of capable
management vanisheth like the north wind
or the south beer or some such fleeting epi-
sode. In the last 10 years—although pre-
vious to that the south had more mileage
than the rest of the State put together—
<6o miles of line have been opened in the
south, and only 260 miles in the centre, and
520 in the north; while at present there
are under reconstruction 142 miles in the
south, 63 in the centre, and 96 in the north.
Also, some of the best agricultural land in
the State is to. be found in the north and
the centre, but it is held in large parcels by
influential men, whom the Government refuse
to disturb. The way the farmer is encour-
aged to go north can be seen bv the follow-
ing figures. In the last 15 years 5i million
acres of agricultural land have been thrown
open in the south,, while the centre gnd the
north have had less than one million acres
each opened. About 20 vears ago the neg-
lected parts of the State gathered sufficient
energy to agitate foy subdivision, but the
influence of the south was too great. Since
then they have been content to remain neg-
lected. To this writer, Bananaland should
be split up into six States (not three, as is
the general inclination), three coastal and
three inland; the inland States to centre on
Cunnamulla, Longreach and Hughenden say.
It is the lack of inland States that keeps
the population to the coast, and the wisdom
of small States with one powerful executive
has been proved. Incidentally, the super-
fluous royal representative and a hundred
other useless expenses could be abolished
ond the money diverted to something bene-
ficial. Moreover, the time is ripe.
The Parting.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was nothing tragic about our parting,
A jest was our summer’s epitaph;
lou were swinging a brief-bag to and fro
* n big brown hand as you turned to go,
And I was trying to laugh.
A newsboy, hawking his evening wares,
Was urging us both to buy;
But you shook your head in a moment’s
frown
As he clattered the dusty stairway down
With his shrilly huckster cry. '
The silence fell. I was swift to break it
In silence sometimes, two souls have met —
fEye looks in eye and a challenge flings!)
And yet, to-day, ’tis the unsaid things
I wish that I could forget!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, May 11, 1914.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
The races at Ascot last Saturday were
badly attended, and did not entice any bril-
liant frocking. On Monday, however, the
usual fashionables fluttered about the lawn
and handed each other racing information
calculated to send the recipients tottering to
ruin. His Ex. and Lady Macgregor ar-
rived unexpectedly, everybody who thought
of the matter (and there were fully half a
dozen) believing them to be at the Eight-
hour Sports. The committee of that enter-
tainment, it appears, forgot to send the
usual invitation, for which, doubtless, the
Chiel blessed them. Invitations to the press
were, by the same far-seeing citizens, con-
fined to one out of the four dailies. So
there were quite a lot of happy people in
Brisbane on Saturday.
Dixie Newton, who fled hence in March,
glanced into India before going on to
Europe. After calling in at Paris and a
few other places she packed her swag for
Wales, where she is saying with the Yerney
RESPICT AV COORT.
WAYBACK CITIZEN (fined £1 for drunkenness): “Look here , Mick , a quid's too much.’
HOGAN, JAY PAY : “ Soilence, sor! Ye may call me Mick out ov coort, but here ye will
plase rimimber O'im yer blessed Worship."
clan, a daughter of which is her sister-in-
law. Before long Miss Newton will flit
north to the Land of Haggis, with her golf
sticks. I don’t know a better way to
tackle haggis.
Elaine Feez, daughter of the Brisbane
lawyer, has just finished up a successful con-
cert tour in Germany, with a pianoforte
cital at Leipzig. Her pet composer is
Beethoven, and German audiences rise en-
thusiastically and murmur ‘‘Hie” or
“Hock,” or something like that, whenever
she puts him on the menu. Lambrino, her
master, has promised her a career, and in
the breach-of-promise line he is without a
stain on his character.
The Eniversity Women’s Club, a haughty
institution with an aeroplane variety of
nose, as it were, climbed into its nicest
evening dress and slippers the other evening
and did the polka and the tango-hug and the
pigeop’s-wing and the double-shuffle and the
olfl George Henry down at the University
Hall. Same night the Croquet set had a
jig. Both shows suffered in the attendance
from rain spasms.
“The Country Girl,” played by the Bris-
bane _ Amateur Operatic Society, has been
running at His Majesty’s all the week, and
has drawn a crowded house at each per-
formance. Lottie Richter Davis and Teenie
Annand Jordan played the two chief parts
(Marjorie and the Princess), and the sing-
ing of both was a joy and a delight. Tall,
slender Lottie Richter faced the work as to
the manner born, and Mrs. Jordan, who
vyas skilfully made up, would have won the
first prize or senior trophy at any “Oriental”
ball. George Webster was the hit of the
show as Barry, and Miss Eunice Drury, as
Sophie, kept the comedy part from sagging
in the middle.
Herbert Walton, of the Green Dandies,
who has played Barry over 200 times,
coached Mr. Webster for the part. As a
mark of appreciation, Mr. Walton was in-
duced to play the role himself on Friday
night.
One of the morning papers this week
voiced the wail of a female who had been
stabbed in the nose —accidentally—by some
unsisterly stranger whose hatpins w T ere of
the stiletto description. The party with
the punctured nose snorted that she “thought
there was a by-law” on the subject. So
there is. Also there are 10 Hebrew by-
laws in the Book of Exodus. What hap-
pens as a consequence?
The Ambulance Sports were to have hap-
pened to-day, but the angel in charge
of the waterworks Up Above (I suppose
he would be called the Archturncock) had
all the taps running. So the sports were
washed off the .slate for the time being.
Firmin McKinnon, a popular Queensland
journalist, who has been editing the Dar-
ling Downs Gazette, is coming back to
Brisbane at the end of. the month, to join
the Courier.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Ilangan” 1 : Brisbane is spending another
£lO,OOO on wharf extension, while a fleet of
tugs is employed to drag big ships off the
mudbanks of the river. Meanwhile, the splen-
did port of Gladstone has been so wholly sup-
pressed by the big city interests that it takes
a half-holiday because of its wild surprise
when a 200-ton vessel calls in to buy a pint
of beer.
Gov. Macgregor alleged, at
Brisbane St. George’s banquet, that Britain
had lent the Dominions and foreign countries
£3,500,000,000 and that the annual income
of the nation was £2.400,000,000. In the
same issue of the Courier that reported this
utterance it was specially mentioned that a
contribution of two shillings had been re-
ceived towards supplying the poor of London
with meat.
Plain Tales from the Isles.
Dear Bulletin, —The old trouble between
Capital and Democracy is proceeding on
somewhat new lines in P-apua. Capitalism
has got one foot planted in the country, and
is preparing to put down the other; but the
foot is booted, so the simple people of the
Territory cannot see that the extrem-
ity inside is cloven. Capitalism wants
imported Asiatic labor. It wants 30,000
or 40,000 or 80,000 Chinks and Japs;
it wants them muck cheap; it wants
to settle them for a term of years,
or for life, in a territory that is just
about an afternoon’s run fiorn the northern,
tip of Australia. This isn’t White Australia,
policy. Capitalism professes to think the
yellow races can never injure us ; anyhow, it
doesn’t care if they do so long as it. procures;
dividends quick, even if they ni‘fe blood-
stained and dirty. And it has g-t "an Heai
that it can get them a little quieurr, by im-
porting the Chink nr.d Lite Jap. As a matter-
of fact, it can t. Chink and the rest cost;
more than the humble Papuan, both in wages-
and recruiting; also, labor of the local kind I
has never been more plentiful than at pre-
sent, and none of the plantations are going;
short, or are likely to. But Capital, pulling;
strings from Melbourne and Sydney, thinks;
that if it could manage to get those crowds ;
of Chows, etc., away from their native count-
try into the wilds of Papua und.r.its sole
control, it could in one way or another-
mostly in another—contrive things so i
as to get its labor for pretty well.
nothing. However it; has learned cunning:
by the leek it was • made to chew over
the Kanaka question. If doesn’t -label itself
Capitalism, just now. In Papua it is Demo-
cracy-Simple Manhood ; Electoral Repre- -
sentation (in a population of a scant one thou-
sand) ; the Sovereign People asking„for their.
Rights. Give it its rights, and it will be
happy. All it wants is a majority in the
Papuan Legislative Council so that it can-
pass resolutions in favor of Chinese or Jap>
labor, till it wears down tbe resistance of
the Commonwealth Government. When the
Australian Government took over Papua a.
law keeping out coolie labor was one of its-
iirst Acts. In those days the labor employ-
ers of the Territory were mostly miners, and
the Papuan miner is nearly always a good
Australian, who doesn’t want the northern
coolies, even if he thought they were cheaper
than the Papuan. But when the day of the
planter came things' were changed. The
planter. is no Democrat' (his- shareholders
would like to catch him at it)A He is to a
man in favor of anything cheap and Asiatic
and dangerous and un-Australian, provided
it is cheap. That the Asiatic is not cheap,
m Papua, is a thing he won’t believe. He
wants to try, anyhow. This is one of the
things he won’t say right out in meeting,,
since most of the white population is opposed;
to coolie labor —but nevertheless, in private,-,
his night and morning prayer is : “O Lord, 1 ,
send me the Chink and the Jap.” This is;
the real milk of the Papuan cocoanut, where.'
elective representation is concerned. It is;
also the “innards” of the persistent system;
of attack upon the Governor made by-
Papua’s only newspaper, and of the supply
of lies about Papuan affairs generally that
trickles through to the Australian press.
Governor Murray is a White Australian and
a Democrat. If the ends of this particular
capitalistic combine are to be attained, he
and his Administration will be plain Mud.
The strange part of the whole show is that
the Papuan Times should have succeeded, so
far, in disguising itself as a Democratic
paper, and the pity of it is that so many
real Democrats should have been confidence-
tricked into, supporting a movement that is
a mere capitalistic dodge. Governor Mur-
ray, by the way, is not the only New Guinea
ruler who is having a trying time. A trav-
eller from Rabaul the other day, between
two friendly beers, told me that Governor
Haul is finding thorns in the stuffing of
his mattress. German New Guinea is more
or less “agin” Governor Haul, and for an
odd reason-—he is friendly to the yellow
people. They are not employed as laborers
in German New Guinea (they know better
there), but they are allowed in, and may
stay long, enough to do some carpentering
and building and boat-repairing and things
of that sort. They are not allowed to take
up a square inch of the Fatherland’s pre-
cious Territory, and they are kindly but
firmly escorted to a homeward steamer after
a year or two ; nevertheless, young Germany
does not like them, and especially does not
like the glad hand that Governor Haul is
said to have extended to them. The result
is that Governor Haul, still in the early
forties, in good health and apparently as
able a Governor as most colonies are blessed
with, is going home soon, and probably won’t
come back any more. It is a queer specta-
cle, the two New Guineas, Australian and'
German, each barking at its Governor, one-
because the said Governor won’t let in the:
yellow man, and the other because he will:.
Apparently, if the countries could only ex-
change their Governors, everyone would be-
satisfied all round.—Tours, etc., Hard Case..

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
A’o notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name end address must appear on MSS. Ao
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MSS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
A Howe: The man who wrote these items
was a war correspondent in a scrap where every man
took his life in his hand, and the prisoner had a good ,
chance of being boiled alive. So perhaps he isn’t
the effeminate person you imagine. Were you ever
boiled alive?. .. .Blue Pencil: He was once a postal
official in Sydney, and afterwards did some press-
work in Australia. He isn’t in “Who’s Who” (1914),
so we can’t give you his present address, but no
doubt you can find it at some of the daily paper
offices.... G.: The tin-scratcher and the footless mad-
man are picturesque, but too horrible. .. .Fritz: For
pity’s sake, drop sending us accurately-typed items
that you have hewn from London and Yankee papers,
and paraphrased for our benefit. They fill up the
w.p.b., and make the labors of the charlady unneces-
sarily heavy. . . .S.A.T.: It was resourceful, but'not
worth print where space is so scarce that life is one
long howl or snarl at the man who makes up the
paper. Incidentally, The Bulletin is glad you are
a “bullock bloke.” One of its kindly spots has
“bullock bloke” branded in the middle. . . .Bard of
the Glen: It’s wonderful what you can’t do when
you don’t try. Incidentally, it may be that you
couldn’t do it even if you did try....C.N. (Mun-
gana) : Tarquah G. M. Co. via Seccondee, Gold Coast,
Africa, via Great Britain. .. .Pill: What George
Reid doubtless meant was that “we shouldn’t dig up
black hatreds at a moment when the bosh is grow-
ing green over them.” “Moss” was a misprint. . . .
Kao: We have no information about the balance-
sheet of that Brisbane paper, so it is no use asking
questions at present. Applications for a copy of
the document seem futile. In fact, one might
imagine the concern was a hidden crime, or a dead
body, or a Venetian Council of Ten, or a Free-
masons’ lodge, so far as publicity goes. Possibly
there is a murdered Chinaman on the premises, and
concealment is essential. . . . Mulgaite: It depends
on the nationality of the ship. All persons born
on British vessels, for example, are registered in a
parish in the East End of London. .. .W.F.M.: Give
over. You lack the writer’s gift. . . . J.P.S.: Win-
deyer heard the news of Dean’s guilt at Adelaide.
He wept tears of joy all over a leg of mutton which
he was eating at the time; finished the entire
joint, barring the bone and a fragment of gristle;
and had a fit on the floor. This is the story of
a horrified waiter who was employed at the hotel
where the dreadful spectacle is supposed to have
been staged. It is possible that the waiter lied.
The story of another waiter is that Windeyer swal-
lowed the mutton, as above-mentioned, and had
two fits. Who shall decide where waiters disagree?
All that one can say, with absolute certitude, is
that Windeyer was glad to learn about Dean’s
guilt, and had an unhealthy appetite. ... S.O.:

nearly 2600ft. above sea level. That is. why the
winter snow hits it in the waistbelt. Jericho, only
half a day’s drive away, has no snow, but then

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)

useable.
It is impressively interesting to hear that
the wife of the forthcoming G.-G., being the
daughter of a marquis, will be known to
Australia ;as Lady Helen, or Lady Helen
Mo-Ferg., not Lady Ferguson. Her hus-
band is a mere knight. Militant re-
presentatives of the downtrodden sex will
' please note that the laws of social “rank”
were made for women. The girl who is born
with a title to her name can “rise” to a
higher title by marriage, but cannot descend
to a lower one, like the Miss Montmorency
who becomes a Mrs. Mudsre. Also the titled
widow who marries again can please herself
as to whether she will call herself by her
second husband’s name or remain faithful in
title to the dear departed. Finally, it seems
necessary to remind suffragettes that, whilst
a younger son of an earl is born only an
“honorable,” his twin sister becomes a Lady
on the spot.

Always Hot!
You need not suffer
like this. Kriischen
Salts provide the remedy
by the simplest of all
means —by putting you
in first class condition.
The human body is so
constituted that it can
endure all the changes
of temperature ever ex-
perienced in any part of
the Dominion without
distress —if it is healthy.
The man who is con-
tinually getting too hot
is not healthy and should
at once set about mak-
ing himself so.
A very common mis-
take is to suppose that
only the violently ath-
letic people who take
constant hard exercise
can enjoy the blessing
of good health.
Muscular exercise is
only one way —and not
always the best —of
getting health.
Every man and
woman can get fit and
keep fit by gently and
systematically cleansing
the system while at the
same time bracing and
stimulating it to proper
normal activity.
For this purpose take
half a teaspoonful of
Kriischen Salts in a
tumbler of hot water
every morning when
you rise.
Not a Fizzy Drink.
Kriischen Salts are not
gaseous or effervescent, so
that you may take them with-
out the least fear of flatulence.
Many people experience dis-
comfort after drinking any
aerated mixture. Everyone can
take Kriischen Salts without
any unpleasant sensation what-
ever.
Kruschen Salts dispel all
traces of uric acid from the
system and bring a strong
sense of vitality and well-being.
Slackness and depression dis-
appear and in their stead come
vigour and cheerfulness, fresh-
ness and energy, and the keen
delight of perfect mental and
bodily health.
Kriischen Salts.
Kriischen Salts are a sure
but gentle aperient. By re-
moving the uric acid from the
system they speedily and per-
manently drive away Gout,
Rheumatism, Lumbago, Liver
and Kidney troubles, Eczema
and Indigestion.
Kriischen Salts are not
laxative nor weakening but act
as a splendid tonic.
The body frequently fails to
supply itself with the necessary
quantities of Sodium, Mag-
nesium and Potassium Salts
which.it should take from the
food it receives. Kriischen
Salts remedy all such defects
and help to build up sound,
healthy tissue.

REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS
I OP
The National Bank of Australasia Limited.
« ——
The Balance-Sheet of the Bank as on March 31 last, and the Profit and Loss Account for the Half-
year which then terminated, are now submitted to the Shareholders.
The net profit, after deduction of all expenses, including £7126 18s. 6d. for Note, Income and
Land Taxes,
Amounted to ..
Add balance brought forward from previous half-year ..
£94,866 1
16.053 13
Which
Leaving available..
it is proposed to appropriate as follows:
Transfer to Reserve Fund Account (making it £500,000)
Reduction of Bank Premises
Grant to Officers’ Provident Fund
Dividend at the rate of 7 per cent, per annum on Preference Strafes
Dividend at the rate of 7 per cent, per annum on Ordinary Shares
Carry forward
£110,909 15 2
During the Half-year a new Branch Office was opened at the corner of Collins and William Streets,
Melbourne (Western Branchl : and Branches at King William Street South, Adelaide; and William Street,
Perth ; and the Branches at Lismore, in Victoria; and York, in Western Australia, were closed.
Agencies were established at Lorquon, in Victoria ; Berri, Melton, Stockwell, Mile End and Nailsworth,
in South Australia; and at Manjimup, Ongerup, Watheroo and Bofgart, in Western Australia.
The Ordinary General Annual Meeting will be held at the Board Room, ajt the Bank’s Head Office,
Collins Street, Melbourne, on Wednesday, May 6, at noon, when the Shareholders will be called upon to
elect two Directors in lieu of John Grice and Edward Trenchard, EsquipJs, who retire by rotation, in
accordance with the Articles of Association, and are eligible for re-election. There are no other candidates.
The Shareholders will also be called upon to elect two Auditors for the ensuing year.
By order of the Board Of Directors,
E. H. WREFORD, Chief Manager.
The National Bank of Australasia. Limited.
Head Office, Melbourne, April 23, 1914.

The Inky Way.
“G.D.M.”: One of the U.S. State Courts —that
of Ohio —laid it down last month that the secretary
of the Columbus (Ohio) Council had no right to
exclude the public from the latter’s meetings. The
principle put forward by the Court was that news
of a public nature belongs, of right, to the public,
or, in other words, to the newspapers. It would be
interesting to get a local judgment on the matter.
Take, for instance, that typical Star Chamber, the
Melbourne City Council, which transacts so much of
its business by means of committees, some of which
have not met for nine months. Reporters are not
allowed to attend any of them, and the ratepayers
have never in 60 years so much as suspicioned what
goes on during their deliberations. What a gracious
reform it would be if the Melbourne councillors were
forced to at all times admit the press to their dis-
cussions. And the Melbourne Council typifies many
smaller concerns all over the Commonwealth.
* * * *
A recent death in London journalistic circles that
attracts attention here was that of “Captain Coe,” of
the London Star. The Star was started (no pun) in
1887, when Passmore Edwards of the Evening News
took his popular ha’penny evening paper over from
Gladstonian principles to Unionism, thus leaving the
Liberals of the metropolis without a single evening
paper to barrack for them. The Star was run by a
well-known Radical professor named Stuart, who sat
for Hoxton (Horful ’Oxton), and the money
that was put into the new venture came from
the wealthy, wowsery, Quakery family of the Cad-
burys, of cocoa fame. The new paper was vimful and
loaded with fight to the muzzle, but it didn’t really
take with the ’orny-’anded, in whose interests it had
been started, until “Captain Coe,” by a series of
phenomenally successful racing tips, gave it a vogue
among all classes. It was a common thing to see
people who were anything but Radical in politics buy
the Star, assimilate “Captain Coe’s” tips, and—
fling the rag away ! Professor Stuart was very indig-
nant to think that his slashing leaders and thunderous
declamation, and really excellent “Personal” column
—one of the first and best things of its sort London
journalism ever saw—were over-shadowed by the
sporting column of “Captain Coe.” But so it was.
The “Captain,” whose real name was Edgar Card
Mitchell (and he was a card, too) was more thought
of by the class that really read the Star than many
politicians. He left the staggering sum of £6569
earned on the Inky Way, and his one son, who is a
clerk on the Stock Exchange, gets the lot. But
the old man, who was a wag in private life, has also
left, with his last will and testament, a final joke for
the Londoners to laugh over. He bequeathed to his
son “the sole right to use the name, ‘Captain Coe,’
on his paying to my trustees the sum of £lO, and
entering into a covenant with them that he will
NEVER GAMBLE, nor give up his regular STOCK
EXCHANGE WORK”!
“M.”: Everybody who went through the mill on
the Melbourne press knew and liked Tom Monaghan
(of the Herald), now dead at the age of 75. We
would have believed anything about Tom's age, for
he seemed to have been in the game from the begin-
ning. Among many titles to fame, Tom could boast
of being a J.P., and he knew more about the work
of Petty Sessions than many of Melbourne’s weird
honorary magistrates. He had no reverence for his
brother justices. I have seen Tom rise up from
the press bench in Melbourne City Court, when P.M.
Panton wasn’t there, to say indignantly to the old
fossils up above, “’Ere, ’ere, you can’t do that!”
It was when they tried to hang a man for loitering,
or something of the sort. When Tom stood up to
direct the law in the way it should go he was
generally right.
Another cry from Macedonia disguised as Wood-
ville, Maoriland. The local Examiner lets it go.
“Will our readers and advertisers please excuse us
if we take advantage of the New Year season to re-
mind them that the printer cannot live upon air. .. .
We have on our books the names of subscribers who
have never paid a subscription for the past five or
six years. Some of these subscribers are people who
could well afford to buy the whole Argus plant half
a dozen times over, and never miss the outlay....
We would ask those who are interested in the mat-
ter to show a little consideration for the printer,
and surprise and delight him by ‘squaring the yards’
early this year....We would respectfully ask these
people to give us a ‘square deal’ in this matter,
recognise their legitimate responsibilities and pay
up. We don’t see how anyone can hope to get to
Heaven if he leaves his newspaper bill unpaid—
it must be an impossibility.”
• * * *
“H. 0.”: The other day I picked up “The Four
Faces,” the latest potboiler by William Le Quex, a
man who writes about how countesses steal despatches
from amorous diplomatists, and invents mysteries
which baffle their author absolutely and keep him
wondering about what is going to happen in the next
chapter. The central character of “The Four Faces”
was born in Melbourne. As Le Quex puts it, Huges-
son Gastrell is “a gentleman, although an Aus-
tralian.” Just when we thought we were getting rid
of our English birth-stains, and had commenced to
hope that all our relations in Whitechapel were dead!
THE OUTSIDERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They don’t even take any notice of England.
— Melba, on the people of the United States.
Good Ged!
And can the blighters live? Dammit! Eh, what!
D’ye mean to say they’ve never heard
Of Asquith, don't you know, and Churchill, don’t you
know ?
Well now, upon my word!
Oh, come, come, I say, surely not!
It can’t be done, you know ; it just can’t be done;
No fellow does it, you know ; no fellow ;
It’s a beastly country, reahlly, don’t you know; it’s
run
By a rotten lot of bounders ; yellow newspapers, you
know ; yellow.
Not notice England ? Oh, I say!
Bosh, old man ; bosh ;
Tosh, old chap ; tosh !
England’s all right! What? What? What?
These outsiders! Oh, dash it all, what rot!
Hang it, old boy.
Think just a bit. Think !
Haven’t we got a navy, don’t you know?
Boast beef, don’t you know? British gravy, don’t
you know ?
Speak through their noses, don’t they?
Trusts, and Presidents, and long speeches, ain’t they?
Not notice England—Oh, come off, old chap !
Have they ever been to Henley, the bounders?
Don’t play county cricket, do they? Some kind of
rounders.
Fond of divorce courts, old chap;
Rotten outsiders in sports, old chap ;
Lot of bally drummers ;
Beastly cads and newcomers ;
Dammit, how many of the blighters have got grand-
fathers ?
Oh, go steady, old fellow!
Did they ever taste English mutton?
Or good old British beef ?
Wouldn’t know it if they saw it, that’s my belief.
And you tell me they don’t care a button !
British beef—you say they supply it?
Well, I deny it.
I say, old cocky, I say !
You needn’t tell me I’ve got to thank
Any demined advertising Yank.
Half-convicts, they are perhaps,
Like—i forget their name —those other chaps.
Never notice England ? Tommyrot!
Then how can the beggars stay on earth?
NJ6.W.
PERCY DE FITZPARAGUS.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SHE bathes in brazen glory
Beneath the brazen sun,
Where Strength and Mirth have
greatest worth
Of all the goodly things of earth,
And Heaven’s gifts have none:
From dreams and their disquiets
From fear and faith set free,
She fights and laughs and riots
Beside the roaring sea.
She knows no mystic torment
Of anxious questioning,
When broods the heart on things apart,
And out beyond all mortal art
The'trembling soul takes wing.
But as the fauns ran shrieking
When squealed the flutes of Pan,
Her own mad revel seeking
She ends as she began.
She is no mistress tender
To stir with questing sighs;
Nor will she yield to Him whose shield
Holds a pale Cross upon its field
Before her burning eyes :
She knows no other master,
And hears no other lord
But him who comes the faster
To take her with the sword.
Go through her clanging traffic
That swings and sweeps and sways.
Where elves and sprites of modern nights
Dismiss with new and strange delights
The gods of earlier days :
No Oread shyly peering,
No Virgin lily-fair,
But wilder nymphs careering
With furious hymnal there.
When strides her steel-shod hero
Across her martial hour.
The market-place receives his face,
His battle is a golden race
To coin and fame and power:
No mercy comes to soften
The ruthless fighting host;
He gains who crushes often.
He wins who crushes most.
There no man shrinks from ruin,
And no man spares to smite;
Hut friend and foe hail every blow
That sends a smitten head below
With fulminant delight:
lie may not sue for favor
Who fails with broken breath:
’Twas his to know the savor
Of victory or of death.
No peace of a Redeemer
Descends, a gentle dove,
In that fierce air which everywhere
Reigns pitiless above despair—-
And no forgiving love :
But all things meek and lowly
The steel-shod foot will spurn ;
Nor love nor hate is holy,
And both are quick to burn.
But when her lusty spearmen
Have flung their spears aside,
Her veil is shed from feet to head,
And she is flamed in white and red
Of naked, shameless pride:
A harlot unexampled,
Her glory is in this —
Who worst was torn and trampled
May have her face to kiss.
All damned and God-forgotten
They lurk in her Domain ;
All night they meet, the broke, the beat,
G ith faces marred by cruel feet,
Yet are they in her train ;
Though misery enslaves them.
And drowns them in the dirt.
This royal city saves them
While they may touch her skirt.
No man so wan and wounded,
So stunned by battle-noise,
But her hot face will give him grace
Once more to join the reckless race
And taste its fiery joys :
Anew to make alliance
With hope that flared before,
And step with bold defiance
Into the lists once more.
Around her dazzling beaches
Her children ripen all;
And some will wail and some will fail
And some will swing a cruel flail
To watch the weaker fall:
But whether Fate discovers
The trampler or the trod,
They all shall be her lovers,
And none will dream of God.
In Sydney, Mirth and Slaughter
Go marching cheek-by-cheek,
And no man cares how each man dares
To grasp and capture unawares
The prizes all men seek ;
The gift is theirs who win it,
To reive and bear away,
For few will care a minute.
And none will weep a day.
Upon her hills in riot
Roars Life, its whole life long;
She does not stoop to sift and scoop.
Nor cares she what may surge or droop,
T So it be glad and strong:
Unvexed, unnitying, tearless
Swift to exalt or strike.
She lies in splendor, fearless
Of God and man alike.
N.s.w.
ADAM McCAY.
The Thrum Lord.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE educated man of Bay Mansions had
wandered into the question of values,
and it somehow came up that the
cheapest thing on earth is life itself. “The
value of life is mostly that which you esti-
mate individually, or personally,” 'he said.
“You must not be led away by insurance."
That would go for nothing by way of argu-
ment. A man insures for what he An
afford. The real value of life lies in the
usefulness of a man to his fellow-men and
it naturally follows that the hiedier a man’s
station, and the more powerful 'his influence
over other men, the more valuable lie is to
the world, or the section of the world where
his influence lies.”
“What price an English lord, then?” asked
Stinker Williams.
"You can’t put a value on a lord because
he is a lord,” said the man of knowledge.
"His value might not be much greater than
yours or mine, which ain’t worth tuppence,
and, on the other hand, his life might be
beyond estimating. It depends on what sort
of a lord he is. A lord might be a Prime
Minister.”
“Well, the bloke I means is a bloke wot
goes _ about layin’ foundation-stones, en
openin’ of bazaars en exhibitions, en dresses
in a grey suit, en wears a grey top ’at. He
ain’t exactly a lord, either ; ’e’s an ’lghness.”
“A what?”
“An ’lghness. That’s wot yer gotter call
’im when yer has ter speak to him.”
“Do yer mean a prince?”
“All I know about it is that the other
blokes useter call ’im an ’lghness when
ennybody else wuz about, but when they wuz
alone together they useter call ’im Jim! But
wot I wants to know is ’ow much wuz ’is
life worth?”
“How do I know? Maybe half a million.”
“It wuz worth a frum.”
“What rot!”
“A frum —not a penny more. 1 knew
becos he told me so ’imself, or as good as.”
“Did you know him?”
Stinker nodded his head, and after letting
his importance in being the friend of an
’lghness sink in, he said:—
“I got three vallies from that bloke. I
got the vall.y of his life, the vally of bein’
honest, en the vally of bein’ his friend.”
“ ’Ow did yer get to know him, Stinker?”
asked one of the others.
“Yer knows ’ow fond I am of scenery.
Whenever I sees good scenery I gets sleepy,
en I’ll lie for hours among it. It wuz when
the lord I speak of wuz doin’ a trip frough
Australia, en ’avin’ a good time layin’
stones en lings like that, I wus up on the
cliffs at South ’Ead, more asleep than awake,
when I ’ears voices en I notices two blokes
m grey suits of clobber en grey top-hats,
standin’ on the edge of the cliff, a-lookin’
at the water beneath, en laughin’ at
the mayor en mayoress of some town
they’d bin to. En I noticed the
ground beneath the lord bloke wuz
breakin’ away under ’is weight, en I rushes
up en grabs ’im be the coat-tails en hauls
im back, en soon after the rock under ’im
went down flop on to (he rocks beneaf.
“ ’P wuz a little bloke, not much ter look
at, but ’e could be mad. my oaf. When ’e
got up ’e says: ‘Damned impertinence,
what!’ Yer see, I’d pulled him so ’ard he’d
sat down. I thought he would ’ave struck
me wif ’is stick, on’y the secrety interfered,
en when ’e saw the rock had gone, ’e’ said
puffin’, an’ just walked to the carriage. Then
’e ’ad a bit of guiver wif the secrety, en ’e
beckoned me up. I could see ’e wuz goin’ to
give me somefing, so I came up wif me two
palms turned into a basin.”
My man,’ he says, ‘ ’ave a beeah, what.’
En ’e told the coachman to drive off, en I
found ’e’d guv me a frum. When I knowed
’im better I knowed ’e’d ’ave guv me ’alf a
crown, _ on’y the secrety told ’im I’d only
spend it in drink en make a beast of meself.
I ust fing I fought of when ’e’d gone wuz
1° run m after ’ iin en frow the frum in his
face, lhen I fought that a beer wouldn't
be a bad idea .instead. En besides that, T
a • ~ e liPle bloke. I know some of yous
Socialist blokes finks lords en dukes are a
bad lot, en yous might be right, but this lord
tepp m e fancy. ’E ’ad a habit of sayin’
What, en everyfing was damned, en ’e ’ad
a bone, in the bridge of ’is nose that looked
as if it would break the skin - frough, en
little round eyes you couldn’t tell what ’e
was finking about, en I don’t fink he useter
fink. much enny way. Ee ’ad two ways of
talking’, one when ’e wuz wif his friends, en
one when ’e laid stones truly. When ’e laid
stones you’d fink ’e wuz full of wind, on’y ’e
knowed ’ow to let it come out if ’e took care
en if he didn’t take care it would burst out
like a bladder wot boys ’ave made into a
rootball. \v hen I useter go en see ’im lay
stones I useter wish ’e’d forget ’ow to talk
so as the wind would come out suddent. My
oaf it wuz funny to listen to ’im, but no-
body useter laugh becos it wuz on’y his
friends who knew ’e could talk like you en
me. The public in general fought it wuz
the proper way for a lord ter talk. Of
course, he usen’t to say ‘What,’ en damn
everyfing then.
“When I got .back to where the rock ’ad
dropped I found a pocket-book Ivin’ there,
en when I opened it I found it wuz full of
bank papers en. notes. It must ’ave dropped
out of the little bloke’s pocket when I pulled
un back. Well, I sat down en wondered
wot I'd do wif it. en I fought I’ll guv it
back to ’im. I'll be ’onest. Ennyway, it
would 'ave been a fool’s game stickin’ to it.
It wuz too big for me.
“When I got inter town I came upon a
lot of people waiting on both sides of the
road, en some slops on ’orses makin’ the
’orses back the people wif their rumps, en
suddenly I saw the little bloke come down
the street in a carriage, en ’ere wuz me
after ’im for all I wuz worf. The slops
on the ’orses left off jambin’ the people back
ter give chase ter me. They must ’ave
fought I ’ad a bomb for 'is ’lghness- But I
dodged ’em like greased lightnin’. en when
the carriage came to where they’d put some
carpet down for the little bloke to walk on.
I rushes up eu says -
‘“Did you drop this, guv’nor?’
“ ‘W’v. if it ain’t my damned preserver,
what!’ he savs to the secrety. En then
when ’e’d taken the pocket-book, ’e said:
'Do you know WO f wuz in the book?’ he
says. En I says. ‘Yes, mate.’ En ’e says,
‘Wlio’d ’ave thought it, what!’ ”
“What did he give you, Stinker?”
“Five sovrings,” said Stinker. “En ’e
told me to go an’ get blind wif it. which
showed ’e wasn’t such a bad little bloke if
’e wuz on’y let. But the sovrings didn’t do
me much good, becos one of the slops who
seed me rushing down after the little bloke
reckernised me en arrested me es a vagrant,
en for creatin’ a disturbance, en ’e whacked
the five sovrings wif another slop who ’elped
search me at the police station. I got a
monf.”
“But I thought you said you became
friends with the aristocrat,” said the man
who had been educated.
“That wuz after I came out. T wuz pass-
in’ one of the big buildings whim a bloke in
a room frew out the end of a cigar, en just
when I wuz stoopin’ to pick it up, ’e said,
‘Damn it_all, if it isn’t the honest tramp

Do YOU get Pains in the Loins and Back?
Gravel? Dropsical Swellings? Rheumatism?
Kidney and Bladder Trouble is Serious!
Backache is generally misunderstood. Many people
neglect it because they believe it to be muscular weakness
due to overwork. So they wait for it to pass off.
But when backache does not pass off we know that the
pain comes not from the back, but from the kidneys, which lie
beneath the back.
It is a warning, and it is a serious warning, for when the
kidneys are weak the uric-acid poison they leave in the blood
begins to spread all over the system —-
Makes your back ache, your limbs ache —settles into
urinary sediment or gravel, which irritates the bladder and
keeps it in a constant state of discomfort —clogs the urinary
channels and stops the free passage of water, causing the
swellings of dropsy —forms into sharp, jagged crystals in the
muscles, joints and nerves, as in rheumatism and sciatica.
If you have any of these symptoms, begin with Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills without delay, for the longer kidney
trouble is neglected the harder it is to cure. Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills gently lead the kidneys back to health and
activity. They relieve the urinary system like laxative
medicines relieve the bowels. They prevent waste water from
staying too long in the body, and they drive out the uric acid
poison which causes the above symptoms and many more.

the little bloke.
“That’s ’ow I knows the vally of a lord’s
life, the vally of bein’ ’onest to ’im. en the
vally of his friendship,” concluded Stinker.
ROBERT BROTHERS.
A Wet Day at Invercargill.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE got here in two jumps from
Dunedin, the solidest looking, and
probably the most solid city in
Maoriland. We had dribbled down the map
from its sunny head at Auckland to the old
Presbyterian settlement of red roofs and
brass doorsteps, which brews the best beer
in the Dominion, and is intimate with
whisky. Another step south and we wei-e
in the “dry” district. At Gore you may-
change your mind and go north again to the
majestic snow-clad mountains of the Maori
Westland. The choice of first stop lies be-
tween Lumsden, where liquor is naked and
unashamed, and Invercargill where it is
hidden in a keg and consumed secretly.
Gore itself is “dry” and for its sins sus-
tained lately an inexplicable flood which put
six feet of water in its best parlor and
washed the family cow out of the paddock
behind the house. As to that flood, they tell
a characteristic story. In a Prohibition area
everything is supposed to go right. Where
there is no whisky there can be no want; so
when Gore had cleaned out the wreck of the
antimacassar from the parlor sofa, and
rinsed out two inches of slime from the fry-
ing pan, and burned the late lamented cow,
and when the farmers had reported that their
stock of cocksfoot and clover seed had been
washed 40 miles down the river, Maoriland
was sufficiently sympathetic to rain down
offers of assistance. But Gore remembered
its “no whisky, no want” theories and re-
plied that it wasn’t in need of anything—re-
plied in haste and repented at leisure. It
recalled that there are no circumstances in
which the State should not be milked.
Wherefore at latest Gore was asking for a
grant from the Government to put its sodden
affairs once more in order.
From Gore if you jump south you clasp
the cold feet of Maoriland at Invercargill.
Six miles out you pass the last hotel. Be-
tween that shrine of sin and Dunedin lies the
longest stretch of “dry” country in the
Dominion. After a long course of Canon
Boyce the Australian is liable to have the
idea that Maoriland is mostly “dry.” It is
quite a mistake. Sticking to the ordinary
tourist routes, which presumably take in the
parts best worth seeing, you can travel from
the sunnv head to, at any rate, the knee of
the country and find hotels wherever you
would expect to find them in a land that had
never heard of Prohibition. The fact that
there are “dry” spots here and there seems,
however, to have this effect: local travellers
who elsewhere would have no flask in then-
baggage, carry one with them. Hence a
marked smell of whisky at the dining table
of unlicensed houses even in “wet” districts.
It may be coincidence or it may be cause
and effect; certainly it is a fact, that almost
without exception the No-Liquor districts
are flat and drear of outlook. You push our
of the hill country that comes down to the
sea at Dunedin, and you are in the plains of
Southland —fine grazing and agricultural
territory most of it, dotted with fat sheep
and freezing works, where an oat crop of less
than 60 bushels makes the farmer have
lanH g h! S of bankruptcy. The best of the
a£|c ' reached fabulous prices. You hear
of farms sold at £6O an acre. But they are
paid tor almost always with lOU’s. The
wISxfSS Plan worked for all it is worth.
vitn xiuu or so in your pocket you can buy
a sheep farm and the stock thrown at
jou. We could not work it that way in Aus-
tralia ; but here in Southland droughts are
unknown and the creditor is sure of a clip of
wool and the increase of lambs. So the
puce climbs higher and higher, not regulated
by what a man has and is willing to give,
but only by what he has vague hopes of
being able to save. A hundred or two is
neither here nor there to the man who has
nothing, lie cannot lose anything anyway.
fcomehow 1 had a vague idea that Inver-
cargill hung on a few rocky crags that stood
up against the furious blasts of the Antarctic.
JNo idea could be more wrong. There is not
a rocky crag on the horizon. The South-
land plains just die of exposure and drop
into the sea. Invercargill itself is partly
dry land, and partly tussock swamp, and
part y something intermediate. The early
inhabitants were evidently uncertain whether
to have streets or canals, so to be on the safe
aide they called them all after the rivers ot
Scotland—the Dee and the Tay, the Clyde
and the Spey, and so on through the chapter.
A stream of mud and water flows through
ttm town. Its sides have to be propped up
Jest someday they should fall in and engulf
the surroundings. The streets are wide and
treeless and dreary. Rain falls most of the
trme and the wind blows incessantly. If
there were anv place high enough you could
look out over the saddest town in Australasia.
V\ e had asked in advance from the persist-
ent traveller for the address of the most com-
tortabie hotel accommodation. When we got
it it fell like a winter funeral. The house
had once owned the warming glory of a
license, but that was gone. Passing in out
ot the dismal street you reach a “temperance
bar thivuah a narrow freezing chamber in
which four pot plants shivered inside the
rici.yecl door mat. There is no life outside
the pot plants and very little in them. You
jangle a bell and are led through other
freezing chambers to your bedroom. Every-
where there are signs of comforts that have
been struck by an iceberg and frozen to death.
Only in the dining hall is anything of the
old order maintained. You have your food
and wonder how it could be so good in such
circumstances.
There is apparently no reason why the un-
licensed house should not be as good as the
licensed house, but the general testimony of
travellers is that what we met at Invercar-
gill they meet in almost every Prohibition
town, if not in every one. The truth seems
to be that the man who knows best
how to run an hotel takes care that
the hotel he does run gives him the
extra profits of a bar trade. The others
who drift into the management of unlicensed
houses are full of good intentions, probably.
But the hell of which good intentions are the
paving stones is-not always full of fire £nd
brimstone. Sometimes it is occupied by de-
jected furniture, cold draughts and travellers
who are mostly looking to see when the next
train leaves.
When Invercargill begins to suffer from
what are known as the dry horrors it takes,
either tram or motor, or slouches along per
foot to the six-mile-out pub. Sometimes it
goes personally, other times it appoints an
attorney. The duty of the attorney is to
bring back the case or the keg. Keg parties
are commoner in Invercargill than sunstrokes
in Singapore. When the syndicate has
drained the vessel it usually leaves it to the
mercy of the municipal dustman. Almost
every Sunday morning you may find an empty
keg or two on the post-office steps. There
is a proposal to change the name of the town
to Inverkegville. It will probably not be
adopted for some years ; for the present the
folk of the dominant majority take their Pro-
hibition far too seriously.
And yet Invercargill is prosperous. It
lives by frozen meat, talks frozen meat, thinks
frozen meat, and most of the year almost is
frozen meat. To its meats it is adding cheese
—fine cheese and superfine meat as meat
ought to be, which is grazing on turnips to-
day and is frozen mutton in the morning.
Herein Nature has been more bountiful to
Maoriland than to Australia, whose stock
lose their bloom in the long railway racketing
and the often longer wait in the bare pens
which gape into the shambles.
But man does not live by cross-bred alone;
and because Invercargill adds little to its
cross-bred it is cold and uninviting. It has
raised its own standards; its prosperity it
reckons not by its happy days, but by the
pence it adds to its pence, the pounds to its
pounds. Not wholly out of the bigness of
its heart and love for its fellow man has
Invercargill voted itself dry ; many of the bal-
lots have been cast with a view to saving
itself from the awful possibility of some day
having to shout for someone. It might take
a lake of whisky to make Invercargill gay,
and Prohibition is perhaps not altogether re-
sponsible for the chill that strikes to the
visitor’s marrow. Still it might at least be
frank about it, and instead of raising the
standard, “Prohibition Saves the People,”
chalk up what to a large part of Southland
appears to be the greater truth, “Prohibition
Saves the Saxpence.”
S. H. P.
A Change.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Her feet beneath her petticoat
Like little mice peeped in and out”—
The verses used to run;
But nowadays the text is wrong
As given in the old-time song,
Clarinda wears not one.
The petticoat has vanished quite;
The little feet from human sight
Pretend not to be hid ;
What women wear, ’tis not for me
To mention, it would haply be
A bloomer if I did.
Not feet alone, but to the knee
The dainty legs most fair to see
(The makers shape them right)
In quite a bold and shameless wav
For all beholders make display—
A very charming sight!
N.S.W.
